The author of this book, having completed for you, my children, this book, which is a complete part of medical knowledge, and having reached the summit of its clarity and explanation, saw fit to complete it for you with this chapter, which is a part of practical application.
Because practical application, in our country and in our time, is completely lacking, so much so that the study of its knowledge is almost lost and its effects have ceased. But only a few traces remain in the books of the ancients, which have been distorted by hands, affected by error and confusion, until their meanings have become obscure and their benefit distant.
Therefore, I saw fit to revive it and compose this chapter on the path of explanation, clarification, and summary, and to present illustrations of cauterizing irons and other instruments of practice, for it is an enhancement of clarity and a collection of necessities.
The reason there is no skillful craftsman with a skilled hand in our time is that the art of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must first diligently study the anatomy, as described by Galen. So that he may understand the benefits of the organs, their arrangements, temperaments, connections, and separations, and the knowledge of bones, nerves, muscles, their numbers and exits, arteries, veins, both pulsating and stagnant, and the locations of their exits.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in practice, especially in the art of the hand.  We have mentioned a part of this in the introduction of this book.
For whoever is not knowledgeable about the anatomy we have mentioned is prone to making mistakes that kill people, as I have frequently witnessed in those who imagine themselves knowledgeable in this science without knowledge or understanding.
I saw an ignorant physician lance a swine-like tumor on a woman's neck, severing some of the neck's arteries, and the woman bled to death in his hands.
I saw another physician, attempting to remove a stone from the bladder of an elderly man, a large stone. He acted rashly and removed it with a piece of the bladder itself. The man died about three days later.  I was called to remove the stone, and, having observed the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I considered it beyond my abilities.
I saw another physician who practiced medicine for hire among the merchants of our city.
A black boy, with a fracture near his heel and a wound, fell into the hands of this physician.  In his ignorance, the physician quickly bound the fracture tightly against the wound with splints and bandages, leaving no room for the wound to breathe.
Then he indulged his desires, and left him for days, ordering him not to loosen the bandages until his leg and foot swelled, and he was near death.
I was called to him, and I quickly loosened the bandages.  He found relief and his pain lessened, but the corruption had already set in the limb and I could not prevent it.
The corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
I saw another physician treat a cancerous tumor.  After a few days, it ulcerated, and the patient's suffering grew worse.  This is because if the cancer is purely of black humors, it should never be treated with iron.
Unless it is in a limb that can be completely removed.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work is divided into two categories: work that is accompanied by safety, and work that, in most cases, results in harm.
I have warned in every place in this book of work that involves risk and fear, so you should avoid and reject it.
So that the ignorant may not find a way to speak ill of you, be firm and cautious, and be gentle and steadfast in your treatment.
Employ the best path leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult-to-cure diseases.
Free yourselves from that which you fear might introduce doubt into your religion and worldly affairs; it will be more lasting for your honour and will elevate your status in this world and the hereafter.
Galenus said in some of his advice: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you become a bad physician.
This treatise is divided into three chapters.
The first chapter.
In cauterization by fire and cauterization by sharp medicines, arranged from the foot to the head, illustrations of instruments, cauterization tools, and everything necessary for manual work.
And the second chapter.
Concerning incision, lancing, phlebotomy, cupping, surgeries, removal of arrows, and similar procedures, all arranged, and illustrations of instruments.
And the third chapter.
Concerning fractures, dislocations, treatment of sprains, and similar procedures, arranged from the head to the foot, and illustrations of instruments.
The first chapter.
Concerning cauterization.
Before mentioning its use, it is necessary to mention the benefits and harms of cauterization, and in which temperament it is used.
I say that discussing the benefits and harms of cauterization is a lengthy discourse, a precise science, and a hidden secret, and many wise men have spoken about it.
And they differed in their opinions, but I have concisely summarized their few words, fearing to become overly verbose.
I say that cauterization is generally beneficial for all bad temperaments, with or without material substance, excluding two temperaments: the hot temperament without substance, and the dry temperament without substance.
As for the dry temperament with material substance, they differed in opinion. Some said that cauterization is beneficial in it, while others said the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable for diseases resulting from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to be cured of a hot, dry disease with a hot, dry remedy.  The one who says the opposite claims that cauterization with fire may be beneficial for a hot, dry disease that arises in people's bodies. Because when you add the human body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the human body cold.
And I say with his opinion, because experience has shown me this repeatedly; however, one should not assume this matter without extensive training and practice in the art of cauterization. And he should understand the diversity of human temperaments, the nature of diseases themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for other temperaments, there is no fear of them, especially cold, moist diseases. All physicians agree on their benefit from cauterization.
Know, my children, that the secret of beneficial cauterization with fire lies in its superiority over cauterization with burning medicine. This is because fire is a single element whose effect is limited to the organ cauterized and it does not harm another connected organ except to a slight degree.
Cauterization with burning medicine may extend its effect to other organs, and may even cause a difficult-to-treat disease or death in the organ.  Fire, due to its nobility and the excellence of its essence, will not do this unless it is excessively applied.
This has become clear to us through experience, due to the length of our service, attention to the craft, and understanding of the facts.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discourse, though had it not been inappropriate for this book, I would have presented to you a profound secret concerning fire, and how it acts upon the body, and its power to dispel ailments using a philosophical and rigorous argument that might exceed your understanding.
Know, my children, that they have disagreed on the appropriate time for cauterization, and they consider spring to be the best time.
I say that cauterization may be appropriate in any season, because the harm caused by the season is absorbed by the benefit gained through cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for a strong, necessary affliction that cannot tolerate delay, lest it be followed by a greater calamity than the slight harm caused by the season.
Do not imagine, my children, what the common people and ignorant physicians suppose, that the cauterization which cures a disease will never return, and consider it a final remedy.
This is not as you suppose, for cauterization is like a medicine that changes the temperament and dries the moisture which is the cause of the afflictions.
However, cauterization is superior to medication in its swift success, strength of action, and severity of its power.
Yet the disease may return sometime in the future, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength of the disease, its power, and the accumulation of impurities in the body, and his negligence in acquiring proper foods, and similar causes.
Unless the disease for which cauterization is used is a mild one, in a limb with little impurity and moisture. Like cauterizing a tooth to relieve pain, and similar cases, the pain may not return, and this is at least possible.
And as for the common saying that cauterization is the last resort in medicine, it is a correct statement, not in the way they believe, since they think that no treatment can be effective with or without medicine after cauterization, and the matter is otherwise.
The meaning of cauterization being the last resort in medicine is that if we have used various treatments for a disease and the medications have not been effective, and then cauterization is used last and it is effective, then this is why cauterization is called the last resort. Not in the sense understood by the common people and many ignorant physicians.
The ancients stated that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they said this because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence.  They said that the cauterized area does not fester, but this is not universally true.
For I have tested this and found that it only works in some bodies, and not in others.  Cauterization with gold is better and superior to iron, as they said.  However, if you heat a gold cautery in the fire, you will not know when it has heated to the desired degree of gold's redness.
Because cold quickly affects it, and if you increase the heat in the fever, it melts in the fire and is ruined.  The craftsman is thereby engaged in work.  Therefore, cauterization with iron is quicker and closer to the correct procedure for use, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections, organized from head to foot, so that the student may easily find what he needs, God willing.
The first chapter.
Cauterization of the head, a single cauterization.
This cauterization is beneficial for excess moisture and coldness in the brain, which are the cause of headaches, frequent colds from the head to the eye and ear area, excessive sleep, toothache, and sore throat. And in general, for every illness resulting from coldness, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and stupor, and similar illnesses.
The method for this cauterization is that the patient should first be made to vomit with a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, depending on the patient's strength, age, and usual condition.
Then you should command him to shave his head with a razor, then seat him before you in a squatting position with his hands on his chest.
Then place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes.  Wherever your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-oil cautery, which has this form.
If the bone is exposed to the extent of a needle head or a carbuncle seed, remove your hand. Otherwise, reheat the iron itself or another one, if it has cooled. Until you see the bone as I have described. Then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a piece of cotton in it, place it on the area, and leave it for three days.
Then apply a piece of cotton soaked in clarified butter and leave it on until the burning sensation from the fire subsides.  Then treat it with the four-ingredient ointment until it heals, God willing.
They have said that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus it produces, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have suggested cauterizing the skin down to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the bone's thickness burns. Then the burned bone is subsequently scraped away and treated.
Others said that the cauterization should be vigorous, affecting the bone strongly until it falls away, like a small piece of charcoal or a small flake. They claimed that this releases vapors from the head from that location, and the wound should be left open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals.  I do not consider these two types of cauterization at all, except in some people and by way of a gamble.  I consider leaving it alone to be better.  With safety, if the head weakens when its natural connections are dispersed, as we have seen in other organs.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cauterization is safer and better in my view.  Use that one, and you will be safe, God willing.
Second Chapter.
On cauterizing the head also.
If a chronic pain occurs throughout the head, and this affliction lasts for the patient, and if applications, plasters, and flogging, ointments, and bandages have been used, especially if the single cauterization we described has been applied and nothing has helped, then look. If the patient's head is strong in constitution and not weak, and if he experiences severe cold, cauterize again a little above that first cauterization. Then cauterize each horn of his head with a cauterization until the thickness of the skin disappears and the amount of bone we described is revealed. And cauterize a cauterization at the back of his head, in the place known as the axe, and be gentle here; do not expose the bone, for the patient experiences intense pain, unlike the pain of all other head cauterizations.  I will mention this cauterization in its place.
The cautery used for the horns and back of the head should be gentler than the cautery used for the middle of the head. This is its image.
Chapter Three.
In cauterizing the chronic sister.
If pain occurs in the cleft of the head, accompanied by headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then the patient should be purged with medicines that cleanse the head, and use all the other treatments that I have described in the sections of diseases.  If this is not successful, then cauterization in this case is twofold. Either by cautery with a sharp, burning medicine, or by iron.
As for cautery with a burning medicine, take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends. Then, with a wide scalpel, open the painful area of the temple until there is a subcutaneous space large enough to accommodate the clove.
Place it under the skin until it disappears, then press it firmly and tightly with bandages for about fifteen hours. Then remove it and take out the garlic. Leave the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Apply a cotton swab dipped in clarified butter until the area becomes suppurative. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you wish, you can use some of the burning medicines I have mentioned in the eighteenth article on burning medicines.
And as for cauterizing with iron, do it in this way: heat the cautery iron, which is illustrated in this image:
The cautery iron is called the nail-shaped cautery because its head resembles a nail, with some grooves and a small protrusion in the center.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while slowly turning the iron, so that the amount of skin burned is about half its thickness. Then lift your hand so you do not burn the artery underneath and cause bleeding, then soak a cotton swab in salt water and apply it to the area, leaving it for three days. Then wrap the cotton with oil, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, cauterize this vein with the sharp tip of the cautery iron, preserving the vein from being cut, especially in chronic cases.
Fourth Chapter.
Treating chronic veins.
If you have treated the vein using the aforementioned treatment and what we have mentioned in the classifications of diseases, and the treatment does not work, and you see a cause that does not respond to the initial cauterization with medication or cauterization with fire. Then you should heat the sharp-tipped cautery iron until it glows white, after marking the site of pain with a line of about half a finger's length or similar, and you should withdraw your hand once, and applying force until you sever the artery and reach the bone.
However, you must be careful not to touch the moving jaw during chewing, lest you burn the muscle or nerve that moves it, causing spasm.
And be cautious and vigilant about the bleeding from the artery you cut, for there is risk in severing it. Especially if one does not know what to do and has not practiced the procedure, abandoning it is better.
The treatment of accidental arterial bleeding will be described in its appropriate place in this book, God willing.  If you see a condition this cauterization cannot manage,
And if you see the patient's condition as suitable, cauterize the center of the head as described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you prefer, use the cauterization mentioned in the chapter on arterial ligature with the double-bladed cautery; it is a better and more effective cauterization than this one.
Fifth chapter.
On cauterizing earaches.
If an earache occurs due to cold and is treated with laxatives and other remedies mentioned in the classification, but the pain does not subside, cauterize with the cautery called the point, which is pictured thus.
Then, after heating it, apply it around the entire ear, or around both ears if the pain is in both, as you would move it around. Keep the cauterization slightly away from the base of the ear after marking the area with ink.  The cauterization should be about ten points on each ear, or thereabouts. Then, treat the areas until healed, God willing.
Chapter Six.
In cauterization of the abscess.
The abscess treated by cauterization is the type that arises from phlegm, as I described in the classifications of diseases.
One should avoid cauterizing the type that arises from dryness and nerve tension.
When you have treated this type of abscess with poultices, cupping, and gargles, and your treatment has been ineffective, then the affected area should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly below the cheekbone, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the opposite side of the affected area, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The cauterization should be performed with a cautery positioned opposite the upper part of the ear, slightly beneath the crown of the head, and another on the temple, its length being equal to the length of the thumb.
Apply the cautery until you have burned through about half the thickness of the skin.  This is the image of the cauterizing iron, a type of scalpel, similar to the previous image, but somewhat gentler, as you see. The knife should have a slight excess of thickness, then treat the affected area as previously described until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
On the cauterization of chronic paralysis.
If the paralysis has become chronic and you have treated it as described, and your treatment is ineffective and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize four times. A cauterization on each temple, a cauterization in the middle of the head as we described, and a cauterization at the back of the head, as previously mentioned.
The cauterization method as previously mentioned, and a cauterization can also be applied to the mouth of the stomach, which is more effective. Then treat as previously mentioned.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
The ailment caused by phlegm should first be treated by giving the patient large doses of purgatives and drugs that cleanse the brain.
Then his entire head is shaved, and a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on dressings, is applied repeatedly to his back.  This is a form of cauterization.
Follow the same procedure as mentioned there. If he recovers, leave it at that; otherwise, apply three cauterizations to the back of his head, arranged in a line from the top of the head to the base of the neck.
Place the distance of a finger's breadth between each cauterization.  Then treat the cauterizations as described above. If you wish to increase the treatment, and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle cauterization.
If you wish to increase the treatment, cauterize the two horns. Then treat it until he is healed.  The cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterization for paralysis and general body relaxation.
You should first cleanse the head with the procedures we have mentioned, then shave the patient's head and cauterize a burn in the center of the head, a burn on each horn of the head, and a burn on the back of the head.
And three on the cervical vertebrae. If, in a case of body relaxation, more than that is needed, and the patient can tolerate it, and the illness is strong and entrenched, then cauterize four burns on the vertebrae of his back. And cauterize deeply, until most of the skin is burned, then lift your hand, and treat it as previously mentioned, until he is healed, God willing.  The cautery should be olive-oil based.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterization for epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is due to phlegm should be cauterized.  First, his brain should be cleansed with large leeches and with all the other treatments we mentioned in the classification, if the patient is large and able to take medication.
However, if it is a child who cannot tolerate medication, then gargles and mouthwashes that cleanse the brain should be used many days beforehand, along with improving their diet.
Then his head is shaved, and one cauterization is applied in the middle of the head, as described before, and another in its back. And on each horn of his head, a cauterization. If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, apply the cauterizations mentioned for stroke and relaxation of the body on the cervical and dorsal vertebrae, and the cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is putrid moisture and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauterizations we mentioned for stroke.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess leaning towards black bile and the patient's body is moist and putrid, purge what cleanses the brain as previously described in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of linen like a circle, and place it in the center of his head. The patient sits crouched, holding onto it from each side. Then take one pound of old sheep's butter.
Then heat it over the fire to a moderate heat that the finger can tolerate if inserted into it, then pour it into the circle in the center of his head and let it cool. Do this to the patient once a week, along with good general treatment, until he recovers, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small, scattered dots, without holding the cautery in your hand, but rather with a puffing motion. This type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a linen cloth soaked in butter or chicken fat to it.
The twelfth chapter.
On cauterizing the water flowing from the eye.
If you observe the water flowing from the eye initially with the signs described in the classification, then immediately apply remedies to cleanse the head and protect it from all moisture.  Have him sweat in a bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize the middle of the head, and cauterize the temples twice if the water is flowing from both eyes or from one side if it is from one eye only.
Then, with the cautery, cut all the veins and arteries under the skin, and the cauterizations should be lengthwise and across the temples, while guarding against blood loss.
If you see something of it, cut it at the location with any possible treatment, and we will come to the wisdom regarding the ligation and cutting of arteries and the prevention of bleeding.
He may also be cauterized twice vigorously at the base of the skull, below the bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic weeping.
If the weeping of the eyes is chronic and persistent, and it is known to originate from the veins and arteries located on the surface of the head, exterior to the skull, and you are certain that it is due to thick, cold, phlegmatic excess, cauterize it with the cauterization that I have described specifically at the start of the descending water. A cauterization in the center of the head, and two cauterizations on each temple, and two cauterizations at the base of the skull, beneath the bones.  If further cauterization is required, cauterize on each side of the eye's socket, on the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
On cauterizing a foul-smelling nose.
If treatment with what we have previously mentioned in the classification proves ineffective, immediately bleed the patient from the temples three nights in a row, then shave his head and cauterize the center with an olive-oil cautery iron. Then cauterize with a nail-shaped cautery iron, two cauterizations above the eyebrows, slightly under the hair, keeping away from the artery, do not cut it.  This is the image of the nail-shaped cautery iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid is relaxed due to disease or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize above the eyebrows slightly, two cauterizations on each side, keeping a distance from the temples, and the length of each cauterization should be the length of the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cauterization, but only to the point where one-third of the skin is burned, and the shape of the cautery iron should be like this.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its lashes turn inward, damaging the eye, then there are two types of cauterization for it. Either cauterization by fire or cauterization by a burning medicine.  If cauterization by fire, instruct the patient beforehand to allow his lashes to grow long and even, if he is one who plucks them.
If he injured it while they were growing, bind his eyes with a bandage so they won't move until they grow.  Once they have grown long and even, place the patient's head on your lap. Then, mark the eyelid with ink in the shape of an as-leaf.
Begin the mark near the eyelashes. Then place a cotton swab soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a badger under the eyelid. Then heat a cautery iron—this is its image.
Then cauterize it in the shape you marked, little by little, many times until the surface of the skin, which is completely visible in the shape of an as-leaf, is burnt.  A sign of your successful work is to see the eyelid pulled tight and the hair raised from the eye's flesh.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days. Then apply a cotton swab with ghee until the scab comes off. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, if God wills. If any hair grows back later and the eyelid relaxes,
Then repeat the cauterization at that spot as you did first.  If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with the burning medicine, tell the patient to leave the eyelashes to grow long and even.
Then make a picture of an as-leaf from paper. Then take from the usual soap and quicklime, each one a weight of a dirham or similar, and grind them thoroughly. Do this quickly so they do not dry. Then spread some of it on the paper, shaped like an as-leaf, and place it on the one or both eyelids. Place a cotton swab soaked in egg white under the eye, and place the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on the medicine and gently press it while moving it whenever the patient feels the medicine's burning sensation, because it feels like fire.  As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine, moving it with your finger.  When the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And see if you see the eyelid has risen, as it rises when cauterized with fire or by cutting. If not, apply more of the medicine to the area where it hasn't taken effect and hasn't blackened, until you complete your work and the eye is cauterized. Then place cotton with oil on it to remove the burning sensation.
Then treat it with palm ointment or another, until it heals.  You must be extremely careful during the procedure to prevent any of the medicine from entering the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after a few days, and you fear something in the eye,
Then reapply the medicine to that relaxed area of the eyelid, specifically as you did before, and treat it until it heals. This is an image of an as-leaf.
Know that people's eyes vary in size and age.  Therefore, adjust your cauterization accordingly.  The correct method is evident to those skilled in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the tumor that appears in the eye's corner.
If you treated the tumor as described in the sections on diseases, and your treatment was unsuccessful, then it is necessary to cauterize it in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head in your lap, and have an attendant hold his head firmly, without movement or shaking.
Then place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or in the juice of a certain plant on his eyes, then heat the cautery iron, which is pictured here.
It should be hollow, shaped like a bird of prey's feather tube, on the cauterizing end.  If you wish, make the other end pierced, or if you wish, solid like a rod. However, the hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then, squeeze the tumor if it is open, and remove the matter from it and dry it, or if it is not open, then open it and remove its pus. Then, apply the intensely hot cautery to it, holding it with your hand until you reach the bone.
Keep your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose when cauterizing, so that your hand does not miss the mark or the patient becomes agitated, and the cautery falls on the eyelid and damages it.  If you reach the bone on the first application, otherwise, reheat the cautery a second time if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball soaked in ghee and treat it with a dry ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply the caustic, strong medicine until the bone is exposed and treat it as described in the relevant section, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing the tumor, mentioned by some of the ancients, is to carefully incise the tumor and then place a thin funnel within the incision.  This is its image.
Pour into it an amount of molten lead equal to the weight of a dirham.  Hold the funnel firmly in your hand, and the patient must remain completely still so that the molten lead does not drip into their eye.  A cotton ball soaked in egg white or water should be placed over the patient's eye.
The lead will burn the tumor and cure it remarkably.  If the tumor heals with the cauterization and treatment mentioned, then no further action is needed. If not, it will be necessary to use nasal perforation and return the tumor to the nasal passage, as will be detailed in the specific section.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing cleft lip.
A cleft in the lip, often called a fissure, frequently occurs, especially in the lips of young boys. If the cleft is treated as described in the section, but the treatment is ineffective, a small, knife-like cautery iron should be heated and applied in this manner.
Its cavity should be as thin as a knife, and then it should be placed hot and quickly in the same cleft until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft.  Then treat it with qirrot (a type of medicinal plaster) until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing a fistula in the mouth.
If a tumor appears on the gum, palate, or roots of the teeth, then ulcerates and bursts, forming a fistula from the discharge of pus, and treatment proves ineffective, then a cautery iron should be heated to fit the size of the fistula.
Then, insert it hot into the fistula's opening, holding it until the heated iron reaches its deepest part. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterward as previously described until it heals, God willing.
If the material is cut off and healed, well and good. Otherwise, the area must be examined and the diseased bone removed, as will be described in its section, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Loose teeth and gums.
If the gums have loosened due to moisture, and the teeth have moved, and remedies have failed, then place the patient's head on your lap and heat the cautery iron, its image to come later, after placing the tube onto the tooth.
Quickly insert the hot cautery iron, and hold your hand for a short while until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth, then remove your hand. Then, repeat the cautery as desired, after which the patient should fill their mouth with salt water.
He should hold it for an hour and then spit it out.  The loose tooth will be stabilized, the relaxed gums will tighten, and the bad moisture will dry.
Chapter Twenty-one.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is caused by cold or worms, and medication is ineffective, cauterization can be performed in two ways: either with oil or with fire.
For cauterization with oil, take cow's butter and boil it in an iron spoon or a shell.  Then take a cotton ball, wrap it around the end of a rod, dip it into the hot butter, and place it on the aching tooth quickly.  Hold it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth.  Alternatively, if you wish, dip wool or cotton in cold butter, place it on the aching tooth, and put the heated iron on top until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterizing with fire, use a brass or iron tube, slightly thick-walled to prevent the heat from reaching the patient's mouth.  Then heat the appropriate cautery iron, place it on the same tooth, and hold it until the iron cools. Repeat this several times.
The pain will cease either that same day or the next.  Following the cauterization, the patient should fill their mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out. This is a depiction of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you prefer, according to what is possible.  This is a depiction of the tube.
The Twenty-second Chapter.
On cauterizing swine.
If the swine are affected by phlegm and cold humors, and do not respond to ripening with medication, and you desire rapid ripening, then heat the hollow cautery, the image of which is shown with both ends pierced.
To allow smoke to emerge from the opposite end during the cauterization, place the protected cautery on the same tumor once, and again if necessary, until it reaches the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, adjust the cautery to fit the size of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply a cotton ball dipped in clarified butter until what was burned by the fire disappears, then treat it with ointment and bandages until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humidity predominates in the trachea, especially if the temperament is cold, the patient should first be purged with laxative medicines, then cauterized with a cautery on the neck, at the base of the throat, in the lower position.
Be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, and only burn half of the skin.  Then cauterize again at the joint of the neck, at the end of the last bead, intensely.  Let the cautery be a nail-shaped one, as described before.
Then treat him with what was previously mentioned until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
In cauterizing lung disease and coughs.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold moisture, and there is no fever or consumption in the patient, and the illness is chronic, cauterize twice above the collarbones, in the soft, hollow, lower areas, and once more in the center of the chest between the breasts.
And the cautery should be pointed, as described previously.  If you prefer, your cauterization can be done with a drop cautery, the one called the "point," a description of which is provided in the section on treating earache.  The number of points should be from thirty to approximately that amount.
Then treat him with what was previously mentioned until he is healed.  You may also use a cautery with three branches, in this manner.  Using this, cauterize more quickly because you cauterize three times in one application.  This is a depiction of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus has dislocated due to slippery moisture, or if it was not properly repositioned when reducing the dislocation, so that it habitually dislocates again and dislocates at the slightest movement, as we have observed, then the joint must first be reduced. Then the patient lies on their back or on their right side.  Then the skin inside the armpit is raised upwards by the fingers of your left hand if the joint has dislocated inwards.
Then heat the double-pronged cautery, which is pictured here.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side.  The form of the cauterization is four cauterizations. It may be cauterized with a three-pronged cautery; in that case, the form of the cauterization will be six cauterizations, and the prongs are on the surface of the wound.
And this is the image of the three-pronged cautery.
One more may be added to this number, making the cauterizations eight. Then, apply the crushed balls mixed with salt to the cauterization.  Then the patient should be kept at rest and not move the limb for a time until it strengthens. If the dislocation was upward, that rarely happens.
Cauterize above the shoulder with one good, nail-like cauterization, or with many small cauterizations; then the joint will strengthen, the moisture will disappear, and the patient will be cured.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In cauterizing the stomach.
If a cold and excessive moisture develops in the stomach, causing its temperament to be disrupted, and frequent discharges occur, and various treatments have been ineffective, then the patient should lie on their back, extend their legs and arms, and then be cauterized three times. Cauterize below the breastbone to the extent of a finger using a nail-like cauterizing iron, and cauterize two more points below, each one at the sides of the initial cauterization, so that the cauterizations form a triangle.  Keep them apart so that they do not unite if they become suppurated. The depth of the cauterizations should be about two-thirds the thickness of the skin. The shape of the cauterizations should be [as described].
In this form and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a single large cauterization in the middle of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cauterizing iron used for this single cauterization should be circular in shape, in this manner.
The stomach can also be cauterized by pricking, for those who are hesitant about this cauterization.  Mark the stomach with dots to the desired extent with ink, then cauterize the dots with a pointed cauterizing iron. Then treat it with the aforementioned treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On cauterizing the cold liver.
If the liver presents pain due to coldness and dampness, or due to a boiling wind, such that it has departed from its natural disposition to an excessive degree, and the patient has been treated as described in the classification, yet this has not been effective. Then the patient should lie on their back, and three cauteries should be applied with ink to the liver, in this shape and this exact amount, below the clavicles, where the human forearm ends.
And after each cautery, there should be a space equal to the thickness of a finger.  The cauterization should be done along the body in a straight line, and do not press the cautery tool too forcefully.
The amount of skin burned should be about half its thickness, no more.  The patient should stand on their feet; if they cannot stand, they should lie down with their legs extended and arms raised.  This is the shape of the cautery tool.
You may perform these cauteries with a knife-like cautery tool if you have skill and dexterity in the craft, and take precautions to avoid over-cauterizing, burning the entire skin, burning the abdomen, and reaching the intestines. The skin there is thin, so be aware of this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cauterizing a liver tumor.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know if the tumor is in the liver's flesh or its lining, if it is in the liver's flesh, the patient will experience heaviness and pain, but without sharpness.
If it is in the liver's lining, the pain will be sharp and intense.  And if the physicians have exhausted treatment options, the patient should lie on their back, and then mark the swollen area with ink.
Then, heat the cautery iron in the fire; it is a cautery iron resembling a spatula. This is its image. Cauterize with a single cautery until the entire skin is burned and the cauterization reaches the lining, so that all the matter comes out.  Then treat it with an abscess treatment until it heals.
This type of cauterization should only be used by those whose knowledge of medicine is extensive and who have treated these illnesses repeatedly by experience.  Only then should they undertake such a procedure; otherwise, abandoning it is preferable.
Chapter Twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients recommended cauterizing cold fistulas with the roots of the zuraund plant in this manner:  Take a single, longest, dried zuraund root, as thick as a finger.  Then dip it in oil and hold it in the fire. Then cauterize once between the collarbone and the neck, and two small cauterizations slightly below the veins, angled toward the area under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, angled slightly backward; another in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach; and three cauterizations from behind, one between the shoulders, and two on the sides of the lower back, below the cauterization made between the shoulder blades.
One should not deeply cauterize, but rather cauterize superficially, on the surface of the skin. Some of the ancients have mentioned that some people used a heated iron tool, similar to a spear, and heated it then inserted it between the ribs until it reached the tumor.
The pus will come out, as we have mentioned in cases of liver tumors.  In this condition, cauterization carries the risk of the patient dying immediately or, alternatively, causing an incurable fistula at the site.
Chapter Thirty.
Cauterization of the spleen.
If, after treating the spleen as described in the classification, your treatment is unsuccessful, then cauterization can be done in three ways, all correct: one is to cauterize three or four times, aligned along the length of the spleen.
In the shape of liver cauterizations, previously described, with a space between each cauterization equal to or slightly exceeding the thickness of a finger.  The cautery iron should be applied as described for liver cauterization; do not insert your hand deep with the cauterization. The patient should lie on their back.
The other method is at the elbow.  Lift the skin opposite the spleen, where the patient's left elbow ends.  Raise the skin across the width of the body, so that the cauterizations are aligned along the body.
Then, carefully introduce the probes until they pierce the skin on the other side, then withdraw the cautery iron.  The cauterizations will be four.  If desired, use the other cautery iron with three points.
Then treat the cauterized area after allowing it to discharge pus for many days; this is more effective than any other previous treatment.
Chapter Thirty-one.
Concerning cauterization for dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in ascites, specifically.  If, after treating the dropsy patient with the methods of treatment we previously described in the classification, your treatment has been ineffective, then you should cauterize four times around the navel and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, and one on the spleen.  Two cauterizations behind his back, one opposite his chest and the other opposite his stomach.  The depth of the cauterization should be nearly the thickness of the skin. Then leave the cauterization open to discharge pus for a long time.
Do not discontinue treatment of the patient after the cauterization, but apply what is necessary to bring together the affected areas.  This will speed recovery. The shape of the cautery used for the back is, as previously described, nail-shaped, and the one used for the front is olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot at the point between the little finger and the ring finger.
Hold your hand with the cautery straight, do not bend it. Then lift your hand and do not return it; this will cause the yellow water to drain.  The cautery should be of this shape.
Then cauterize the legs, two cauterizations on each leg.  The cauterization should be with the sharp tip of the cautery.  Make one cauterization along the leg, one below the knee, and another lower than that, towards the middle of the leg. On each thigh, make two cauterizations.
Leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time so that the water drains from it, then treat it with the other methods mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-three.
On cauterizing diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is due to cold and dampness, weakening the digestive and binding forces in the stomach and intestines, and if various treatments have been ineffective, and you see the patient is strong enough to endure the cauterization, then cauterize a large area on their stomach. As previously mentioned, cauterize the stomach with a circular cautery, and make four cauterizations around the navel with a fine pointed cautery. Make one or two large cauterizations on the lumbar region above the coccyx.
If you see a significant amount of dampness, and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the pubic region, and one cauterization on each hip.  Possibly add two small cauterizations to the stomach near the large one. This is a successful and unfailing treatment, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cauterizing hemorrhoids of the anus.
If the anus has numerous chronic hemorrhoids, or a single one, composed of thick, cold humors or corrupt moisture, and the treatment mentioned in the previous section has been unsuccessful, cauterize the patient three times on the lower part of the lumbar spine, slightly below the table, in a triangular pattern.
And cauterize below the navel by the length of two fingers.  If you suspect the patient's stomach is cold and food is not digesting, and you see the face swollen, cauterize the stomach with a large cauterization as previously described and cauterize the liver. And another on the spleen with a pointed cautery. Leave the cauterization open for a time, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery, which resembles an awl, and insert it hot into the same cut wart, and press your hand until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as described, then treat them with ointments that are suitable for them until they are healed. If you cauterize a large area on the cotton, it is more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula that occurs in the perineum and its surroundings.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and the treatment described in its place and avoids it, perhaps the cautery will heal him. If a fistula occurs in someone and it is chronic, pus and corrupt humors flow from it, then the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then, determine the depth of its penetration with the probe, then heat the cautery iron, which resembles a needle, and insert it hot into the same fistula along the direction and depth where the probe entered.  Repeat the cauterization until all the corrupt bodies are burnt, once, twice, or thrice, as needed.
And be cautious of burning a nerve, if one exists, or a large vein, if the fistula opens into the bladder or the intestines.
And be cautious of all these locations.  Only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area and you judge it to be non-penetrative.  Then treat the area until it heals. If the area heals, the material stops and it remains like that for some time, know that it has healed completely.
If the material does not stop flowing, know that it is penetrating or has a corrupt bone in its depth, or something similar to that which will be mentioned in its proper place.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
On cauterizing the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys due to cold or thick wind, and the patient's ejaculate decreases as a result, one should cauterize the loins, on the kidneys themselves. Cauterize each kidney with the nail-shaped cautery, which was previously mentioned.  Perhaps we cauterize it a third time in the same area.  This results in three aligned cauterizations, which will be more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder due to cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot retain urine, cauterize with one cautery below the navel, on the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and another cautery to the right of the navel, and another to the left.
After each cautery on either side, cauterize to the size of a thumb's breadth, and cauterize once or twice more on the lower back if needed.  Use a nail-shaped cautery, as previously described.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
On cauterizing the uterus.
If the uterus contains a material of cold and dampness, preventing conception, corrupting her menstrual flow, and making her unable to urinate or causing pain at the time of menstruation, then three cauteries should be applied around the navel, as was described in the cauterization of the bladder. One cautery on the lower back, near the lumbar region, or two, using a nail-shaped cautery.
Chapter Forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Incorrect humors may accumulate in the hip joint, causing dislocation.  A sign is that one leg will be longer than the other when the legs are compared.  A space will be found where the hip is dislocated.  It is necessary to cauterize the affected area on the hip joint itself with a nearly circular cautery.
After marking the area around the joint, as if to draw a circle, place the affected joint in the center of the circle.  Use the cautery illustrated in the treatment of the stomach. If this cautery is unavailable, use three cauterizations with the large olive cautery.
Make the cauterization deep enough to encompass the entire thickness of the skin.  Then treat it until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Forty-one.
In cauterizing the sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint due to cold and dampness, and treatment according to our previous classification has been ineffective and prolonged, it is necessary to loosen the thick humors with rotten seeds or prickly pear seeds, and similar remedies.
Then cauterize.  The cauterization has two forms: either with burning medicines or with fire.  Cauterization with fire can be done in many ways.  One is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
And it will create a beneficial depth. The interval between each cauterization should be the thickness of a finger. The cautery should be olive-shaped.  One may also cauterize the very top of the hip joint itself with a single cauterization in the center, resulting in four cauterizations in total. Or, if you wish, you can cauterize it with the circular cautery previously mentioned for hip dislocation, applying a single cauterization.
So that the circle encompasses the entire hip and burns the entire thickness of the skin. This is the method of cauterization for sciatica, if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize twice on the thigh, at the location where the patient indicates the pain. Cauterize once above the heel, a little to the outer side, with four fingers' distance.  The cautery should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should be only the thickness of the skin.
And if he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize at the indicated location with a pinpoint cautery, three or four times, or more if needed. If he points to the pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize once with a knife-shaped cautery there.
In all your cauterizations, be careful not to cauterize a nerve or major artery, as this would inflict a serious injury or ailment on the patient.
I have witnessed two cases where a cauterization was performed above the heel, with excessive cauterization resulting in gangrene extending to the foot, causing its complete perforation and the destruction of the entire leg.  Subsequently, diarrhea and death followed.  If the pain is in both areas, cauterize them in the same manner.
Some wise scholars have described a cauterization of the hip using a cautery in the shape of a cup, made of iron, with a diameter of half a span. The rim of the cup should be as thick as a date pit, or slightly thinner. Within this cup is another cup, and a third.
The cups are spaced apart by the width of a thumb's circumference.  The cups are open on both sides and are about one or two thumbs in height.  A handle of iron is securely attached to the cups.  This is its design.
Then, it is heated in the fire until it glows red and sparks fly.  It is then placed on the affected hip area, with the patient resting on their right side. Three circular cauterizations are performed at once.  The patient is then left for three days, bandaged with ghee, and the wound is left open for several days.
Many more (cauterizations) are then performed with ointment until healing occurs. The author of this book states that this type of cauterization is rarely used due to its unpleasantness and frightening appearance, and few can endure it.  However, it is a good cauterization for those who can endure it and are affected in its location.
As for cauterization with burning medicines, it is done by making two cups resembling the rings into which the door hinges are fitted, either of copper or iron.
Their height will be approximately two thumbs, and the space between them will be the thickness of a finger.  The lower and upper parts will be open, with the cups held together in this manner.
Then, place them on the affected hip area with the patient lying on their right side.  Hold your hand gently. Then, pour a small amount of sharp water heated with fire between the circles.  Hold it for the time it takes a sandglass. The patient will endure the stinging and burning, as it will feel like fire.
Until the stinging subsides. Then, remove the instrument and wash the entire hip with fresh water. Leave it for three days and apply ghee until the burn's blackness disappears. Then leave it for several days to drain the pus, as it is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals.  If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for it, containing water, in the same manner as for the hip. This is the description of sharp water.  Its efficacy has been demonstrated in my treatise on the preparation of remedies.
Take one part each of lye and quicklime.  Grind them together and place them in a new pot with a single small hole at the bottom, large enough for a rod to enter.  Place another glazed pot underneath the base of the first pot.
Pour fresh water over the lye and lime to cover them by a finger's breadth after gently pressing them with your hand. Leave the pot until the sharp water runs into the lower glazed pot. Then, collect all the water and pour it over fresh lime and lye.
It will then be very potent, effective in many medical procedures and in cauterizing other limbs, because it acts like fire itself.  Some remedies also cauterize the hip, such as *thaafsia*, *baladhri* honey, and lime mixed with soap, all ground into a paste.
Galen, recounting a treatment from an ancient physician for hip and sciatica pain, greatly praised it, claiming that it needed no other treatment and cured in a single application.
Indeed, it is said that a person might enter the bath carried and emerge cured, by taking fresh green *shiṭraj* (or, if unavailable, fresh dry *shiṭraj*), finely grinding it with some fat and applying it to the hip, leg or thigh where the pain is.
Apply the dressing and leave it on for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside. Then have him enter a bath. Once his body is moist, place him in a basin.  The pain will subside and he will be healed, God willing. If he is not healed, reapply the dressing after ten days.
He will be healed, God willing.  Know that no one should use these cauterizing remedies until the body has been emptied. Dioscorides mentioned that goat's hair, when used to cauterize sciatica, is helpful.
The cauterization should be done like this: take wool, soak it in old oil, and place it on the deep area between the thumb and the elbow, closer to the elbow.  Then take dry goat's hair.
Ignite it with fire until it becomes embers, then place it on the wool and leave it until it dies out.  Repeat this until sensation reaches from the middle of the upper arm to the hip and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cauterization is called cauterization with hair.
Chapter Forty-two.
On cauterizing back pain.
Back pain may arise from various causes, such as a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like.  It may result from the effusion of cold, damp matter.  Cauterization is only effective in this type of case.
Following the patient's vomiting, with medicines like rotten herbs, the back should be cauterized where the pain is located.  Three rows should be made, equal to the width of the table.  Five cauterizations, or more, should be made in each row, depending on the patient's tolerance and strength, after marking the area with ink.
The cauterization should be performed with a pointed cautery.  Alternatively, three or four cauterizations with a medium-sized nail cautery may be performed in this way.
Chapter Forty-three.
In cauterizing the initial stage of the swelling.
This ailment often affects young children.  A sign of its initial stage in a child is shortness of breath when standing or moving, and at the end of the spine's vertebrae, a pearl-like prominence appears, protruding above the other vertebrae.
If you see this and wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery of this design. Apply the cautery evenly to each side of the vertebra.  If desired, cauterize around the vertebra with a pinpoint cautery in two or three rows, keeping the points close together.  Then treat the area until healed, as described.
And be mindful that cautery should be used for swellings that are due to nerve spasms.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pain.
If joint pain is due to cold dampness flowing to any part of the body, and if the pain occurs in the legs, physicians customarily call it gout, especially.
If the cold gout, its cause, is treated with the methods we have described in the classification, and the pain does not subside, cauterization will relieve it.  This is done by cauterizing the area around the joints of the legs with many cauteries after emesis. The cautery should be olive-oil based, of medium heat, in this manner.
If it is necessary to apply points on the leg, use the pointed cautery.  If the pain ascends to the knees or other joints, as often happens, cauterize each knee with three or four cauteries on each side, using the same olive-oil cautery.
If more cauterization is required, cauterize, but do not go deep with the cautery; it should only be to the thickness of the skin. If the pain ascends to the hips or back, use the cauterization mentioned in its section.
If the pain is only in the hands, apply points around the elbows in two rows. If the pain persists in the fingers, apply a point to each knuckle and to the palm. If, after a few days, the pain ascends to the forearms or shoulders,
Cauterize both sides.  The patient must also follow good treatment and take medicine.  If he/she improves their diet and expels phlegm, he/she will recover with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter Forty-five.
On cauterizing hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin, and some of the intestines and bowels descend into the testicle, and this is a recent occurrence, the patient should be instructed to abstain from eating that day and use laxatives to evacuate his bowels.
Then, the patient should lie on his back before you, and you should instruct him to restrain himself until the bowels or intestines protrude.  Then, you should return them with your finger, and then mark the area under the hernia on the pubic bone with ink, a mark resembling a semicircle, its ends towards the upper body.
Then, heat a cauterizing iron, this is its form.
Until it becomes white and throws sparks.  Then, return the bowels or intestines to their place inside him.  Then, have a servant place his hand on the area so that the bowels do not protrude again.  Support the patient's legs apart and place a cushion underneath.  Another servant should hold the legs together.
And another on his chest, holding his hands.  Then, place the cauterizing iron on the same mark, with your hand holding the iron straight up.  Hold it until it reaches the bone or apply it again if it did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
Be careful to prevent the bowels from protruding while you cauterize, so as not to burn the patient, which may cause either death or a serious injury.  Understand that if the cauterization does not reach the bone, your treatment will not be successful.  The cauterizing iron should be appropriate for the patient's age, i.e., gentler for children and more robust for adults.
Then, treat the cauterized area with oil after three days to remove the burnt marks.  Then, treat it with other ointments until it heals.  The patient should lie on their back for forty days until the wound is completely closed.
His diet during treatment should be such as to soften his bowels, so that the bowels do not protrude during bowel movements.  After forty days, if he wishes to stand, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days.  He should reduce exertion, overeating, and excessive drinking and loud noise.
If he follows this procedure, he will be completely healed, God willing.  I will discuss the treatment of hernias by incision in its proper chapter, God willing.  As for a hernia that occurs anywhere on the abdomen and is just beginning, if you want to prevent it from increasing, cauterize the hernia with a circular cautery that fits its size. Burn approximately a third of the skin. Then treat it as described; it will not worsen, God willing. Some earlier physicians have suggested cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After drawing a line with ink, the line should be at the top of the hernia and another line at the bottom, with your hand going down the center with one single, pointed cautery. The first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If, in some part of the body, a fall or blow causes pain that persists for a long time and is not relieved by medication, it is necessary to cauterize with a pinpoint cautery. The cauterization of each part of the body should be in accordance with its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
And if the pain and fistula are alleviated, then the patient will be cured from the first cauterization.  Otherwise, repeat the cauterization, for it is the nature of these pains to spread from one part to the parts nearby.  Thus, it is necessary to follow up with cauterization until the patient recovers.
Chapter Forty-seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for those afflicted with leprosy, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that results from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize him, observe.  If the leprosy is in its initial stage, and you have treated it as described in the division, and it has not deteriorated or stopped, and you fear that corruption will take hold of all the patient's humours, cauterize the head five times. The first in the usual center of the head, the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, two on the temples, and one from behind on the base of the neck.  Apply the cauterization thoroughly until it affects the bone slightly.
And scabs will peel off, making it easier for thick vapors to escape.  Cauterize the spleen as described previously.  However, if the leprosy has persisted and become clearly apparent, then these cauterizations on the head, which we have described, should be applied. One cauterization on the tip of the nose, two on each cheek, two on the vertebrae of the neck, six on the vertebrae of the back, one large one on the coccyx at the base of the tail, another above it in the same area, and two on each thigh, one on each hip. Two on each knee, one on each knee, two on the shoulders, two on the elbows, two on the ribs, and cauterize each joint of the fingers and toes, a cauterization on each heel and each wrist.
And protect the nerve behind the heels so that you do not burn them.  A cauterization may be applied to the pubic bone, another to the opening of the stomach, and another to the liver.  Know that the more cauterizations you apply, the more beneficial and effective they will be.
Know that the patient feels less pain from the cauterizations than a healthy person, because their body is numb.  The cauteries should be of varying sizes, depending on the organs and joints, according to the characteristics of the irons described previously.
Then treat the cauterization with fine carob powder and honey, and with other remedies, until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter Forty-eight.
Concerning cauterization of numb areas: If a part of the body is numb and treated with medications, ointments, and dressings, but does not heal, cauterize the same numb area with cauteries, according to the size of the affected part of the body.  Let your cauteries be slightly smaller than the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  Sometimes, cauterization is used for the numbness that occurs in the hand or foot due to spinal problems at the point where the nerve that moves that limb exits, and this will remove the numbness.
Only those knowledgeable about the anatomy of the organs and the points of exit of the nerves that move the body should undertake this.
Chapter Forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of leprosy.
If leprosy persists and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it with a slight depth, proportionate to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and the color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil, and the leaves of the common mullein, pigeon blood, or the blood of hookbills, one part of each. Mix everything together, apply to a cloth, and bandage the area until healed.
Chapter Fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop its progress, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it revolves.  Some scholars have suggested a severe cauterization in the middle, but I do not recommend that, for I fear it will ulcerate, and I have witnessed this on several occasions.
Therefore, the correct method is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we mentioned, or with many small cauterizations.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On the cauterization of the abscess.
If an abscess occurs and is slow to mature, either because of the agent causing it, or the age of the patient (if elderly and with little blood), or due to time, and you wish to hasten its maturation, cauterize around it with many small, dotted cauterizations.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.  If you wish to apply further cauterization, heat the cauterizing iron which is depicted as follows.
Lower it into the center of the abscess until it penetrates the skin.  Let the cauterization be situated below, so that the discharge of pus is easier. Then treat it appropriately until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On cauterizing ulcers.
Ulcers are nothing but corruption that spreads throughout the organ, consuming it as fire consumes dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a place where cauterization with fire is appropriate, heat a cauterizing iron with many small and large nails, according to what is suitable for the place where the ulcer is.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing remains of it at all.  Then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur with oil to the cauterized areas, so that all the scabs and all corruption are removed.
Then treat it with healing ointments.  If after three days you see that the flesh grows healthily, without any corruption, then leave it alone. Otherwise, repeat the cauterization on the remaining corrupt areas. Ulcers can also be treated with strong medicine.
For it acts as a substitute for fire, with faster benefits.  I have mentioned its treatment with strong medicine in the division.  So take it from there when you need it.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On cauterizing impacted and non-impacted nails.
This affliction often occurs on the soles of the feet; it is a rough and painful condition.  Cauterization can be performed in two ways: either with fire or with strong caustic water.
As for cauterization by fire, heat a hollowed-out cauterizing iron, similar to an eagle's feather, crafted from iron, to completely encircle the nail.  Its border should be thin.  Then lower it, heated, onto the nail, and rotate your hand with the cauterizing iron around the nail until the iron reaches the nail's depth.
Leave it for three days to produce pus. Then, bandage it with crushed wild herbs mixed with salt. Leave the bandage on overnight.  The nail will then detach from its roots.  Then treat the wound with a healing ointment until it heals; this is for non-impacted nails.
It often occurs on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet.  Thus, one should take a tube of copper, iron, or eagle's feather and apply it to the nail or wart.  Then pour a small amount of strong caustic water into the tube.
Hold the tube, rotating it while gently pressing your hand so the edge of the tube affects the base of the nail and the caustic water penetrates to the root of the nail. The patient should endure the stinging sensation of the caustic water for a short while.
Then leave it; the nail will detach from its roots.  Do this with each one until all such occurrences on the body are addressed.  Then treat the affected areas with healing ointments after the detachment.
Chapter Forty-five.
On cauterizing the inflamed.
If an inflammation occurs due to cold in the nerve, or from a fever, or otherwise, it is necessary to cauterize four or five times on the back, with a cauterization between each bead, and a cauterization on the chest. And cauterize his stomach with an olive oil cautery, for the inflammation will subside and hasten the maturation of the cold disease.
Chapter Fifty-five.
On cauterizing a wound that has arisen in the body.
Ugly boils may arise in the body due to thick, corrupted cold materials.  Upon their first appearance, a gentle cauterization should be applied to the head of each boil using a heated stick or the long root of a plant or a lens-shaped cautery.
Boils, in their initial emergence, may be cauterized in this manner.  Thus, the active cause of the boil will not increase, but will dissipate, and the patient will recover.  However, this should only be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter Fifty-six.
On cauterizing bleeding from a severed artery.
Bleeding often occurs from a severed artery in a wound from the outside, or when a tumor or organ is incised or cauterized, and similar cases.  It is often difficult to stop.
If this occurs, quickly place your index finger over the opening of the artery, gently pressing to constrict the blood beneath your finger, so that nothing escapes.  Then, take several small and large olive-oil cauterizing irons and heat them intensely in the fire.
Then, take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery has ruptured, and apply the cauterizing iron to the same vein after quickly removing your finger.  Hold the cauterizing iron until the bleeding stops.
If the blood spurts out when you remove your finger from the artery opening, and if the cauterizing iron has cooled, quickly take another cauterizing iron from the heated ones and continue to use one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn the nerve, for this would cause additional suffering to the patient.  Know that if an artery is bleeding, it cannot be severed, especially a large one, except by one of four methods: either cauterization, as we have said, or amputation if it has not already been severed. If it is severed, the ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.  Alternatively, it can be tied tightly with threads, or medicaments that can stop the bleeding and firmly compress the area can be applied.
As for those who attempt to stop it by tying, compressing with cloth, or applying burning objects, and the like, it will not stop in this manner, except rarely.  If anyone is presented with this situation and a physician or medicine is not readily available, they should immediately place their index finger over the opening of the wound itself, as we have described.
He should firmly compress it until the blood is contained and emerges from above the wound and the artery, and the finger should not be removed from it; continually apply very cold water to it until the blood clots and thickens and stops flowing.  During this time, assess what further cauterization or medication is needed.
Chapter Two.
On the incision, lancing, phlebotomy, and similar surgeries.
In the first chapter, we discussed every illness amenable to cauterization with fire and caustic medications, their causes, reasons, and instruments; and the various types of cauterization.  We arranged this in sections, from the head to the foot.
I will follow the same approach in this chapter to make it easier for the student to find what they need.
Before I begin, you, my children, should know that this chapter contains more uncertainties than the first chapter on cauterization.
Therefore, caution must be greater here, for in this chapter, actions frequently result in the loss of life-sustaining blood when opening a vein or cutting through a tumor, lancing an abscess, treating a wound, removing an arrow, or cutting out a stone, and similar procedures, all of which are accompanied by risk and fear, and in most cases, lead to death.
I advise you to avoid actions involving doubt, for in this profession, you may encounter various kinds of people suffering from different illnesses. Some may be weary of their illness and indifferent to death.
Due to the severity of their illness and the prolonged suffering, their condition may indicate death. Some offer you their wealth in hopes of regaining health, and their illness is fatal, so you should not aid those who display such characteristics.
Let your caution exceed your desires and eagerness. Do not undertake anything without certain knowledge of the desired outcome. In all your treatments of patients, use advanced knowledge and warn them of the potential outcome for their well-being.
This will aid you in gaining praise, glory, and remembrance. May God, my children, inspire you with wisdom, and may He not deprive you of correctness and success. For that is in His hands; there is no god but Him.
I have arranged this chapter's sections following the previous chapter on cauterization, from the head to the feet, so that you may easily find what you need, God willing.
The First Chapter.
On the treatment of water that collects in the heads of children.
This ailment often occurs in infants at birth if the midwife presses the infant's head roughly.  It may also arise from a hidden, unknown cause.
I have not seen this ailment in anyone but infants, and all those I saw succumbed quickly to it.  Therefore, I have deemed it best to refrain from treatment.  Indeed, I have seen an infant whose head filled with water, and whose head grew larger every day. Until the infant could no longer sit up due to the size of its head, and the moisture increased until it perished.
This moisture either collects between the skin and bone or under the bone on the peritoneum.  If the moisture is between the skin and bone and the swelling is small, then one horizontal incision should be made in the middle of the head, about two fingers in length, to allow the moisture to drain.  This is the image of the lancet.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling larger, make two intersecting incisions like this.
If the moisture is under the bone, and the sign of this is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side, and the water sinks inwards when pressed with your hand—this should not be hidden from you—then three incisions should be made in the middle of the head, like this.
After the incision, all the moisture will come out. Then the incisions should be closed with strips and bandages.  Then it should be covered from above with poultices and oil until the fifth day.  Then the bandages are removed and the wound is treated with lint and ointments. Do not restrict the head's movement excessively. Feed the patient a diet of all dry foods low in moisture.
Until the limb strengthens and heals.  Another method of incision is to observe where the swelling's bone and the accumulation of water are visible, because it may be more in the back of the head, or the front, or the right, or the left.
Therefore, aim the incision where the swelling and the water accumulation are apparent.  Make the incision as best you can, taking care not to sever an artery, causing bleeding that could kill the patient from the hemorrhage, while draining the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Severing the two arteries behind the ears, known as the *ḥasīsīn*.
If someone suffers from a severe affliction of the eyes or chest, and this persists despite treatment with medications, the best treatment in such cases is to sever these two arteries.
If you wish to sever them, you should first shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to expose the artery.
