Then, hold the patient's neck firm with his own garment, and look for where the vein pulsates, at the two recessed areas behind the ears.  These locations are rarely hidden, except in some people.
Then, mark them with ink, and sever them with a dissecting scalpel, completely to the bone.  This should be done with the head oriented horizontally.  If you prefer, introduce the scalpel beneath the artery, and cut upwards.  The incision should be about two closed fingers in length.
If the artery is severed, the blood will flow in a pulsating fashion, forward and rapidly, in a continuous stream. If the artery is not visible, then you should estimate from the ear, about three fingers' breadth, and mark it with ink and cut to the bone.  What is necessary to...
Six ounces of blood will likely be released immediately on average; sometimes more, sometimes less.  This depends on the patient's strength and the fullness of their arteries.  Then, examine the wound. If any part of the peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it away.
So that a hot swelling does not occur. Then, bind the wound with a linen bandage and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
The arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic headaches or acute illnesses brought on by sharp humidity and heat in the muscles of the temples, or from severe chronic headaches, and the usual medical treatments have proven ineffective, we have used the method of cauterizing or cutting the arteries of the temples, as described.
The method for cutting them is that the patient should shave the hair from the temples, and locate the visible artery in the temple, as its pulse will be apparent; it is rarely hidden, except perhaps in some individuals.
Or, if it is difficult to locate the artery due to extreme cold, the patient should tighten their neck with their clothing, then you should rub the area with a cloth, or apply warm water to the area until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the scalpel, which has this shape. Then, gently slice the skin until you reach the artery, then insert a hook and pull it upward until you remove it from the skin and free it from the membranes beneath it on all sides.  If the artery is thin, twist it with the hook. Then cut off a piece of the artery, just enough to separate its ends and cause contraction without bleeding. If it is not severed and cut, the blood will not recede.  Then drain the blood from six to three ounces.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with strong doubled thread, whether of silk or bowstring, so that gangrene does not set in before the wound heals, thereby causing further bleeding.
Then cut away the excess between the ligatures, doing this at that time or later. If you wish, cauterize it down to the bone with a knife-shaped cautery, until its ends are severed, achieving the same result or even better, as we said.
However, if the patient has a fever or a heated temperament, cauterization helps to consume moisture, making it more beneficial.  After the arteries have been treated, the area should be packed with old linen and secured with firm bandages.  After healing, treat with dry medications to promote tissue growth and with lint until healing, God willing.
If, during your work, bleeding occurs from the artery, quickly stop it, either by cauterization or by filling the area with a sealant and applying pressure until the bleeding stops.  If none of these is available, apply your finger until the blood clots.
Apply very cold water to the area to reduce inflammation and secure it properly.  A gentler and easier method than treating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the areas with ink.
Apply the cautery very intensely until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed at two points, so that the ends are separated.  This cauterization will not allow them to rejoin.  This is an image of the cautery.
The cautery is sharp-bladed, resembling small daggers, but significantly less sharp than a knife.  If they were as sharp as a knife, the cold would reach them faster and the flesh would not quickly sever.  However, if there is some thickness to them, the heat of the fire will stay contained.
It quickly severs the flesh.  This method is better than all others, and gentler and easier.  The distance between the blades should be the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In treating the constant, hot watery discharge from the eyes.
If the discharge of tears is constant, originating from the veins on the skull's exterior, and no medicinal treatment is effective, and you see the patient's face has reddened. And he feels a tingling sensation on his forehead, like an ant's crawling. His eyes are sunken and wet, their edges worn and their eyelids glued shut from the intensity of the tears.  The early practitioners of this treatment were forced to employ this method, which they call the "treatment with the sword". It involves ordering the patient to shave the hair on his forehead.
Then, three parallel incisions are made on the forehead, running along the length of the forehead. Each incision is approximately two fingers in length, parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second one slightly away from the action of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the opposite side.
And beware of cutting the arteries on either side, keeping your hand away from the contact of the jaws.  After each incision, there should be a space of about three fingers clenched together. Have at the ready prepared sponges or numerous dry cloths for absorbing the blood.
Then, insert the sharp double-ended lancet, whose image is this. From the incision next to the temple, to the middle incision, use it to remove all skin and periosteum between the incisions. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision. Then withdraw the lancet. And insert into the first incision another instrument, called a single-edged, sharp knife, smooth and blunt on the other side. This is its image.
Place the sharp side of the knife upwards towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and the smooth side towards the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision, cutting all the vessels descending from the head to the eyes, without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do the same from the middle incision to the other incision.  After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, compress the areas of coagulated blood. Then, in each incision, place a cotton wick and apply a compress soaked in a mixture of vinegar and oil, or oil alone.
So that a hot swelling does not occur. On the third day, the ligatures are removed and copious application of lukewarm water is used. Then, treat it with basil ointment mixed with rose oil, and other remedies for wounds, until healed, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In treating tears and discharges to the eyes from the inside of the head.
When someone has frequent, severe, persistent discharges, and the eyes are weakened, small, and have poor vision, and the eyelids are ulcerated, and the eyelashes fall out. And there will be a sharp, painful headache deep within the head, and repeated sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these substances and discharges come from numerous deep veins. The best treatment is to have the patient shave their forehead, and then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there will be a sharp, painful headache deep within the head, and repeated sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these substances and discharges come from numerous deep veins. The best treatment is to have the patient shave their forehead, and then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or slightly higher, laterally, beginning the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and the incision should be into the bone. Keep your hand away from the movable temple muscles when chewing.  Once the bone is exposed and all the blood has been wiped away with a sponge.
Separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or linen thread, then tie it from above with bandages.  Soak the bandages with medicine and oil to prevent a hot swelling.  Once you have treated it and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a desiccated remedy that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts wheat flour and four parts of clyphonium and preparing an ointment from them to use in promoting flesh growth in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of things that fall into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or a stone-like object such as iron and glass, or a seed or plant part such as chickpeas, nuts, and the like, or a liquid substance such as water and vinegar, and the like.
Or if it is an animal, if a stone or a stone-like object has fallen into the ear, and it does not grow within the ear, expose the ear to the sun.  If you see the stone, put some violet or syrgia oil on it, then try to remove it by moving the head or by inducing a sneeze with a khandas. And plug the nostrils when the sneeze comes, after placing a bandage of linen or wool around the ear and extending the ear upwards.  This treatment often removes the object. If it does not come out, try to remove it with a gentle, soft cloth that has the following description.
If it comes out with the cloth, otherwise, try removing it with a delicate, slightly bent, blind hook. If it does not come out with that, then make a copper tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal.
Seal around the tube with softened pitch mixed with oil, so that the wind has no other way than through the tube.  Then pull it out forcefully with your breath.  This often removes it.  If it does not come out with the described methods, take a small piece of Nabataean gum or the kind used to catch birds and place it at the end of the tube.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, gently insert it into the ear canal after drying the ear from moisture.  If it does not come out with all the methods described, proceed to incision before a burning swelling or inflammation occurs.
The method of incision is to first bleed the patient in the temples, taking blood according to their strength.  Then, have the patient sit before you and turn their ear upward.  Make a small incision at the base of the ear near the cartilage, in the lower part of the ear.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone. Then remove it with the best tools possible.  Then quickly stitch the incision and treat it until it heals.
If the object that fell into the ear is one of the kinds of seeds that swell and enlarge, try to remove it with the methods mentioned. If it does not come out, take a slender, delicate scalpel shaped like this.
Try to cut that type of fallen seed in the ear with it.  Do this only if you are certain the seed has become moistened by the ear's moisture, so that it becomes numerous small pieces. Then remove them with a blind probe, or a gentle forceps, or by suction.
As mentioned, it will be easy to remove them.  As for water that has entered the ear, the patient should first use a handkerchief to induce sneezing.  The patient should lie on the ear that has water in it, having first filled the ear with some old cotton.  If it comes out that way, that's good.
Otherwise, take many small, smooth stones, warm them slightly over the fire. The patient inserts one into the ear canal. The patient then hops on one leg, toward that side, and strikes another stone against the stone in the ear.
He continues doing this, stone by stone, until all the water comes out.  Water may also be removed by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and heating the other end in the fire until it is mostly burnt.  Then another feather is used.
Repeat this until all the water is removed, or extract it with a tube, as previously mentioned for stones.  As for removing an animal that has entered the ear, if it is small, like a flea, or the like, treat it using what was previously mentioned in the section on the division of [problems]. As for...
If it is large enough to be felt, attempt to remove it with swabs and forceps.  This is easier than anything that grows in the ear.  As for removing worms that have developed in the ear, if treatment using what we have discussed in the section on the division of the topics on catarrh has not been successful.
Therefore, you should examine the ear in the sun. If any worms appear, remove them with swabs or fine forceps. If nothing is visible, take a tube, the illustration of which is as follows.
The tube is narrow at the bottom and wide at the top.  Insert the thin end into the ear, as far as the patient can tolerate, and then suck strongly with it.  Do this repeatedly until all the worms are removed.  If this does not work, seal around the tube with wax, as I have previously described for the stones.
If it does not come out with the methods described, use the ear drops that I have mentioned.  The ancients have used these to kill the worms, and you can find this in the section on ear drops.  You use the drops and medicines in the ear with this device, which has the following illustration:.
As you see, it is made of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, and wide at the top.  If you prefer, the tube within the main tube can be made of secure copper.  Alternatively, you can take a thread and wrap it around the end of the tube with a tight knot.
Then pour the ointment or juice, or whatever medicine you desire, into the tube while it is in the ear.  Then, insert the thread with the cotton on top and squeeze it with your hand in a moderate way, so the ointment flows into the ear and creates a sensation.
Have the patient lie down inside, and make sure whatever you put in the ear is warmed slightly in the fire.  Be careful that what you are dripping into the ear is not excessively cold or excessively hot, as the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of obstructions of the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear canals unpierced.
Some people may also experience an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or a growth of flesh there.  This obstruction may be deep within the ear canal, hidden from sight.
It may also present slightly outside the canal, and be visible.  That which is deep in the ear, and not visible, is in most cases difficult to cure.  If it is visible, then the affected ear should be placed in the sun. And examine it. If you see the obstruction externally, then open it with a gentle scalpel, in this form:
Its tip will have a slightly limited projection, while the rest of the scalpel will be smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, then grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently and thoroughly until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a smooth, gentle probe, warm it slightly in the fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction, along with the patient experiencing a heaviness of hearing, gently remove it.
Be careful not to injure the ear's nerve. Then, insert a wick into the ear, sized according to the canal opening, and smeared with Egyptian ointment.  Do this for several days until the wound is healed. Or, take a wick, soak it in water, and sprinkle powdered alum on it.
Use it. If you see that the ear has become swollen with a hot tumor, then it is necessary to remove the wick and replace it with another wick smeared in Qirawi ointment, made with rose oil, to reduce the hot swelling. Then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear.  Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding, as described in the section on bleeding.
Chapter Eight.
On treating warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  One must grasp them with a forceps or needle, and cut them from their roots with a scalpel, then apply powdered alum to the area.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-shaped cautery iron.  Cauterizing is better after cutting.  It often returns if any part of the roots remain.  If cauterized with fire, those roots are burned away and it does not return.
Chapter Nine.
On treating cold afflictions of the eyelids.
A condition resembling cold in its severity and hardness often appears on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called "cold" (برد), being a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids.  If the cold is protruding on the surface of the eyelid, moving in every direction, the treatment is simple: make a slit across it.
Then gently peel it from all sides until it is released, then hang it with a hook and cut it off.  If cutting it does not appear possible without penetrating the eyelid, that will not harm the patient.  If the slit is large, gather it with stitching and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no problem; the ointment will heal and mend it. If the cold is sloping inwards towards the inner surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid inside out, hang the cold with a hook without needing a slit, and trim it from every side.
If it penetrates the eyelid with the cut, that will not harm anything. Then wash the eye with salt water after cutting off the cold, and treat the area with what will heal it until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
On treating the hordeolum that appears on the eyelid.
A hordeolum is a swelling that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid.  It occurs most often in young boys, causing their eyes to feel heavy, and it leads to colds in the early hours of the morning. They are unable to look at the sun's light. So that the tears hasten to them.
Therefore, you see them leaning on their faces and sleeping on them, with their eyelids under the eyebrows damp, swollen, and the hordeolum clearly visible.  If you press the area with your fingers, you feel the hordeolum between them.  The treatment is to have the patient place their head in your lap.
Then, take a linen cloth, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick large enough to encircle the hordeolum from every side.  Then place it over the hordeolum and press with your fingers from every side to gather the hordeolum in the center of the circle.
Then, make a crosswise incision in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, an incision no larger than that made in bloodletting.  As for the depth, you should cut through all the skin until you reach the hordeolum.
In most cases, the hordeolum will protrude from the incision as a white fatty piece.  Pull it out with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, moving your hand right and left, until it heals.  Be careful not to enlarge the incision, lest the lancet injure the eye.
If the hordeolum does not appear at the initial incision, you should gently enlarge the incision slightly until the hordeolum protrudes.  Then, pull it out as we described.  Then, dip a cloth in vinegar and water, place it over the area, and secure it with a bandage.  Some people crush salt.
They place it in the incision to dissolve any remaining moisture. Then treat it until it heals.  If a hot swelling occurs at the site, treat it with cooling compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the methods of bandaging the eye.
If extra eyelashes grow on the eyelid, deviating from the natural course beneath the natural eyelashes and becoming stubborn, they harm the eye and cause various ailments such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, and inflammation, even causing the eye to become unusable.
The bandaging of the eye has four aspects: either by cauterization with fire, or by a sharp medicine, as mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or it may be by cutting and stitching, or by applying a reed, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, then insert a needle with thread from below the eyelid and pierce the needle with the thread from above, close to the hair itself. Pull the thread upward on the eyelid.
Turn it with a rod, then cut into the inner part of the eyelid, avoiding the excess hair, with a needle-scraper from the larger to the smaller incision.  Then, pull out the thread and place a small pad of cotton or cloth beneath the eyelid.  Then, bandage the eyelid with a dressing.
It should be like the shape of a rue leaf, but the shape should be as much as needed to raise the eyelid, since this may differ from person to person. Some may need a specific amount of the eyelid cut off, in proportion to how much the eyelid has relaxed.
Some require a smaller cut, all according to the degree of eyelid relaxation. Then, cut with the needle-scraper along the lines you have marked, beginning from the larger incision to the smaller one. The incision should be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a rod.
Then, insert the hook into one corner of the skin, then peel it all off.  Then, stitch the two lips together with a needle and thin wool thread. Wipe away the blood and attach any remaining threads to the eyebrows with adhesive substances.
You may do this, or not bother.  Then the stitches and threads remain for about three or four days, then treat it. If you prefer, you can leave the wound unstitched and treat it with something that dries and contracts it. The eyelid will lift when the wound is closed and brought together. Stitching is better.
This method of tightening, the ancients mentioned it, though it is somewhat arduous for the patient.  It is a good method, and there is no danger.  Another method of tightening is to shape the eyelid like a rue leaf, as described. Then lift the eyelid with three hooks, which may be separate or grouped in this manner:
Then cut off any excess eyelid with a small pair of scissors, in this manner, cutting evenly.
If you are unable to hold the hooks in place or if they are not properly positioned, take a needle with thread, insert it into the center of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger corner, and a third thread near the smaller corner.  Bring the threads together evenly with your fingers, then raise your hand steadily using them.
Raise the entire marked skin as described, then stitch the edges of the wound and treat it until it heals.  If a hot swelling occurs during the cutting or incision, relieve that swelling with a `qiyru`ti and similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also have its folds turned inward.  In this case, the cutting, stitching, cauterization with fire or with a sharp medicine described above should be used.  Cauterization with fire or with a sharp medicine is more effective and less harmful to the patient than cutting and stitching; in these cases, the better approach is preferred.
Cauterization with reeds is done in this way: turn the eyelid, make the incision from the inside as described, then make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to that of the eyelid, and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
Having trimmed them from both sides where the threads attach, gently bring them together on the remaining part of the eyelid, and tightly tie the reeds from both sides.  Leave it for some days, and the tightened skin will die, turn black, and decay.
Until it falls off on its own. If it takes too long, trim it with the scissors, then treat it until it heals.  When it is healed, the eyelid will rise and the lashes will not prick the eye. This is the shape and size of the two reeds, made precisely to this length and width:.
Chapter Twelve.
For removing irritating hairs from the eye with a needle.
If there is a hair or two, the procedure is to take a thin needle, thread it with a fine, smooth silk thread, tie its ends with a very gentle knot, and make the thread about a span long.
Then, thread a second, thinner thread into the tweezers, and tie its ends, if desired.  The patient should place their head on your lap, close to the light so the work is clear, for it is delicate work.  Then, with the needle attached to the tweezers, insert it into the base of the extra hair.
And, quickly, pass it through the eyelid until you bring it out beyond the normal hair.  Then pull the thread upward with the tweezers and extract the hair from the tweezers, whether one, two, or three—no more.
Then, pull with your hand until the hair comes out with the tweezers, in the eyelid, along with the natural hair.  If the tweezers are pulled and the hair does not come out with them, pull the tweezers downward with the thread you attached, until the tweezers exit through the upper hole.
Then, reinsert the hair into the tweezers and pull them out.  Repeat this until it rises through the upper hole and you see it clearly.  Then release the tweezers and thread and remove them.  Press the eye shut and leave it pressed for two or three days, until the hair is firmly in place and the flesh grows around it.
If the hair is short, either add a longer hair from the natural head, so it rises with it, or let it grow longer, and then remove it.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the treatment of the stye that occurs in the upper eyelid.
This stye, which is called a "hare-lip," can be either natural or accidental.  The accidental stye is from the inflammation of a wound, cut, burn, or similar.
The treatment involves incising the inflammation, separating its edges, and placing a flax thread between them and tying it until healed.  It is not advisable to use things that dry and constrict in treating it.
If you do that, the stye will return more severely.  Instead, use things that soften, such as fenugreek, lentil-water boiled with saffron, flaxseed, and a diachylon ointment mixed with some oils.  Apply this to the thread and use it for treatment.
The aim of the treatment is to prevent it from re-adhering in its original form.  This method we described is only a partial correction of the stye, not a restoration of the original condition.
Chapter Fourteen.
On treating the stye that forms in the lower eyelid.
This stye, which forms from below, is properly called a stye.  It is either natural or accidental; the accidental stye results from a wound, incision, cauterization, or similar causes.
The method is to take a needle with a double thread, insert it into the flesh, piercing from the inner to the outer eyelid until the thread is on both sides of the flesh.  Then, pull the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper form, then well and good. Otherwise, take a spatula, apply it to the wound, and with it, turn the eyelid inside out. Make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, positioning the edges of the incisions at the angles of the incision you made, so they meet to form an angle. So that when they come together, its shape will resemble this form:
Then, remove the flesh to the extent that the sharp side of it is below the eyelid. Then, bring together the separated parts with two stitches, sewing it with a thread of wool. Then, treat it with the relaxing and astringent medications we mentioned, until it heals.
If the wound is from a cut, stitch, or cauterization, a simple incision should be made, also devoid of eyelashes, as previously described. Then, separate the lips with astringents, as we have mentioned.
In summary, for treating a wound (shatra) whether from above or below, perform the procedure according to the presentation of the wound, as it can vary significantly in appearance. The skilled craftsman devises a solution by any means possible.
Until the shape returns to its natural form, or nearly so. The disease itself indicates the appropriate treatment and tools in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may affect many people, with the upper eyelid adhering to the white of the eye, preventing its ease of movement and hindering its normal functions.  Therefore, a slender instrument should be inserted under the eyelid.
Lift it upwards, or extend it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not as sharp as other scalpels, but rather like a scraper, so that the patient is not disturbed during the procedure, preventing the scalpel from slipping and injuring the eye with its sharpness; your incision should be as if you were peeling away the adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its natural position and the adhesion is completely healed. Then apply salt water to the eye to wash it or instill a solution of verdigris in water from a dropper, and then separate the eyelid and eye with a linen wick.
Place wet egg white on top of the eye.  After three days, use lead-based eye drops until healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
In the excision of the toenail and deep flesh growths.
The toenail is of two types: either it is nervous, resembling a tough, thin membrane, or it is non-nervous, resembling a hard, white moisture that breaks if touched by iron or attempted to be removed with forceps.
For forceps do not hold it, and both types begin in the larger area and gradually spread to cover the eye and prevent light and eye movement. The procedure for removing it is to have the patient place their head in your lap.
Then, open their eyes and raise the eyelid with your hand. Then, grasp the toenail with a slightly bent forceps and pull it upward. Then, take a needle, thread it with horse or cow hair and strong thread, slightly bend the needle tip, and insert it into the middle of the toenail.
Pierce it with the needle, tie the toenail with the thread, pull it upward, and peel the toenail side that is next to the pupil as if spreading it out with the thread. Then cut the rest at the base of the growth with a gentle scalpel or small scissors and leave the natural flesh of the growth.
So as not to cause constant tearing from the cutting, the toenail may be pulled with forceps alone or with thread, as we stated, and then the smooth scalpel is used to peel the portion that looks like this:.
Be careful not to touch the corneal membrane with the scalpel, causing a perforation in the pupil. Then, after cutting, place some powdered salt or zanjari shiyaf into the eye and leave it until the next day, then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the toenail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle or secure forceps, this type of toenail is often treated with medication, and it heals as described in the classification.  If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a gentle, smooth, blunt scalpel, like this image. Gently scrape the toenail from above with the scalpel. If you see it crumbling and breaking apart, after the scraping, put a drop of zanjari shiyaf, or red shiyaf, or powdered salt into the eye and compress the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until all of it is gone, unless a hot tumor appears in the eye.  Leave it and treat the hot tumor until it heals, then repeat the scraping until it heals.  As for the fleshy protrusion...
If the protrusion is causing excessive irritation to the eye, then grasp the protrusion with forceps and cut off a portion.  Do not cut excessively, lest it cause excessive tearing. Then apply red or zanjari shiyaf to the area until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the excision of the warty growths and excess flesh in the eye.
In some people's eyes, red, layered flesh may grow, covering the pupil or nearly reaching or overflowing onto the eyelids.  Sometimes the eyelids turn outward, resembling a rose.
The treatment is for the patient to place their head in your lap, then open their eyes, and you should use appropriate forceps to remove all the red flesh.
Or hold it with a scalpel or a needle, then cut the flesh piece by piece until it is all removed.  Protect the eye from injury during the procedure.  The excision can be done with the scalpel that we described for removing a toenail, or with a small pair of scissors. Like the forceps used to remove splinters, as their image is shown in the following chapter.
When the excision is complete and all the flesh is gone, fill the eye with finely ground salt or instill red or similar corrosive medicines. Apply a bandage of egg white to the outside of the eye to prevent a hot swelling.
If any flesh remains and bleeding is heavy, and you fear a hot swelling, leave the eye and treat it with something that alleviates the swelling. Then return to the procedure until it heals.  Do the same with any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye by nature or by appearance with surgical procedures using iron. Do not insert your hand into any of these procedures until you see that the organ is capable of withstanding the procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
In the excision of the eye.
The vessels are red veins that weave over the eye, hindering its function and weakening it over time.  Therefore, you must first examine it. If the eye containing the vessels is strong and has no other ailments besides the vessels...
Then, the excision is to instruct the patient to place their head on your lap, and to tie those vessels with a single hook or two, depending on your skill.  The hooks should be gently curved in this manner:
Or, two hooks can be double on one instrument, like this:
Then, gently excise those vessels with a fine scissors. Wipe away the blood periodically until you see the eye is free of those vessels and the bleeding has stopped.  Be careful not to injure the eye with the scissors' edges.  Work in the middle of the day, in the light of the sun.
Be very meticulous in your work so that you only cut the vessels. Once finished, apply green or red acacia powder to the eye to consume any remaining vessels. If you cannot remove all of them at once, bandage the eye to alleviate any pain.
Leave it for a few days until the pain subsides and the inflammation is gone.  Then repeat the procedure in the same manner until healed. This is the form of the scissors for this purpose.
Leave it [and let the patient's head rest on the student's lap.  Another student should wipe away tears and examine the front and back of the eye.] The best practice is to open the eye with fixed or triangular openers held by a student.
Carefully, with the patient's head resting in the student's lap, and another student wiping away tears and examining the front and back of the eye, you have the option to start the excision from the upper or lower part of the eye.  It is preferable to first remove two vessels on the upper side, one at the end of the circle and the other at the beginning.
Hold it with the middle and index fingers, then attach forceps beneath them.  Then hold it with the ring and little fingers, gently removing your hand so you see all the vessels rise.  Cut the smaller ones with scissors, or a gentle scalpel, if you wish.
Insert the probe on a needle and explore all the vessels until they rise above the conjunctiva.  Do not cut the vessels while they are still moist, because the tears and blood will make them (soften/liquefy) on you.
Then cut with scissors whatever extends beyond the circle from above and below, so that nothing remains protruding except on the cornea. If possible, lift it from the cornea with the probe; this is better, and may be possible in some people.
If you cut it, the area will be irritated for several days; the pain will subside and the hot swelling will diminish, then repeat the procedure on it.
Chapter Nineteen.
On returning the reed to the nose.
If physicians call the reed a nasiri, and if, after treating it with cautery or a sharp, burning medicine, as previously described, it does not heal, then the only recourse is to make an incision on the tumor when it is ripe, and to extract all the moisture or pus from it.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see damage or blackness in it, scrape it with an instrument of this shape:
The scraper, called the *khashna al-ras*, is made from Indian iron, with a round head like a seed.  It is engraved with fine grooves like a file or a scraper.  Place it on the damaged part of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers while slightly squeezing your hand, until you know that the damage has been removed.
Do this several times, then bind the area with drying, astringent medicines.  If the area is sealed, flesh grows there, and the discharge stops, and forty days pass without renewal, suppuration, or any incident.
Know then that it has healed, otherwise there is no remedy but to return the nasiri to the nasal opening in this manner: expose the bone again with iron or a sharp medicine.  Once the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The tip of the iron is triangular, and its shaft is a tapered cone narrowing to the tip, as you see.  Then place it on the bone itself, near the nostril, with your hand a little further from the eye.  Then rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
If he feels air coming out when he holds his hand to his nose, then bind the area with drying, astringent medicines, as I have taught you.  If flesh grows and the area hardens, the matter that was protruding outward will return to the nose, and this will be easier on the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On returning the eye protrusion.
If the whole eye protrudes, and no damage or deficiency occurs in vision, then you should ease the patient, then bind it with a bandage, then place a compress on the back of the neck without any specific preparation, and gently suction it.
Then, apply a poultice to the eye made from acacia, senna, frankincense, and henna.  Then, apply a bandage to the eye from above, using many bandages (refa'id).  The preparation of these bandages is to take many soft pieces of cloth, fold each piece into four layers, and make many of these.
Then place them one upon another as much as the eye needs, then tightly bind them with a strong cloth, and repeat the suctioning with the compress without any specific preparation.  Then remove the compress, and leave the eye bound for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during this time, then it should be tightened.  Then, remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said. If you see the eye has returned, then [stop], otherwise, repeat the bandage, the refa'id, the tightening, and the compress until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In cutting the conjunctiva.
If a rupture occurs in the conjunctiva, then it tears and protrudes outside the eyelids like a piece of conjunctiva, making the person's appearance unsightly, and you wish to cut it, then the procedure is as I describe.
Insert a needle into the base of the conjunctiva from bottom to top.  Then, insert another needle carrying a double-threaded suture from the medial side and pierce through it, leaving the first needle in place.
Then cut the bend of the double thread and tie some of the conjunctiva around the needle and tighten it. Then remove the needle and apply wool soaked in egg white to the eye and leave it until the threads fall with the conjunctiva. Then treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals. The conjunctiva may be tightened from all sides with a circular bandage until the eye is more prominent.  Then, using a thin scalpel, it is incised into the depths of the eye, allowing the whitish moisture to drain.  The eye subsides and withers in its place, then it is tightened until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of abscesses.
This ailment, called an abscess, is a gathering in the eye resembling flowing water but without it. The method of treatment is to have the patient sit upright on a chair, then take their head in your hands from both sides.
Move it until you see the mass descend below with your eyes, then stabilize it, so that it does not move. Light will then emanate, and the patient will see objects as they used to. If the mass does not descend, we know it is water. If the described method does not cause the mass to descend.
Otherwise, seat the patient before you, then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision above the cornea, near where the adhesion meets the cornea at the limbus, until the material comes out.
Then, instill warm water into the eye, mixed with honey, or water boiled with licorice and honey.  Then, continue with further treatment until recovery.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already described the types of water in the classification, and which are suitable for instillation, with detailed explanation.  Obtain this knowledge from there.  Then, the patient should be seated before you, squatting facing the light, near the sun.
Tie and firmly hold the healthy eye, then raise the eyelid of the affected eye with your left hand if it is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then, place the tip of the cup near the limbus, with the thickness of a date seed, on the white part of the eye near the smaller eyelid, and push the cup firmly, rotating it with your hand, until it penetrates the white of the eye, and you feel that the cup has reached an empty space.
The depth to which the cup should penetrate should be the distance from the boy to the edge of the black part (i.e., the limbus of the eye). The copper can then be seen by the observer in the same way as the eye, due to the clarity of the cornea.  Then, move the cup upward to the place where the water is.
Then press it downwards repeatedly. If the water descends immediately, the patient will see what his sight has opened to him immediately, the cup in his eye, then remains still for a while. If the water rises, lower it again without removing the cup.
If it settles and does not rise, remove the cup gently, twisting your hand slightly, then dissolve some pure, fine rock salt in the water and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then, apply moist cotton or lint to the entire exterior of the eye.
With rose oil and egg white, bandage the healthy eye.  As for us and the people of our time, we use ground cumin mixed with egg white. If the cup will not enter the eye because of its hardness (for some people have extremely hard eyes). One should take the lancet, called the *bريد*, which is depicted as follows:
Pierce only the adhered tissue with it [the lancet].  Do not make the hole deep, but rather lightly touch the cup in a gentle spot. Then insert the cup as previously described.  Once you have finished, prepare a well-secured bed for the patient in a dark room, having them lie on their back.
He must refrain from all movement and coughing.  His food should be soft, and he must not move his head left or right at all.  Keep the bandage in place for three days.  Then, in the same dark room, remove the bandage and examine his eyesight.
Show him objects, then reapply the bandage for seven days.  You should not do this during the treatment or after the cupping, but rather avoid it before the water ascends quickly from the intense gaze.
If a hot swelling appears, then the eye should be treated before the seventh day, and the swelling should be soothed until it subsides.  Then, release the sight and place a veil over his face to train his eyesight from beneath it for several days in that dark room. Then...
He is then gradually taken out of the room and allowed to perform his duties.  Know that the learner cannot dispense with observing the cupping process several times before undertaking the procedure.  It has reached my ears that some Iraqis say they make a hollowed-out cup in Iraq that absorbs water.
I have not seen anyone in our country do that, nor have I read of it in any of the books of the ancients.  It may be a recent innovation.  Here is an illustration of the different types of cupping instruments for him to become familiar with them.
It is made of copper, specifically, and its tip is precisely that thin, triangular, and sharp-pointed.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
Various superfluous flesh growths can occur in the nose, some resembling scorpions with numerous legs, others cancerous, hard, and dark colored, and still others soft and not dark colored.
For those growths that are soft, not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun, open their nostril, and insert the hook into the flesh.  Then pull it out, and cut what was caught with a gentle, sharp scalpel, on one side only.
Until you know that all the flesh is gone. If any remains that you cannot cut, then scrape it off gently with a suitable instrument, so that nothing is left.  If bleeding or a hot tumor appears.
If it is malignant or cancerous, treat it quickly, cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone.  Then put into the nose, after cutting, vinegar and water, or a drink, if the nose opens and moisture flows down the throat.
Then know that it is healed. If the moisture does not drain properly, know that there is growing flesh within the spongy bones, which the cutting instrument did not reach.  In that case, you should take a thick linen thread.
Tie many knots in it, and make the space between each knot equal to or less than a finger.  The patient should use a rod or whatever is available to insert the end of the thread into their nostril, making it like a button, and pull on it until it reaches the septum.
It will come out through his throat.  Children often do similar things in school.  It is easy for whoever wants it.  Then tie together the two ends of the thread, one that came out the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to scrape the flesh, doing this until you know the flesh has been cut by the thread knots. Then take out the thread, and place it in the nose, after wiping away the blood, as a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment.  Do this for three days, or more.
Until the ointment has consumed all remaining flesh.  Then, as the last thing, place a lead tube in the nostril for a number of days until healed.  If further drying treatment is needed, use that.
This is a picture of the syringe used to instill ointments and medicines into the nose.
A small, open, ointment-applying lamp, made of silver or copper, is placed.
If you wish, make the tube closed, like a reed, and the applicator's ointment-applying surface flat.
It has a handle at the end, as you see, to grasp when warming the ointment or whatever liquid substances or materials you desire in it.
Chapter Twenty-five.
Concerning warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow on the tip of the nose, increasing in size and number over time, until their appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, one should cut it off at its first appearance, removing the entire wart.  Then apply either cautery or a burning medicine that acts as a cautery to the affected area. If one delays cutting it until it grows large, then examine it. If it is hard, stony, dark-colored, and insensitive, ...
Do not use iron on it, for it is a cancerous tumor.  I have often seen that cutting this tumor has resulted in a severe affliction for the patient. If the tumor is soft to the touch and not dark-colored, and you see that it can be completely excised, Excise the tumor completely without hesitation or delay, and treat the area with something that dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear. If its connection is severed by a wound or similar injury, know that if a separation occurs in any of these cartilages, it rarely succeeds, except in some individuals.
Therefore, if such a thing happens to someone, examine if the wound is fresh with blood. If so, bring together the edges of the wound by suturing and then treat it until it heals. If the separation has separated its edges and each gap is intact, it is necessary to strip each gap of its visible skin thickness until it bleeds.
Then, bring the edges together with sutures, tighten them, and dust them with pulverized cinnamon and frankincense. Apply a plaster of palm ointment or other adhesive ointments on top of the powder. Leave it tightened for two or three days, then remove it.
Then change the medication and leave it until the threads dissolve on their own, then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to bring together the separated parts, either with needles as we described in abdominal suturing, or with thread, as I explained earlier.
Chapter Twenty-seven.
On the removal of knots that appear in the lips.
Many people may develop small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling carbuncles, others smaller or larger.  Therefore, you should turn the lip over and cut open each knot, and remove it with a forceps.
Cut it away from all sides, then fill the wound with powdered alum to stop the bleeding. Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the affected areas with astringents until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
On cutting excess flesh in the gums.
Excess flesh often grows on the gums, which the ancients called *apolus*.  It should be grasped with forceps or a scalpel, cut at its base, and the bleeding allowed to cease. Then, powdered alum should be applied to the area.
Or one of the astringent, dried roots. If that flesh returns after treatment, it often does. Cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-nine.
On scraping teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly scales may accumulate on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and out, and between the gums.  These may turn black, yellow, or green, causing decay to reach the gums and disfigure the teeth.
The patient should be seated before you, head resting on your lap.  You should scrape the tooth or teeth showing scales and sand-like matter until nothing of it remains.  Likewise, do this with any black, green, or yellow discoloration, until they are clean.
If the scraping removes all the matter on the first attempt, otherwise, repeat the scraping the next day, and again on the third, until you achieve your goal.  Know that teeth require scraping implements of various shapes and forms, according to what is suitable for your work.
Because the scraper used to scrape the tooth from the inside is different from the scraper used to scrape from the outside, and the scraper used between the teeth is of a different form.  These are different shapes of scrapers, all to be prepared beforehand.
Chapter Thirty.
On the extraction of teeth.
One should treat the tooth's pain by every means possible and delay tooth extraction, for there is no remedy after extraction, as it is a noble essence.  Only when extraction is unavoidable should it be done.
Therefore, if the patient is determined on extraction, one must make sure the pain of the tooth is resolved. Often, the patient is misled by the pain, thinking it is in a healthy tooth and extracting it; then, the pain does not subside until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have seen this repeated in the actions of blood-letters.
If the tooth's specific pain is resolved, then one should make an incision around the tooth with a strong scalpel, separating the gum on all sides, and then move it gently, bit by bit, with your fingers or fine forceps, until it is loosened.
Then, firmly grasp the tooth with large forceps, holding the patient's head between your knees, ensuring the tooth does not move.  Pull the tooth straight to prevent breaking it. If it does not come out, take one of those instruments, gently insert it beneath the tooth from all sides, and repeat the movement as done previously.
If the tooth is hollow or decayed, it is necessary to fill the cavity with a cloth and seal it tightly with a thin, smooth material to prevent it from shattering while you pull on it with forceps.  One should also carefully cut around the gum on all sides.
Use care to avoid breaking the tooth, as a piece remaining would cause the patient greater suffering than the initial pain.  Do not act like the ignorant blood-letters who attempt extraction without the methods we have described.
Often, they inflict great harm on people, the least of which is breaking the tooth and leaving the roots, either entirely or partially. Alternatively, the extraction may pull out part of the jawbone, as we have observed repeatedly.  After the extraction, the patient should rinse with liquid, vinegar, or salt water.  If bleeding occurs at the site, which often happens, then crush some alum and apply it to the area.
Otherwise, if alum does not work, use the delicate forceps, which first move the tooth.  These should have long handles, short grips, and thick construction to avoid bending when you grip the tooth with them.  Here is the image of the larger forceps.
Its grips should be thick, so that when you grasp them, they will not yield or bend.  The handles should be short.  They should be made of Indian steel or tempered, strong, well-forged steel.  At the tips, there should be teeth that fit into each other, creating a tight and firm grip.
The tips can also be shaped like a file, making for a strong grip.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On extracting the roots of teeth and removing fractured jawbones.
If a root of the tooth remains broken after extraction, you should apply cotton soaked in oil for a day or two to allow the area to relax, then use a forceps or pliers. Which resembles the beak of the bird called a 'blerja' and this is a picture of the pliers.
If it is not possible to remove it with these pliers, it should be fashioned like a rasp from the inside or like a scraper.  Then, one should make a hole in the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, and then insert the instrument, which is like a small lever, whose image is this.
Short, somewhat thick at the end, and not too sharp so as not to break. If the root is removed by this, then fine; otherwise use these other instruments, whose image is this: The first is triangular at the end, with some thickness.
Another delicate triangular image.
We may also use this instrument with two branches, whose image is also this.
And with other instruments and tools mentioned in the inventory of teeth, and we may also use this instrument which resembles a large fishhook, whose image is this.
Triangular, bent at the end, with some slight thickness to prevent breakage, and not sharp.  Know that instruments for teeth are numerous, as are all other instruments—scarcely countable.  A skillful craftsman, through his craft, may invent instruments according to the indications of the work and the diseases themselves.
Because there are diseases for which the ancients did not describe instruments due to the variety of types.  If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth breaks or becomes decayed, search for it in its place with what is suitable from one of these instruments and pliers mentioned in the extraction of the roots.
And we use a forceps, its image is this.
It should have some slight thickness to grip the bone firmly so it doesn't slip out until it is extracted.  The area is treated with appropriate medications. If the bone is slightly decayed, remove the decay and discoloration until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
The Thirty-second Chapter.
In the extraction of erupting teeth.
Teeth that erupt out of their natural path disfigure the appearance, especially in women and slaves.  Careful consideration should be given; if a tooth has erupted behind another tooth,
If it cannot be extracted or repositioned, remove it. If it is attached to another tooth, cut it off using this instrument, whose image is shown here:.
It resembles a small beak, made of sharp, Indian steel.  Cut it in several sessions due to the tooth's hardness, and to avoid disturbing other teeth. If it is protruding and firmly positioned, use an Indian-made file to smooth it down, the image of which is given here.
It should be entirely Indian steel, with very fine carvings, similar to the file used to make needles.  Carefully smooth the tooth down a little at a time over many days, to prevent it from loosening and falling out.  Then smooth it finally and clean it with some scrapers.
If a tooth has broken, causing the tongue discomfort during speech, it should also be smoothed until the roughness of the break is gone, and it is level and smooth, no longer causing discomfort to the tongue or impairing speech.
Chapter Thirty-three.
In fastening loose teeth with silver or gold thread.
If older teeth become loose and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot eat anything for fear of them falling out, and treatment with astringent medicines has been ineffective.
The solution is to tie them with gold or silver thread. Gold is better, because silver corrodes and rots after a few days, while gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium thickness and thinness, fitting comfortably between the teeth.
The method of fastening is to take the thread and pass its bend between the two sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, one or more, until reaching a sound tooth on the other side.
Then weave back to the starting side, gently and skillfully tightening, so the teeth don't move.  Tighten the thread at the tooth roots to prevent it from loosening.  Then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them, twist them together, and tuck them away between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
So the tongue is not harmed.  Then leave it tightened as long as it remains. If it loosens or breaks, tighten it with another thread, and it will remain in this way for a lifetime. This is the image of the teeth and the manner of fastening between two sound teeth and two loose teeth as you see.
A single tooth, or two, may be repositioned and fastened as described, and remain. However, this is done by a skilled craftsman. They might carve a piece of cow bone into the shape of a tooth and place it where the missing tooth was. And it is tightened as we said, and it remains in place.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On severing the ligament that lies under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament under the tongue may be either natural, present from birth, or accidental, resulting from a healed wound.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth, support their head in your lap, and lift their tongue.  Then, sever the nerve ligament transversely, allowing the tongue to be released. If there is some stiffness or entanglement, and it's from a healed wound, use a probe and slit it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knots are loosened.
Be careful not to cut too deep into the flesh, thereby severing an artery and causing bleeding. Then, after the incision, rinse the area with rosewater or vinegar and cold water.  Place a linen wick under the tongue for the patient to hold each night.
To prevent re-adhesion.  If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the area.  If the bleeding is excessive, cauterize the area with a suitable lens cautery. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until healed.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
Removing the frog produced under the tongue.
A tumor resembling a small frog may develop under the tongue, hindering its natural function, and possibly enlarging to fill the mouth.  The procedure is to open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the tumor.
If you see it dark-colored or black and hard, with no sensation for the patient, do not attempt to treat it, for it is cancer. If it leans toward whiteness and has moisture, use forceps to open it and carefully dissect it from all sides.
If blood overwhelms you during your work, apply powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then continue with your work until you extract it completely.  Then have the patient gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with any other appropriate treatment until healed.
Chapter Thirty-six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And any tumors growing in the throat may present glands resembling those appearing externally, called tonsils.  If you treat them as described in the classification and they do not heal...
If the tumor is hard, dark-colored, and insensitive, do not use a scalpel.  If it is red and thick-rooted, likewise do not use a scalpel, for fear of bleeding.  Instead, leave it until it ripens, either by compression or spontaneous rupture.
If it is white, round, and thin-rooted, this is the one that should be cut. Before operating, observe if the intense inflammation has completely subsided or if some of it has abated.
Then, have the patient sit facing the sun, head in your lap, mouth open.  Take an assistant, and using an instrument, press the tongue downwards.  This is its shape:
Make it of silver or copper, thin like a knife.  When you press the tongue with it, and the tumor is visible, and you see it, take a hook and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it outward as much as possible without pulling any of the membranes along with it.
Then, cut it with an instrument, similar to scissors but with the ends bent, each sharp edge facing the other.  It is made of Indian steel or Damascus steel.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel.  This is its image.
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other.  After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner.  Then, after cutting, have the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or rue leaves, or similar astringents, have been boiled until the bleeding stops.  Then treat until healed.  Other tumors may grow in the throat, besides the tonsils, and these are cut in the same way as the tonsils.
I treated a woman who had a tumor growing inside her throat, approaching the uvula, almost blocking the throat.  The woman was breathing through a narrow passage. Eating and drinking water had become difficult.
She was close to death if she had lived another day or two.  The tumor had grown two branches extending to her nostrils.  I acted quickly, inserting a hook into one of them, and pulling it out, removing a suitable piece.
Then I cut it where it emerged from the nostril, and then did the same with the other nostril.  Then I opened her mouth and pressed down her tongue. Then, I inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut off a portion of it.  Only a small amount of blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately drank water and ate.  We repeatedly cut away from that tumor over a long period, and the tumor grew back what we cut away, until the task took up a long time for both of us.
I devised a method and cauterized the tumor inside the throat, which stopped its growth.  Then I left the area. I do not know what happened to her after I left.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
On cutting a tumor of the throat.
If a tumor descends to the throat and swells, and is white and oblong, it is called a column. If it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called an enbul. If treatment as described in the classification does not succeed.
If the hot tumor has subsided and it is thin, then it should be cut.  If it is a solid, round mass, without length, and is blood red, or dark-colored, or black, or lacks sensation, then it should be avoided.
For there is danger to the patient.  Therefore, if you see it with the characteristics mentioned, its whiteness and length, the patient should sit facing the sun, and the tongue should be held down with the instrument previously described, then the probe should be inserted into the enbul.
Pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments mentioned in the section on tonsil removal. Only the portion exceeding the natural size should be cut;  if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after the cutting, use the methods described for tonsil removal and treat it until it heals. If the patient refuses the cutting, then apply cautery without fear or hesitation.  The application of cautery is with a sharp medicine, placing the patient's head in your lap.
Then, hold down the tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take the sharp water mentioned in the chapter on cautery, mix it with unslaked lime, making it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of this instrument. This is its form:
The end of the instrument where the medicine is placed has a concavity like a tamarind spoon. Place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, while the patient lies on their side, so that saliva flows from the mouth, from the inside of the throat, so that nothing goes down into the throat and harms him.
Then, hold the instrument with the medicine and press it onto the uvula for about half an hour until you see it turn black and the burning from the medicine subsides. If you wish, take a cotton ball, wrap it around the end of a tamarind spoon, and soak the cotton ball in the medicine.
Insert the tamarind spoon with the cotton into the tube from above so that the cotton adheres to the uvula. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired level of cautery of the uvula. Then leave it; it will wither and fall off after three or four days.  If you need to reapply the medicine, do so.
After the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with a cotton ball soaked in ghee, removing any remaining medicine. Then, the patient should rinse with cold water and treat the area externally with compresses and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and avoids fear.
The uvula can also be treated with gentler methods than cauterization and cutting, using incense in this manner:  Take frankincense, saffron, sage, wormwood, dittany, chamomile, and rue, or similar herbs. Gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
And boil it, with the pot covered with clay, and having a hole in the middle of the cover, into which the hollow instrument is fitted, crafted from silver or copper.
Insert the end of the instrument with the pomegranate into the patient's mouth until the steam rises to the uvula through the tube.  The uvula will contract. Then repeat this several times until it withers.  Beware, do not apply this treatment at the very beginning of the swelling.
For it often aggravates the swelling.  Instead, it should be done when the hot swelling subsides.  If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit a hard-boiled egg shell onto its end so the patient's mouth is not burned.
Because the egg shell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth. This is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On removing thorns and other foreign objects lodged in the throat.
Thorns, fish bones, or other objects frequently lodge in the throat.  One should remove any visible object that can be seen, after pressing down the tongue with the instrument, in the sun's light.
To make clear what is in the throat, and if something is hidden and not visible, the patient should vomit before the food is digested in their stomach.  Perhaps the lodged object will come out with the vomit, or the patient will swallow a piece of lint or a stalk of lettuce.
Or a piece of dry bread might be swallowed, or a soft piece of sea sponge is tied to a thread and swallowed.  When it reaches the thorn, quickly pull the thread repeatedly.
Often, the thorn or bone adheres to it and comes out.  If it does not come out by the methods mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this manner:
It should be slightly thicker than a lead rod.  At its tip, there is a bend.  The patient, gently, inserts it into their throat, with their head raised.  They must be careful not to touch their throat lest it cause coughing and dislodge the bone or thorn.  Or the physician inserts it with their hand.
The patient inserting it is better because they know the location of the lodged object.  They push it downwards or pull their hand with the instrument upwards, all in a manner suited to removing the object.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
On removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If the leech, treated as described in the section on medicinal treatment, proves ineffective, then look into the patient's throat in the sunlight after you have pressed their tongue down with the instrument I described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a gentle, firm forceps.  If you cannot do that, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech. Then, insert a heated iron into the tube's interior.
Do this several times. The patient should abstain from drinking water all day. Then, they should take a jar filled with cool water, open their mouth over it, rinse their mouth with it, but not swallow a drop.  Move the water with their hand from time to time.
The leech will fall off when it feels the water. If it does not come out using the methods described, fumigate the throat with urine and with incense using the instrument described for treating the nose. Do this several times, and it will fall off.
The method for using incense is to take a container with charcoal heated by fire. The container is covered with a lid having a hole in the center.  Place the end of the instrument in the hole.  Then, place the incense in the container.  The patient should place their mouth over the end of the tube and close their mouth to prevent the incense from escaping until it reaches their throat.
The leech will fall off at that point. If it does not, the incense is repeated several times. The patient should endure thirst and eat salty food and garlic, but not drink water.  It is essential that the leech be removed by this method. This is the image of the instrument used to draw the leech out of the throat when it is seen.
It resembles pincers, as you see, except that it has this curvature that enters the throat. Its tips resemble the beak of a bird.  It has the roughness of a file; if it grasps something, it will not let go.
Another instrument is used to press the tongue.  It is a wide ring of iron, sized to fit the mouth above the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth, standing on its side, keeping the mouth open against the patient's will. The patient cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument used to open the mouth in this exact manner.
Chapter Forty.
It deals with the incision and lancing of tumors.
Tumors are diverse and varied, as will be described one by one in this book.  They differ in their incision and lancing in two ways: first, from the type of tumor itself and the humors it contains.
The second type is by the locations in the body where they occur, since a tumor arising in the head is different from one arising in the buttocks, and a tumor in fleshy tissue differs from one in a joint. Each requires a different approach.
Some tumors should not be incised until the pus within has matured and become complete. Others should be incised while still unripe, such as tumors close to the joints.
For if a tumor near a joint grows until it infects the surrounding tissues, it might damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing the disability of the limb, or the tumor might be near a vital organ.
Because if you delay incision until it matures, you harm the vital organ, or if it is near the buttocks, you should incise it while still unripe.  It is necessary to incise an unripe tumor near the buttocks, lest the infection spread and reach the inside of the buttock, resulting in a fistula.
And it will become incurable to some degree.  You should know when to incise fully matured tumors, when the tumor's pain subsides, the fever goes away, the redness and throbbing diminish, the head of the tumor softens, and other symptoms abate.
The incision should be made at the lowest part of the tumor, if possible, to facilitate drainage downwards, or at the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  The incision should be made lengthwise if the tumors are in the arms or legs.
The incision should be made in the areas of the muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and generally in all flat areas without folds.  As for areas that bend, the incision should follow the contours of the area.  If the tumor is in fleshy areas, it is better to leave it alone.
Until its maturation is complete, as we have said.  If you incise it before then, the pus will drain excessively, which is harmful and messy.  The edges and depths of the wound may become hardened.  Some tumors may need an incision across the body, if necessary, or in accordance with the needs of the organ.
Small tumors should be incised once, while large tumors should be incised with a wide incision or multiple incisions, depending on the tumor's size.  Some tumors may require a scraping of the skin and a cutting, if they have become like a patch of cloth.
Some have become somewhat dead, as occurs in many boils and abscesses. Some are incised in a three-sided shape, while others are cut like a rue leaf shape, like a scrotal tumor.
Some use a circular incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar cuts.  For tumors without a head, like flat, smooth tumors, a simple incision is sufficient.  If the tumor is large and has accumulated for a long time, and you have incised it, do not rush to drain all the pus at once.
Instead, drain some of it, then compress the tumor for another day, and then drain some more pus.  Repeat this gradually until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, a pregnant woman, a young child, or an elderly person, because the animal spirit often weakens with the sudden expulsion of pus.  The patient might die without your noticing it.  Be extremely cautious in this regard.
After incising these tumors, you should clean the wound and examine it.  If the tumor wound is small or the incision is simple, use linen or worn cotton. If the tumor is large and the incisions are numerous,
Then, you should insert a wick into each incision, so that some reach each other. If part of the skin has been cut or torn from the tumor, you should fill it with worn cotton or linen strips, without moisture, and keep it compressed for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.  If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after which you should soak a linen cloth in them and apply it to the bleeding area repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the methods and remedies and cauterization that we have described in many places in this book and in the sections. If it is winter and the tumor area is highly vascular, you should moisten the dressings with warm herbs and oil.
And place them on the area. If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply dressings moistened with water and oil, or with herbs and oil, all cold.  Until, as we said, the third day, you should then dissolve the tumor and clean it.
Use similar treatments until it heals.  This is what you need to know about treating tumors in general.  Regarding detailed methods, each tumor has been described with its specific treatment, summarized and categorized.
Chapter Forty-one.
On the treatment of tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, which are a type of swelling containing sacs.  They are like the sacs of a chicken egg.  There are many types, some fatty, some containing a moisture like mud.
Some contain a moisture like pus and soup, and similar things. Some are hardened and solid.  All can be lanced and removed without danger, unless a blood vessel is encountered during the incision.
And the procedure for lancing them is to first probe them with the instrument, whose image will follow, called the *midas*, so you know what they contain.  If they contain moisture, lance them lengthwise with a simple incision, in this manner:
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~c~. If the moisture has been drained, strip away the sac containing the moisture and cut it all away completely, leaving nothing at all.  Often it will return if anything is left.
Then dip a cotton swab in the astringent ointment, if available, otherwise in salt water, and fill the wound with it.  Leave it until the next day, as it will consume any remaining sac. Then apply the cotton swab with the astringent again, and a third time, if necessary.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until healed. If the swelling contains fatty material, lance it in this manner:
Make a lancing incision, and place the forceps on the edges of the wound.  Strip it from every side, and exert all your effort in removing the membrane that contains it. If an artery obstructs you, do as was previously described for you. And do the same for a swelling if it is stony hard from the incision.
The treatment is exactly as mentioned.  Incision on a hardened swelling is easier, because it has less blood and moisture.  Some of these swellings in the head in some people contain no moisture at all. This is because I incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman, and found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone, that could not be broken, even if someone threw it, it would only be chipped.  And it was different from other swellings occurring in the head, like the swellings that occur in the heads of children.
At the roots of the ears, make a simple incision along the entire length.  Shape the edges from below, to facilitate the flow of material downwards.  Then treat it with appropriate remedies.
Chapter Forty-Two.
Concerning the incision on swine that frequently occur on the neck.  These swellings often appear on the neck, under the armpits, and in the groin.  They may also appear elsewhere on the body.  A single one may appear on the neck, or many, and some arise from others.
Each such swelling is contained within its own membrane, as is the case with tumors and swellings of the head, as we have described.  These swellings come in various types: some are stony, others contain moisture, and some are malignant and unresponsive to treatment.
Of those I have observed, those that felt good and whose surface resembled the skin color, were movable in every direction, and were not attached to the neck nerves, vessels, or arteries, nor were they deep-seated. Then it should be incised simply from top to bottom of the body, in this manner, from line ~J~ to line ~B~.
And peel it from every side, stretching the edges of the skin with a hook or two, or several hooks if needed, as we have said regarding swellings of the head, removing it little by little.  Care should be taken not to cut a vein or nerve.  The scalpel should not be overly sharp.
So that your hand does not over-cut, or the patient become disturbed, and you cut what need not be cut. If you cut a vein or artery and it impedes your work, then place powdered alum or some of the powders that stop the bleeding into the wound.
Close the wound and leave it until the severity of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to fester.  The bleeding will then stop.  Return to your work until it is finished.  Then examine with your index finger if any remaining small pieces or slivers remain.  Cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the base of the sliver, you should not cut the sliver at its base. Instead, you should tie it with a strong doubled thread and leave it until it falls out on its own without harm. Then fill the wound with used cotton, having dipped it in Egyptian ointment, and then treat it.
And if you cut the entire sliver, then you should bring the edges of the wound together and stitch it immediately after verifying that no remnants remain. If you see that a remnant from the sliver of bone remains, then...
