Then you should aim for the root and base of the sliver and use the stitching and what we mentioned.  For slivers that contain moisture, apply a simple poultice where you see the place of its ripening, and make the poultice from what is below, as we said.
Then, after the poultice, use a bandage with Egyptian ointment, or similar, to consume any remaining corruption.  Once you see the wound is clean, treat it with ointments that promote the growth of flesh until healed.
Chapter Forty-three.
On the incision of the windpipe for a tumor occurring within the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the windpipe, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it.  This accords with their statements.  However, those suffering from quinsy should avoid incisions in the windpipe, as they will not benefit from it.
In order that all the tumors and lungs may be diseased, and for those with a burning tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no affliction in the trachea, the incision of the windpipe must be used to escape the harm of suffocation.
Therefore, if we wish to do this, we must make an incision in the windpipe below three rings of the trachea or four small transverse incisions between two rings, as much as the incision is in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This location is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the practitioner is timid, he should stretch the throat skin with a hook, then cut the skin until, when he reaches the trachea, he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he incises the peritoneum we have described, and he determines the incision of the trachea from the phlegm that comes out of it along with what is punctured and from the cessation of voice.  He leaves the wound open for a time.  When the time feared for suffocation has passed, he brings the edges of the wound together from the skin.
And he cuts it alone, separate from the cartilage, then the medicines that grow flesh are used until it heals. The author of this book says, "The meaning of this discourse is that, if they see the patient's throat obstructed by one of these tumors mentioned.
And when the patient was near death, and he intended to die, they went to incising the windpipe so that the patient could breathe through the wound site and be saved from death.  But they were instructed to leave the wound open until the course of the disease is completed, and its duration is usually three days or so.
At that time, they ordered the wound to be sewn up and treated until it healed.  What I saw with my own eyes was a servant who took a knife and slashed her throat, cutting a part of her lung.  I was called to treat her, and I found her weakening as a slaughtered animal weakens, and I uncovered the wound.
I found little blood coming from the wound, so I was certain that she had not severed an artery or vein.  Air was coming from the wound.  I immediately sutured the wound and treated it until she healed.  The servant experienced nothing except a slight hoarseness in the voice; nothing more.
And after a few days, she returned to her best condition.  From this, we say that incising the windpipe carries no danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On incision of the tumor appearing on the larynx from the outside, called the laryngeal tumor.
This tumor, called the laryngeal tumor, is a large tumor the color of the body.  It is common in women and comes in two types.
Either it is natural or accidental.  The natural one is incurable.  The accidental one is of two types; one is similar to fatty tumors, and the other is similar to the tumor that arises from the entanglement of an artery.  There is danger in incising it. Therefore, one should not use a knife on it at all unless it is small.  If you probe and examine it with a blunt instrument and find that it resembles a fatty tumor and is not connected to any blood vessels, then cut it as you would a fatty tumor. Remove it with its contained sac, if it is in a sac.  Otherwise, remove it entirely.  Then treat the area with appropriate treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
On incision of the various tumors.
Tumors are of many types, and all their types have been mentioned in the classification.  It is necessary here to explain the difference between a tumor and an abscess, since it is problematic.  I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and frightening pains until the boiling of the matter subsides. When the putrefaction is complete, the fever and intensity subside.  A tumor does not have heat, fever, or pain. It is contained within a serous sac, a special covering, and is the same color as the body.  Its beginning is like a lentil, and it becomes like a fig, larger or smaller.
Tumors are of two types: either fatty or containing moisture.  The colors of the moisture are numerous, as mentioned in the classification.  Therefore, when you approach the treatment of a tumor, you must first probe and examine it with the instrument called the "medess," as its image will appear in the chapter after this one.
Estimate the size of the tumor, then withdraw the probe and observe what emerges afterward. If flowing moisture of any color comes out, incise it with a small incision as described in other tumors. If no moisture emerges after the probe, know that it is fatty.
Incise it with a cross-shaped incision, as I have instructed you.  Suspend it with forceps and carefully separate the skin from all sides.  If possible, remove the sac intact along with the tumor.
If the sac bursts during the procedure and you cannot remove it intact, as often happens, remove it piece by piece until nothing remains. If any part of it remains, large or small, the tumor will likely return.
If you are unable to remove all of it and a small portion remains, cauterize the wound with sharp cauterizing materials when finished.  Suture the wound and apply a cooling agent over the inflamed tumor, and treat it with other standard treatments until healed. If the tumor is large, suture its edges and treat with methods to promote healing.
If a bleeding or non-bleeding vessel obstructs you, immediately apply pulverized alum to the affected area, and leave it tight for two or three days until the wound becomes infected and the bleeding subsides.  Then proceed to remove any remaining tumor.

Chapter forty-seven.
In the treatment of the male breast.
The breast of some people may swell at the age of puberty, becoming like a woman's breast, remaining swollen and unsightly.  If one dislikes this, then one should cut into the breast with a crescent-shaped incision, like this.
From line ~b~ to line ~c~. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with the medicated ointment, then stitch the edges of the wound together and treat it until healed.  If the breast droops and relaxes due to its bone, as happens in women.
Then, incisions should be made in its upper sides, shaped like crescents, each touching the other at their ends, so that the larger incision encircles the smaller one, like this. From line ~b~ to line ~c~, then the skin between the incisions is peeled back, the fat is removed, and the mentioned sutures and dressings, and whatever treatment is needed, are used until healed.
If you do not remove all that needs to be removed due to the patient's distress or bleeding, then the wound should be packed with cotton soaked in caustic ointment, and left until it consumes the remaining fat, then treated until healed.
The forty-eighth chapter.
On the treatment of tumors appearing under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the pig-like, hard, encapsulated variety, some containing moisture. Those containing moisture should be incised with a crescent-shaped incision, like this.
From line ~B~ to line ~C~. If all the fluid in the tumor is expelled, pack it with used cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with the appropriate ointment until healed. If the tumor is of the pig-like cyst type, incise it as described in the section on pig-like cysts. If the wound continues to form pus, apply cauterization as mentioned previously.
Chapter Forty-nine.
On incising a tumor that arises from an artery or vein, called an aneurysm.
If the artery is wounded and the skin above it is adhered, a tumor often results. Similarly, a vein can also develop swelling and a tumor.
The signs by which one knows if the tumor and swelling are from an artery or vein: if from an artery, the tumor is oblong, compacted deep within the body, and if you push on the tumor with your finger, you feel it as though it has a crackling sound.
A tumor arising from a vein is round, on the surface of the body.  Incision of these tumors is dangerous, especially in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many other locations on the body, particularly when large.  One should avoid treating them with a scalpel.
Also, tumors on the extremities or head should be avoided.  For those from arterial dilation, make a longitudinal incision in the skin, then widen the incision with forceps.  Then, dissect and free the artery from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle underneath and pass it through to the other side.  Tie the artery with a doubled thread at two places, as I described for the arteries in the temples.  Then, pierce with a lancet the place between the ligaments, so that all the blood flows out.
The tumor will then subside, and you should then use treatments that produce pus until the ligaments fall off.  Afterwards, treat it with appropriate ointments until healed.
If the tumor arises from a ruptured vein, you should hold as much of the tumor with the skin as possible.  Then, insert a needle below the area you are holding with your hand, and pass a doubled thread through it until you bring it out from the other side. Then, tie the tumor firmly, in the same manner as I described for tying the gall bladder on both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear the threads will slip, insert another needle with another thread beneath the entire tumor, at the point where the first needle crosses.
Tie your threads in four places, then slit the tumor in the middle, until what is inside comes out. Cut off the excess skin, and leave what was tied. Then place a bandage soaked in a mixture of liquid and oil on it. Then use treatments involving twisting and ointments until healed.
Chapter Fifty.
Concerning the tumor resulting from nerve twisting.
Just as a tumor may appear in an artery or vein, so too may a tumor appear in a nerve if struck or caused by excessive fatigue or the like. This is most common in the wrists and ankles.
And in every joint where movement occurs, it is a hard tumor, similar in color to the rest of the body, often without pain.  If pressed firmly, the patient feels a sensation similar to numbness.  The tumor is not concentrated deep within the body.
Rather, it lies beneath the skin and moves in every direction, but does not go forward or backward.  Those in the joints should not be treated with iron, as this may cause a worsening condition.  And those in the head or forehead...
Cut the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from the root. If it is large, suspend it with a hook, dissect it, then remove it. Close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On cutting warts appearing on the abdomen.
Warts, called "fungal" due to their resemblance to fungi, frequently appear on the abdomens and other parts of the body of some people.  Their base is thin, and their head is thick, sometimes with a thickened edge.  They come in small and large sizes.
I once observed a man with two warts on his abdomen, resembling fungi in appearance.  Their thin, whitish base had thickened and cracked; moisture constantly dripped from them.  I cut them away; one weighed approximately eighteen ounces and the other approximately six ounces.
When cutting them, if the affliction is moist and the wart's color is white, moist, and thin-based, cut it with a broad scalpel.  Have heated cauterizing instruments at hand, as considerable bleeding often results from cutting them.
If bleeding overwhelms you, cauterize it.  If the affected area is sensitive and recoils from the cutting instrument, take a tightly wound lead thread and bind the wart of this description. Leave it for two days, then progressively tighten the lead thread.
As the lead thread tightens on the wart, it will eventually detach and fall away without further intervention.  If the wart has a thick base, cutting it involves some risk, particularly if it is on the abdomen. However, it may be possible to cut off half or part of it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat the wound until it heals.  Beware of cutting a wart that is dark-colored, with little sensitivity and a dull appearance, as this is a cancerous tumor.  We will discuss cancer after this.
Chapter Fifty-two.
On treating umbilical protrusions.
Umbilical protrusions arise from various causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum on the abdomen, causing the intestines or viscera to protrude, as occurs in other hernias; or from blood emanating from a vein or artery, as previously discussed; or from accumulated gas within it.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and the protrusion of the viscera, the tumor's color will be similar to the body's color, it will be soft without pain, and will appear with varying positions. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestines, its position will be even more variable than what we described; when you press it with your fingers, it will disappear and then return, and there may be rumbling associated with it. It will greatly enlarge during bathing and intense exertion. If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
If, when pressed by your hand, it does not disappear, nor increase or decrease in size, then if it is caused by blood, the tumor will exhibit these signs, yet appear bluish-black.  If it is due to a growth of flesh, the swelling will be hard, firm, and remain at the same size.
If it is caused by gas, its touch will be soft.  The treatment in this case, if the umbilical protrusion is due to arterial or venous blood or gas, is to refrain from treatment, for there is danger and deception in it, as I informed you in the section where I discussed tumors originating from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical protrusion is due to intestines or viscera, you should instruct the patient to hold himself and stand upright, outstretched, then apply medicaments around the entire umbilicus. Then you should have him lie on his back before you.  Then, using a wide scalpel, incise around the umbilicus at the spot you have marked with the medicaments.  Then, using a large forceps, pull the center of the tumor upward.  Finally, tie the incision with strong thread or fine silk, securely.
The knot of the bandage should be a loop. Then, open the center of the drawn-up tumor above the bandage, insert your index finger, and search for the intestines. If you find that the intestines have been caught by the bandage, loosen the loop and push the intestines back into the abdomen. If you find a hernia, pull it out with forceps.
Cut off the excess.  If an artery or vein is encountered, tie it securely, and return to your work. Take two needles, thread them with strong thread, and insert the needles into the incision you made around the tumor, piercing it firmly.  Then pull the tumor tight at four points on the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the drawn-up flesh rots and falls off of its own accord, or cut it off if it becomes thin and rotten.  Then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals.  If the umbilical protrusion is from flesh that has grown there.
Or if it is from moisture, you should cauterize the tumor as we said, and remove the blood or moisture you find there.  Then treat it with what will heal the area.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have previously mentioned the types of cancer in our classification, and how to treat it with medication, and warned against treating it with iron lest it ulcerate. We have also described cancer that develops in the uterus and warned against its treatment.
The ancients stated that if the cancer is in a place where it can be completely excised, such as cancer in the breast or the thigh, and similar removable organs, it should be removed entirely, especially if it is small and newly formed.  However, if it is advanced and large, it should not be approached.
I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve this.  If it is located in a readily accessible place, as we mentioned earlier, proceed with cupping to draw out the dark blood repeatedly, and then cauterize if there is a clear fullness in the veins.
Then apply cupping in a manner that will allow the procedure to be effective. Then insert the appropriate probes into the cancer, and cauterize it from all sides along with the skin, thoroughly, until nothing remains of its origins. Allow the blood to flow without interrupting it quickly.
Instead, press the areas and release all the thick blood with your hand or any instruments you can use.  If during the procedure, you encounter a large blood loss from a cut artery or vein, cauterize the affected area until the bleeding stops and then continue with the rest of the treatment until healing is complete.

If you see a patient of robust constitution, who has no other illness besides the scabies, and is neither a child nor an old person, then the procedure is to place the patient standing before you. Two-edged, long, and pointed, like a scalpel, except that it has some slight curvature so as not to penetrate the intestines during use and injure them. Then, observe if the scabies originated from the intestines.

At its upper end, there is a ring. When the instrument reaches the water, it will drain from it onto the instrument immediately.  Drain a moderate amount of water, because if you drain more than necessary at one time, the patient may die from the dissolution of their animal spirit.
Or it may cause a near-death faintness.  But drain only what his strength permits, based on the patient's pulse strength and complexion.  Then remove the instrument and retain the water, as it will immediately remain.
Because of the skin covering the opening in the diaphragm (which you were told to lubricate in that manner).  Then return the instrument another day if you see the patient able to withstand it.

A great deal of sweating in the bath and sun, and enduring thirst. Treat with dried medicines until he recovers. Cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the water as described previously.

Chapter Fifty-Six.
Concerning the ulcer that appears in the sheath, urethra, blackness, corruption, and adhesion of the sheath to the urethra.

Chapter fifty-seven.
In the purification of boys and treatment of their errors.
Circumcision is nothing other than separating a connection, like other surgeries.  However, since it is a deliberate act of ours, and particularly used in boys, we must establish the best procedure.
And the easiest path to safety. I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, because it was not used in their rites, but it is something we have acquired.
Through experience. I have found that the majority of craftsmen and barbers use cleansing with a knife, scissors, a cautery iron, ligature with thread, and incision with a lancet. I have tried all these methods.
Therefore, I have found nothing better than cleansing with scissors and ligating with thread, because cleansing with a knife often causes the skin to be damaged.  The skin of the foreskin has two layers; perhaps the upper layer is cut while the lower layer remains.
One is then forced to make another cut, causing further pain. Cleansing with a cautery iron does not prevent cutting the end of the urethra, as it might enter its opening.  Cleansing with a lancet might cause the skin to tear, spoiling your work, or the boy's foreskin might be naturally too short.
Often, such children do not require cleansing, and I have seen this.  Cleansing with scissors and ligation with thread, however, experience has shown me to be superior, because the scissors cut evenly, with the blade on top similar to the blade on the bottom.
When you squeeze your hand with the blades aligned, the cut is uniform and simultaneous.  This makes the ligature like a wall on all sides of the urethral skin, with no mistake at all.  First, the procedure involves calming the boy.

Do not let him sit; hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot.  Do not let the boy see them or any tools.  Then, insert your hand into his urethra and inflate the foreskin, lifting it up until the glans is exposed.
Then, cleanse it of any dirt that has collected there.  Tie a double-threaded knot at the marked spot.  Then, tie a second knot a little lower. Let the blood flow a little, as this is better for reducing urethral swelling. Then, quickly lift the foreskin upward and expose the glans. Let the blood flow a little, as this is better for reducing urethral swelling. This is a suitable scissor design for the procedure.
The scissors should be straight, sharp, and nail-shaped, with the blades the same length as the handles.  A common error in the procedure is that the inner skin may be completely or partially inverted during the cut.  In this case, you should gently spread it using your fingernail before the area swells.
Cut it [the skin] straight. If you cannot hold it with your fingernail, pull it with a hook and cut it. If three days pass and the area under the glans is still swollen and inflamed, leave it until the hot swelling subsides, and then carefully dissect it.

Chapter Fifty-eight.
On the treatment of urine retention in the bladder.
Urine retention in the bladder occurs due to blockage by a stone, coagulated blood, pus, or grown flesh, and similar things.  If you treat it with the types of remedies described in the classifications.
If the urine does not flow and you suspect the retention is due to a stone that has damaged the neck of the bladder, the patient should be placed on their knees, then one leg should be placed on the patient's back and the patient's stomach pressed against the back.  The patient should hold themselves as tightly as possible.
The neck of the bladder will then be forced to push the stone backward, allowing the urine to flow. If the urine does not flow by the method described and the patient's condition worsens, then extraction with the instrument called a catheter, of which this is the image:, should be used.
It is made of silver, thin and smooth, hollow like a bird's feather tube, about one and a half spans in length.  It has a gentle funnel at its head.  To draw the urine with it, a doubled thread with a piece of wool or cotton tied firmly to its end should be used.
The thread's end is inserted into the lower portion of the catheter, and any excess wool is trimmed using pliers, so that it enters the tube and closes it like a button. Then, the catheter is lubricated with oil, butter, or egg white, and the patient sits on a chair.
The bladder and urethra are moistened with soft ointments or lukewarm oil and water. Then, the catheter is gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the base of the urethra.  The urethra is then bent upward towards the navel, and the catheter is pushed further inside.
Once the urethra is bent, the catheter is pushed further inside until it nears the anus. The penis is then tilted downwards, the catheter inside, and it is pushed until it reaches the bladder, where the patient will feel it has reached an empty space.
This is done in this manner because the channel through which the urine passes is curved.  Pulling the thread with the wool slightly tightens it, causing the urine to follow the wool.  Then, the wool is removed, the urine is drained, and the catheter is withdrawn.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter Fifty-nine.

If you want to remove moisture from the bladder, insert the tip of the syringe into the moisture and pull upward with the plunger.  The moisture will be drawn into the syringe.  Then, insert the tip into the urethra, as we described for catheters, and push the moisture out with the plunger.

Chapter Sixty.
On removing the stone.
We have previously described the types of stones, their medicinal treatment, and the differences between kidney stones and bladder stones, along with their symptoms. We also discussed stones requiring manual removal and incision.
This concerns specifically bladder stones, and those that form in the urethra.  I will describe this clearly and concisely.
I say that bladder stones most commonly affect boys.  A sign of this is that the urine is expelled from the bladder like thin water.
Sand appears, he rubs and plays with his penis, and often it hangs down.  Then the anus also becomes apparent in many of them.  Boys recover easily from this up to the age of fourteen.  It is difficult in the elderly, and for young men it is intermediate between these two.
Those with larger stones have easier treatment, and the opposite is true for smaller ones.  If we are to discuss the treatment, first the patient must be given an enema to remove all the filth in his intestines, since it may obstruct examination of the stone.
Then, the patient is held by his feet and shaken downward to bring the stone to the neck of the bladder, or he is made to jump from a high place several times.  Then, he is seated upright before you, with his hands beneath his thighs, so that the entire bladder is tilted downward, and then it is examined.
And you feel for it from the outside. If you feel the stone in the space, immediately make a cut on it.  If it does not come into contact with your touch at all, you should then smear the index finger with grease from the left hand, if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger.
If it is a fully developed boy, you insert it into his rectum and search for the stone.  Once you locate it under your finger, move it gradually toward the neck of the bladder.  Then press on it with your finger and push it outward toward the place where you intend to make the incision.
You instruct one servant to squeeze the bladder with his hand, and another servant to stretch the labia upward with his right hand, and with his other hand to stretch the skin beneath the labia toward the location of the incision. Then take the grooved probe, which has this shape:
Make the incision between the rectum and the labia, not in the center but to the side of the left groin.  The incision should be directly on the stone, with your finger in the rectum, pushing it outward, obliquely, so that the incision is wide from the outside and narrow from the inside, to the extent possible for the stone to exit.
Do not use excessive force.  Perhaps the finger in the rectum will push the stone out easily during the incision.  Know that some stones have angles and edges, making their removal difficult.
Some are smooth, like acorns, and round, making them easy to remove.  Those with angles and edges should increase the incision slightly. If this does not work, devise a method to grasp them with a firm forceps, the tip of which is like a rasp.
To hold the stone firmly, so it does not slip.  Or, introduce a slender, curved instrument beneath it.  If these methods fail, widen the incision slightly.  If there is excessive bleeding, stop it with a cauterizing agent.
If there are multiple stones, first remove the largest into the bladder opening, then incise beside it.  Afterward, remove the smaller ones.  Do the same if there are more than two.  If the stones are very large, it would be unwise to make a large incision, as this could lead to one of two outcomes for the patient.
Either he will die, or he will have constant dribbling of urine because the area will never heal properly.  However, try to push it out.  Or, devise a method to break it using forceps so that it can be removed in pieces.
Once you have finished, treat the wound with kandar, sabr, shiyan, and shiddeh.  Place a bandage soaked in oil and a drink or rose ointment and cool water over it to calm the inflammation.  Then, the patient should lie on their back, and do not loosen the bandage for three days.
Once it heals, wash the area with plenty of water and oil.  Then treat it with nukhali and basilcon ointments until it is healed.  If an excessive, inflamed swelling or ulceration appears at the wound, or if blood coagulates in the bladder, obstructing urination, this is recognized by the presence of blood in the urine.
Insert your finger into the wound and remove the blood.  If it remains, it will cause the bladder to decay and become infected.  Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of problem with a corresponding remedy until healed.
Throughout the treatment, the thighs should be bound together to hold the remedies applied to the area in place.  If the stone is small and lodged in the urethra, causing inflammation and obstructing urination, treat it as described previously before it reaches the rupture stage.
Often, this treatment obviates the need for incision.  I have tested this, and it involves using a hooked instrument of tempered steel, with the following form/shape:
The tip is triangular and sharp, fitted to a handle. Then, take a thread and tie it around the penis below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder.  Then, carefully insert the hooked instrument into the urethra until the hook reaches the stone itself.
Rotate the hook gently on the stone, gradually aiming to pierce it from the other side.  Urine will flow immediately.  Then, firmly grasp the remaining stone outside the penis, and it will break apart and exit with the urine. The patient will recover.
If this method proves impossible due to an obstacle, tie a thread below the stone and another above the stone. Then, make an incision on the stone in the same area of the penis between the two threads, and remove it.
Then, loosen the threads and clean the clotted blood from the wound.  The thread was tied below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other thread was tied above to ensure, after the stone has been removed and the thread is loosened, the skin returns to its proper place, covering the wound.
Therefore, when tying the upper thread, you should lift the skin upwards so that it returns and covers the wound as we have said.
Chapter Sixty-one.
On removing stones in women.
Stones rarely develop in women.  If one of them presents with a stone, treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons, one being that the woman may be a virgin, and the other that you cannot find a woman who will allow herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is chaste or of those with close relations, and the third that you do not find a woman who excels in this craft, especially manual work, and the fourth that the opening for the stone in women is far from the location of the stone, requiring a deep incision.
And there is danger in that; if necessity demands it, a skilled female physician should be sought, though such are rare. If not found, seek a virtuous, compassionate male physician, or a skilled female midwife or someone knowledgeable in this craft to assist.
She should be present and instructed to perform all the examinations of the stone first.  If the woman is a virgin, the finger should be inserted into her vagina to examine for the stone.  If found, it should be pressed against the finger.
If she is not a virgin but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder and applying gentle pressure.
If the stone is found, it should be guided from the bladder opening downward to the extent possible, until it reaches the root of the thigh, then an incision should be made in the vagina, halfway down to the root of the thigh, from whichever side the stone is felt to be located.
Her finger should remain on the stone, pressing against it.  The incision should initially be small.  Then, the instrument should be inserted into the small incision. If the stone is felt, the incision should be enlarged as needed to remove the stone.  Be aware that there are many types of stones.
There are small and large, smooth and rough, long and round, and branched stones.  Recognize the types to determine the appropriate action. If bleeding is excessive, apply powdered alum to the area and hold it for an hour until bleeding stops, then resume the procedure.
Until the stone is removed. Prepare the instruments for removing stones from men to assist in your work.  If bleeding is excessive and you know the bleeding is from a severed artery, apply a ligature to the area.
Tighten the ligature firmly and leave it in place; do not readjust it. Leave the stone; do not try to remove it.  The patient may die.  Then treat the wound.  If the severity of the bleeding subsides after a few days and the area becomes infected, return to your work until the stone is removed.
Chapter Sixty-two.
In the fissure on the watery ducts.
The watery ducts are a collection of moisture in the white peritoneum beneath the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle.  It may be in a special membrane prepared by nature in one area of the testicle.
So that one might think it another testicle, and it exists between the scrotum's skin and the white peritoneum, which we mentioned.  This only occurs rarely.  This watery duct arises from weakness in females, where this substance accumulates.  Or it can occur from a blow to the scrotum.
This moisture comes in various colors: yellow, blood-red, dark-brown, or white and watery, the latter being most common.  The signs by which it is recognized are...
Where the water collects, if it's in the white peritoneum we mentioned, the swelling will be somewhat oblong, like an egg, and the testicle will not be visible, because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a separate membrane, the swelling will be round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, one might imagine it to be another testicle.  If the moisture is between the scrotum's skin and the peritoneum, it can be felt.  To determine the moisture's color, probe the swelling with the square probe, as previously illustrated.  Judge its color by what comes out when the probe is withdrawn.
If we move to treatment with leeches, the patient should be bled, if possible, and if the body is full, they should lie on their back, slightly elevated on a raised surface with many cloths underneath.  You should then sit on their left side.
Have a servant sit on their right side, extending their penis to one side of the scrotum and towards the lower abdomen. Then, take a wide scalpel and cut the scrotum lengthwise from the middle, towards the pubic area.
The incision should be made in a straight line parallel to the line dividing the scrotum in half, until it reaches the white peritoneum.  Carefully separate it, avoiding cutting it.  Make the separation more thorough on the side attached to the testicle, and extend the separation as far as possible.
Then, widely separate the water-filled peritoneum, removing all the water.  Then, using forceps, separate the edges of the incision and stretch the peritoneum upwards, avoiding touching the scrotal skin. Cut the peritoneum as much as possible, either in one piece or in sections.
Especially the thinner part, for if you do not completely sever it, you cannot be sure the water will not return.  If the testicle emerges from its skin while you are working, replace it and then suture the edges of the scrotal skin together.
Then treat it as you would any other surgical wound until it heals. If the testicle has been damaged by a pre-existing condition, you should tie off the vessels in the suspensory ligament to prevent bleeding, then detach the testicle from the ligament, remove the testicle, and treat it as described previously.
If the accumulated fluid is present on both sides, know that it is a double hernia.  Incise the other side in the same manner as the first. If you prefer, perform the procedure on one side only.  Then, wipe away the blood.
Place wool soaked in oil or rose oil into the incisions.  Place another layer of wool soaked in a mixture of wine and oil over the testicles and the abdominal wall, and put a folded cloth (the _rafa'id_) on top. Tie it down from above with the six-sided bandage in this manner:
Then, take two cloths, sew them together, and fill them with fluffy wool, like this example. Make the straps for tying from damp cotton or wool, gather them with a tight drawstring at the waist, and one strap will be taken from the seat to the back.
The second strap will be taken towards the pubic area, and the two remaining straps will be placed on the thighs from below, and the other two on the hips. Gather all the straps together at the waist.  This hernia can also be treated with cauterization instead of incision with an iron.
This involves taking a gentle, knife-like cautery iron and cutting the scrotum skin while it is heated, as described, until the white membrane containing water is exposed.  Take another cautery iron in this manner:
It resembles a Greek eye, then cauterize that membrane with it while it is hot, until all moisture is expelled.  Then, using forceps, pull the membrane and cauterize the layer containing it, cutting it as best as possible to completely remove it, keeping the testicle from being burned.
Know that this method is safer and less prone to blood loss than the incision method.  If, during the incision or cauterization, you encounter a burning tumor, blood loss, or anything else, you should treat each obstacle with the appropriate remedy as previously described.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure due to its unpleasantness, then use cauterization as described, if it is appropriate according to the evidence presented previously.
If the discharge is watery, the patient should sit on a high chair. Then cauterize the tumor underneath with a wide probe.  Make the cauterization along the body, with a wide opening, so that all the water drains out. Then apply cotton to the area and bind it.
Allow the remaining water to drain, then treat it until the wound heals.  If the water doesn't drain during the cauterization, it is because the white membrane is obstructing the wound opening, preventing the water from exiting.
In that case, you should insert a goose feather, or the instrument for extracting water from affected areas (a previously described tool), into the wound, or slightly enlarge the wound opening.
Know that the water may return and accumulate after six months or so.  If it does, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should stay in treatment for the time being.
Chapter Sixty-three.
On the incision of fleshy swellings and their treatment.
Know that incising these swellings is often a perilous act, leading to death in most cases. Therefore, we recommend leaving them alone and avoiding injury.  I will now discuss the procedures for dealing with them, and the types of tumors.
I say that many tumors can occur in the body parts that have a double structure.  This may arise from various causes, either from a sharp, concentrated substance that flows into the dual structure, or from a blow. The tumor's color is the same as the body's color, and there is no fluid in it.
The tumor is firm, and sometimes it becomes hard and dark in color, without sensitivity.  It may also be due to the entanglement and swelling of the arteries, as previously mentioned, or from swelling of the veins.  If it is from arterial swelling, this can be identified by the tumor's dispersal.
If you press it with your fingers, it will not give way; one should not attempt incisions at all.  If it is caused by venous swelling, nothing will disperse when pressed with the fingers.  This type can be incised along with the surrounding flesh.
This involves incising the scrotum, then pulling the testicle upward and removing it from the white peritoneum.  The attachments to the vessels should be freed, the vessels tied, and the attachments cut after freeing it from the testicle on all sides.  If the testicle has fused with the surrounding tissue growths.
Then the testicle should be removed and cut. If the adhesion is between parts of the peritoneum or between the vessels, all of this adhesion must be freed and cut in a circular manner.
If the tissue growth is located behind the point of attachment, then all of it should be cut, and the testicle removed, as we said.  Once this is completed, dress the wound with moist wool in rose-scented ointment and drink, then treat it with further remedies until it heals.
Sixty-fourth Chapter.
On the treatment of dropsy with dropsy.
Dropsy is a swollen, twisted tumor, resembling a cluster, with relaxation of the limbs.  It hinders the patient's movement, exercise, and walking.  In treating this affliction, the approach is similar to that previously mentioned.
However, one must follow the practice of the ancients.  I say that the patient should sit on a high chair, then pull the lower limbs downwards.  Then, grasp the scrotum skin with your fingers, along with the vessels near the penis.
A servant of yours should hold it and pull it sharply.  Then, with a wide, sharp scalpel, make an oblique incision next to the vessels until they are exposed.  Then, dissect from every side, as I described for the arteries in the temples.  Then, insert a needle with a doubled thread into it.
Tie it at the first points where the dropsy appeared, and also at its end.  Then, make a vertical incision down the length of the body in the middle, and remove the accumulated impure, corrupt fluids.
Then treat the wound as other surgical wounds would be treated, allowing time for healing. Removing the vessels feeding one limb is acceptable. If the dropsy affects all the vessels, one limb with the vessels must be removed, lest the testicle be starved of nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, it will wither and be useless.
Chapter Sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal dropsy.
This dropsy occurs when a rupture appears in the peritoneum extending across the abdomen near the groin.  The intestines protrude from that hernia into one of the testicles.
This hernia may result either from a rupture of the peritoneum or from its stretching.  These two types arise from numerous causes, such as a blow, a leap, a shout, or lifting a heavy object, and so forth.  The sign of a hernia from stretching of the peritoneum is that it develops gradually over a long period of time.
It does not occur suddenly.  If from stretching, the swelling gradually grows deeper, because the peritoneum compresses the intestines.  If from a rupture, the hernia is accompanied by intense pain initially, and the swelling will be visible under the skin nearby.
The intestines protrude, escaping from the peritoneum.  Pus may also be expelled with the intestines, making this a combined intestinal and purulent dropsy.  It may also contain air, or the intestines may accumulate faeces and become obstructed, leading to the patient's demise.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially when compressed.  Treatment for these types of ailments with iron is dangerous, so one should avoid it.  The procedure is to instruct the patient to return the intestines into their abdominal cavity with their hand if possible.
Then, the patient should lie on their back with you present, and their legs should be raised.  The skin around the groin is then pulled upwards, and the entire scrotal skin is incised lengthwise.  Hooks are inserted into the edges of the incision, as much as needed, to widen the opening.
The incision should be large enough to allow the testicle to protrude.  Then, the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin should be dissected until the white, firm peritoneum is exposed completely.  At that point, insert your index finger below the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum underneath the testicle's skin and the second peritoneum, detach the adhesions behind the testicle.  Then, with your right hand, draw it inward toward the scrotal skin, while pulling the white peritoneum upwards with your left hand, lifting the testicle and peritoneum towards the incision.
You instruct the assistant to pull the testicle upwards, and you completely detach the adhesions behind it.  Use your finger to search for any twisted intestines within the firm white peritoneum.  If you find any, push them back into the abdomen below.
Then take a needle with a thick, ten-stranded thread, and insert it at the edge of the peritoneum underneath the scrotal skin near the incision. Then, cut the ends of the thread until only four strands remain. Then, intertwine the strands, and tie the peritoneum, which we mentioned is beneath the scrotal skin, tightly on both sides. Then, also wrap the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that no blood vessels reaching it can penetrate. To prevent a hot swelling from forming, also make a second ligature outside the first, at least two finger-widths away. After these two ligatures, leave an amount of peritoneum under the scrotal skin equal to the width of a finger.
To prevent a hot swelling from forming, also make a second ligature outside the first, at least two finger-widths away. After these two ligatures, leave an amount of peritoneum under the scrotal skin equal to the width of a finger. Cut the rest completely around, and remove the testicle.  Then, make a cut under the scrotal skin, one that bleeds and oozes as previously described.  Then, use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound, and apply the ligature as we described.
The white peritoneum, which we mentioned after cutting, may be cauterized cautiously to prevent bleeding, as this often happens.  Leave the ligature until it falls off on its own. If it is slow to fall off, then soak it in hot water and apply something that causes perspiration, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with all other surgical treatments until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-six.
In this type of colic, I have seen no one dare to treat it with iron.  The ancients mentioned that it should be treated as described for colic with a pouch, i.e., the vessels are to be tied gently after incision beneath.
Then cut open the middle and treat the swelling to open it until the vessels fall out, and treat the wound as we described until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-six.
In the hernia which is in the groin.
The hernia in the groin may appear, as we said, bulging the area, but no bowel descends into the groin. If it does descend, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if it persists for a long time, the tear in the peritoneum increases until the intestines and testicles descend into the scrotum.  This results from the extension of the peritoneum in the groin, as we said. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes, and the area bulges.  Treatment is by cauterization, as its description was previously given.
Treatment may also be with an instrument, in the following manner: the patient lies on their back before you.  Then, the bulging, protruding hernia area is cut across, about the width of three fingers.  Then, the peritonea beneath the skin are grasped.
Until the white peritoneum beneath it is exposed. Then, you take a thread and place it on the bulging part of the peritoneum, pressing it back into the abdomen.  Then you stitch the two bulging areas together, one to the other, along the edge of the thread.
Then, remove the thread. Do not cut the peritoneum at all, or touch the testicle or anything else, as I instructed you in the treatment of intestinal protrusions. Then treat it with the treatments for surgical procedures.
If the threads are severed, cleanse and close the wound until it heals. The peritoneum will contract and the hernia will not increase.
And this is the better method, as it is closer to safety.
Chapter Sixty-eight.
Relaxation of the scrotum skin.
The scrotum skin often relaxes in some people without the underlying tissues relaxing, making the appearance unattractive.  Therefore, whoever wishes to treat this should have the patient lie on their back.
Cut all the relaxed skin away from the tough skin, then bring the edges together. If you wish, first stitch the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, securing the stitches, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the usual surgical treatment until it heals and the stitches are removed.
Chapter Sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
For this reason, I should not have mentioned it in this book. However, I have mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that a physician will know it if asked, and understand how to treat a person who has undergone it; and two, we often need to castrate certain animals for our benefit.
Like sheep, camels, and cats, and similar animals.  I say that castration can be done in two ways: either by crushing or by cutting.  For crushing, we place the animal in hot water until its testicles relax, soften, and hang down.
Then crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer discernible to the touch.  As for castration by cutting, one should hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with the left hand, then tie the ligaments and make a single incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles emerge, then cut them off after peeling them, leaving nothing of the membranes except the thin membrane surrounding the vessels. This method of castration is better than that which involves crushing.
Because crushing might leave some of the testicles, causing the animal to desire mating.  Then treat the wound until it heals.
Seventy-seventh chapter.
In the treatment of hermaphrodites.
The hermaphrodite in men comes in two types. One appears as a space or on the scrotal skin, between the testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva with hair. Urine may flow from the part located on the scrotal skin.
As for women, there is one type, occurring above the vulva on the pubis, like small, protruding man's testicles. One of them resembles a man's penis, and both are like female parts.  The treatment for these three types.
The two types of men and the single type of women, the excess flesh must be cut away until the wound heals, then treated with the treatment of other wounds until healed.  As for the second type of men from whom urine issues from the scrotal skin, there is no work to be done, and no healing at all.
Chapter seventy-one.
In the excision of the clitoris and the protruding flesh of women's vulvas.
The clitoris may increase in size beyond the natural limit, becoming unsightly and deformed.  In some women, it may enlarge to the extent of resembling a male organ, interfering with sexual intercourse.
Therefore, grasp the excess clitoris with your hand or forceps and cut it off, but do not make the cut too deep, especially not into the base, lest excessive bleeding result.
Then treat it with surgical procedures until it heals.  As for the protruding flesh, it is flesh that grows in the vaginal opening, filling it up.
And perhaps it protrudes externally, like a tail. Therefore, some ancient physicians called it the "tail disease".  It should be cut off, just as the clitoris is cut, and treated until healed.
Chapter Seventy-two.
On the treatment of ruptures.
Rupture is when a woman's vagina is not perforated or the perforation is small, and it is either natural, born with it, or accidental.  The accidental case arises from a prior cause and may be due to excessive tissue growth.
or thin or thick peritoneum, located either deep within the uterus, or on its sides, or at the top or bottom.  It prevents intercourse, conception, childbirth, and possibly menstruation.  A midwife can ascertain this through observation.
If the obstruction is visible and close by, but not visible, she should probe with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is thin peritoneum and close to the labia, then proceed with puncturing it—making incisions resembling incisions on the labia, and then place both thumbs of the hands together.
The woman should lie on her back with her legs apart.  Then, forcefully spread the labia until the thin peritoneum is punctured and the obstruction opens.  Then, she should take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area. The woman should engage in sexual intercourse every day so that the area does not close up again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, it should be cut open with a broad scalpel, resembling a thin leaf. If the obstruction is from overgrown flesh, it should be suspended by forceps and cut off. Have medications for stopping bleeding without causing punctures on hand, such as qaqia, shiyan, and frankincense mixed with egg whites.
Then a wide lead tube should be used so the wound does not close quickly.  Hold it for days and use a dry linen wick. Then treat with the standard treatment for abscesses until healed.  There may be other fleshy growths within the uterus, which should be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous growth, the cancerous tumor that occurs in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that appear in the female genitalia.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the veins' openings, causing a constant flow of copious blood.  If hemorrhoids advance, they become warts.  We have previously mentioned their types, signs, and we will discuss them here.
Of those that can be treated and those that cannot be treated, I say that hemorrhoids and warts that are deep within the uterus and not felt externally have no cure with iron. Those that are in the mouth of the uterus, which can be felt, are the ones that are treated.
The woman should enter a cool room, then scrape the warts with a lancet or a rough cloth and cut them from their roots.  After the blood flow from one of the blood-stopping tools, without pricking, apply it.
Such as qaqia, sheyan, and frankincense, and similar things.  Then raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cool water. If the bleeding continues, have her sit in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, and astringents, and similar things. Then, take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
Apply this to her back. If the bleeding stops, stop. Otherwise, apply leeches to the abdomen, breasts, without condition.  Then, take wool, dip it in the juice of thistle, or the juice of goat's rue, or the juice of the branches of the willow, along with astringent drink, and apply it to the affected area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until healed.  As for the red sore, resembling the heads of pustules, rough in appearance, you should cut away what is visible from it, in the same way as warts, and treat it until healed.
Chapter seventy-four.
On treating abscesses that appear in the uterus.
Many types of tumors, such as cancer, hard tumors, ulcers, gangrene, the eating ulcer, warts, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors, may appear in the uterus.  All of these diseases and their types and signs are mentioned in the classification.
We should mention in this article the hot tumor that appears in the uterus, if it is one of the tumors that accumulate for a period, how to treat it with iron.  Therefore, one must observe. If the pain of the tumor in its beginning is severe, with throbbing, inflammation, and fever...
If you see it red, and if it is sensitive to touch, do not rush to treat it with cauterization. Treat it with what aids maturation until these symptoms subside. Then, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats and lean on her back.
And bring her legs towards the lower abdomen, and let her thighs be spread apart.  Her arms should be placed below her knees and bound with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the cervix is opened, a depiction of which will follow later.  If you wish to use this instrument, you must estimate the depth of the woman's uterus so that what enters from the instrument does not exceed the depth of the uterus.
For if the instrument is deeper than the depth, it will harm the patient. Therefore, pads should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus. The spiral that runs within the instrument should be on the upper side.
Holding the instrument, the attendant turns the spiral until the cervix opens.  If the abscess is apparent, and it is felt by hand to be soft and thin, it should be incised at its head with a broad scalpel. When the entire discharge has been expelled.
A soft wick, dipped in rose oil or green oil with some astringent, should be placed in the wound. The wick should protrude from the incision in the cervix.  Clean wool, dipped in boiled water with baking soda, should be placed externally, on the uterus and pubis.
Then, after two days, treat with the appropriate ointment until healed.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water in which the root of the sassafras or long zira is boiled. Then return to the ointment. This is if the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
Avoid treating it with iron.  Instead, treat as described in the classification.
Chapter seventy-five.
On instructing midwives on how to treat living fetuses that emerge in an unnatural form.
The midwife should first know the natural form of childbirth.  Signs include the woman's leaning forward, her longing to breathe the air, and the lessening of her postpartum discomfort.
And if the delivery of the fetus is quick, know that this birth is in the natural form, with the head exiting first and the placenta either with it or hanging from its navel.  If you see these signs, you should massage her abdomen to expedite the delivery of the fetus.
If it emerges head first, the placenta will descend with it, and it should be cleaned thoroughly of any impurities. Any birth that differs from this is unnatural and blameworthy, as the fetus may emerge on its feet, with its hands or feet appearing before its head.
And one hand or one foot, or its head and hand, or it emerges curled, or perhaps turned on its back, and similar undesirable forms.  The midwife must be skilled and gentle in dealing with all these things, avoiding errors and mistakes.
And I am describing each form and how to handle it so you may recognize it.  If the fetus emerges headfirst, in the natural way, and the woman's labor intensifies, becoming difficult, and you see her strength weakening, seat her in a chair and order women to restrain her.
Bind her uterus with boiled linseed and moist ointments. Then, the midwife should insert a small scalpel between her fingers to cut the placenta, or cut it with her fingernail, until the moisture inside it flows out.  Massage the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then the woman should be injected with linseed saliva mixed with shiraj ointment.  Then, after the injection, instruct her to cough and use the kneading stick to make her sneeze, and hold her mouth and nose.  The fetus will emerge at that time. If the fetus's hands emerge first, then gently push them back, little by little.
If entry is not possible, place the woman on a raised platform and lift her legs upward. Then shake the platform on the ground, while the woman holds on so she does not fall during the shaking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them out gently. It will come out.  If the fetus emerges on its feet, then push its feet upwards, then turn the fetus slightly, little by little, until it is straightened out, then take one of its feet and pull it out gently, little by little.
If they emerge to the thighs, tell her to cough and make her sneeze with the kneading stick; it will come out. If it does not come out as described, then push the fetus back slightly, little by little, until it is in the natural position, then it will come out smoothly. If it still resists all these methods...
Take some saliva from the *khatmi* plant, linseed saliva, shiraj ointment, and dissolved frankincense. Grind all of them well in a mortar. Then apply it to the woman's vagina and lower abdomen. Then have her sit in lukewarm water until it reaches the ankles.
If you see that it has softened beneath, make her a pessary of myrrh and have her tolerate it. If she keeps the pessary for an hour, have her sit on a chair, then make her sneeze, and block her mouth and nose, and gently press her lower abdomen. The fetus will come out immediately; the fetus emerges on its knees and hands.
If it emerges in this manner, gently insert your hands little by little. Then, the woman lies on her back, her thighs hanging down while lying on a bed, and she clasps her hands.  Then, gently press on top of her abdomen little by little. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her legs are positioned lower. If the fetus does not emerge, take both her legs and move them vigorously. Then press gently on top of the ankles little by little, to bring the fetus upwards. Then the midwife inserts her hand and straightens the fetus little by little.
Tell the woman to strain so the fetus emerges.  The fetus emerges sideways, with one arm hanging down.  Carefully attempt to reposition the arm. If you cannot reposition it, have the woman stand up and walk. If she cannot walk, have her lie down.
Shake her legs vigorously. Then use the poultice described from saliva. If it is delivered, have her sit on a chair and carefully reposition the arm and straighten the fetus to its normal form. Make the woman sneeze and tell her to strain until it is delivered.
If the fetus emerges on its back, with arms outstretched and face towards the mother's back, the midwife should grasp the fetus's arm and gently turn and straighten it. Once straightened, apply the fats and saliva as described previously.
Then have her move slowly.  When she does, have her sit on a chair and instruct her to lean to her right side.  Then have her strain and make her sneeze with a small instrument, for this will help it emerge. The fetus emerges upright on its side.
If it descends to the cervix in this manner, with the placenta, cut the placenta with your fingernail until the moisture is released.  If it is attached to the fetus's face and neck, cut it from the navel so it doesn't suffocate and die.  Then push the fetus inside until it is in the correct position.
Tell the woman to strain repeatedly, and it will emerge easily.  For the delivery of twins or multiple fetuses, know that twins frequently are born, and three or four may be born and survive, though this is rare.  Five is beyond nature and they will not survive.
And they are surrounded by a single placenta, even if numerous, and are separated by a membrane dividing them, attached to the navel of each.  They may emerge like a single fetus, naturally and easily, or they may emerge in a difficult manner, as we have described.
If any of them are difficult to deliver and take on unnatural forms, treat them as we have described.  Let the midwife be gentle and do everything with care and patience, and find a way for each form using whatever method brings safety.
It is possible for one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or more than ten fetuses to form in the womb, and I have been informed of a woman who miscarried seven and another who miscarried fifteen, all conceived.
By the decree of the Exalted and Omniscient, all of this should be within the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing will befall her someday and she will know how to handle it properly.
Chapter Sixty-seven.
In the removal of the dead fetus.
If you have treated the dead fetus with the methods of treatment described in the section and it has not been expelled, and you are forced to use manual intervention, you should observe the following: if the woman is healthy and has no symptoms that cause concern about her suffering damage, she should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be tilted downward, her legs raised, and a servant should hold her from both sides or tie her to the bed so that her body does not move when the fetus is drawn out.
Then, the mouth of her uterus should be moistened with moist ointments, along with the saliva of the *khatmi* plant, fenugreek, and flax seeds.  The midwife then anoints her hand with these ointments and saliva, and gently inserts it into the uterus.  Then, she should look for a place to insert the hooks, targeting the appropriate areas of the fetus.
If the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, insert the hooks into its eyes, nape, mouth, palate, under its chin, clavicle, areas near the ribs, or beneath the collarbones.
And if it is descending feet first, into the pubic area and the middle ribs.  The hook should be held in the right hand, its curve positioned between the fingers of the left hand, and the hand should be inserted gently, driving a hook into one of the locations previously mentioned.
Until it reaches an empty space, then a second or third hook is inserted opposite it, to ensure an even pull, and not favoring one direction.  The pulling should be even and not simply in a straight line, but rather moving in all directions.
So that it may come out easily, as is done with a tooth when extracted.  In between, the pulling should be relaxed.  If it becomes obstructed in one area, the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them to rotate whatever is obstructed.  If some part of the fetus comes out, the hooks should be moved to other locations.
She [the midwife] should do this slightly higher, until the entire fetus is expelled. If a hand emerges before the rest, and cannot be repositioned due to compression, it should be wrapped in a cloth, then pulled out.  Once the whole thing is out, cut it.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.  Similarly, this should be done with the other hand and feet.  If the fetal head is large and has difficulty exiting due to pressure, or if there is accumulated fluid in the head, a pointed scalpel should be inserted between the fingers.
With it [the scalpel], the head is split open to release the water, or it is ruptured with the instrument called the *mashdakh*, the image of which will appear with the images of other instruments in the subsequent chapter.
Likewise, if the fetus has a naturally large head, the skull should be split or ruptured with the *mashdakh*, as we mentioned.  Then the bones are extracted with forceps. If the head emerges and is compressed at the collarbone, it should be split to drain the moisture in the chest.
Then the chest will adhere. If the chest does not adhere and cannot be extracted, cut as much as possible. If there is a swelling or lump in the lower abdomen, it should be punctured to drain moisture.
If the fetus is on its feet, pulling it and aligning it to the cervix is easy. If it is compressed in the abdomen or chest, it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and the abdomen or chest should be split to drain the moisture inside.
If all the other organs have been removed and the head remains lodged, the midwife should insert her left hand. If the cervix is open, she should insert her hand into the cervix, locate the head, and pull it with her fingers toward the cervix.  Then insert a hook or two and pull it out with them.
If the cervix has adhered to a hot, swollen tumor, it should not be forced. Instead, moist, oily substances should be applied, compresses used, and sitting in relaxing, moist water is recommended.
If the fetus is on its side, if it can be straightened, the methods described for a living fetus should be used. If this is not possible, it should be cut and removed.  Absolutely nothing of the placenta should be left inside.  Once your treatment is complete, use the methods described for treating hot tumors in the uterus.
If bleeding occurs, she should sit in astringent water and use other treatments.  I have witnessed a woman who conceived, then had a fetus die inside her, and subsequently conceived again.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a tumor appeared in her womb, swelling until it opened and discharged pus.  I was called to treat her, and treated her for a long time.  The wound did not close.  I then applied some strong, drawing ointments to it.
Then a bone emerged from the area.  Several days later, another bone came out.  I was amazed by this, as the abdomen does not contain bones.  I surmised that these were bones from the deceased fetus.  I probed the wound and removed many bones from the skull of the fetus.
I continued this procedure until I removed many bones, and the woman was in the best of health. She lived in this condition for a long time, with a small amount of pus discharging from the area. I have included this unusual case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skilled physician attempts with his hands in treatment.
Chapter Seventy-seven.
It contains images of the instruments needed for extracting the fetus.
Image of a spiral for opening the cervix.
This ligature, with which books are bound, has two spirals at the ends of the planks.  However, this spiral should be gentler than the ligature, and should be either ebony or boxwood.  The width of each plank should be two finger-widths.
Their edges should be about a finger's width, and their length one and a half spans.  In the middle of the planks are two pegs of the same wood, to which they are fastened.  Their length is half a span, or slightly more, and their width about two fingers, or slightly more. These two pegs.
They are the ones that enter the cervix to open it when you turn the spirals.  Image of another instrument for this purpose, gentler and lighter.
Made of ebony or boxwood, in the shape of claws, but with two pegs at the tip, as you see.  The length of each peg is about a span, and their width is two fingers.  If you wish to open the cervix with it...
Place the woman on a bed with her legs spread apart between her thighs.  Then insert these two pegs, closed, into the cervix.  While holding the end of the instrument below between her thighs, open your hand with the instrument, just as you would with the claws, to the extent that you desire to open the cervix. So that the midwife can achieve what she wants.  Image of another spiral, mentioned by the ancients.
