Chapter seventy-eight.
In removing the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, one should instruct the woman to hold herself, then have her sneeze with a cupping instrument.  Press your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, then do so. If not, take a measure [of something].
Pierce a hole in the cover, and place in it the herbs opening the cervix, such as fodenj, sedab, dill, chamomile, chih, sliha, and qanṭūriyun.  Place all of these, or some of them, in water and place it on the fire. Then place a silver tube on the hole of the pot's cover and the other end in the cervix.
Hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, then have her sneeze as we said.  The placenta will then come out quickly.  If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in the fat of a she-goat or in the saliva of the hawk.
Then, she should insert it into the [vaginal] passage and search for the placenta.  If she finds it, she should grasp it and pull it out slowly, little by little.  If it is stuck deep within the uterus, insert the hand as described.  When the placenta is located, pull it out slowly and gently.
So that the uterus does not fall with a sudden pull, but it should be gently moved to the sides, right and left.  Then, the amount of pulling should be increased.  It will then respond and detach from the adhesion.  If the cervix is closed, we have described the treatment with sneezing and the pot and herbs treatment.
If it does not come out with all the methods described, avoid force when pulling again.  Instead, tie what has come out to the woman's thigh.  Then, inject her with the four-component ointment. It will cause it to fester after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out.  However, if it festers, a foul odor will ascend to the stomach and head, harming the patient.  Therefore, appropriate incense should be used.
Therefore, some early practitioners have tried the incense of frankincense and dried figs.  Here is a depiction of the device that is used to fumigate a woman when menstruation or the placenta is retained.
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or made of copper.  The narrow end is placed in the vagina, the wide end on the fire, and incense is placed on the embers. It is held until the incense is gone and replaced with more.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of the unperforated anus.
Many boys may be born with unperforated anuses, blocked by thin peritoneum.  The midwife should perforate this peritoneum with her finger, or otherwise, lance it with a sharp scalpel.  Care should be taken not to injure the muscle.
Then place on it wool dipped in wine and oil, and treat it with ointments until it heals. If you fear it will close again, place a lead tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child desires to defecate.
A blockage of the anus may also result from a wound or tumor.  Such an abscess should be incised, then treated with the remedies mentioned previously and cauterized.
Chapter Eighty.
On the treatment of warts that occur on the soles.
Warts that occur on the soles are a thickening and hardening that develops near the anus, either externally or within the surrounding space on one side, and may be one or more in number.
If this thickening persists, it may open and discharge a whitish watery fluid or thin pus.  Some of these warts may penetrate into the rectum and intestines.  Those that do not penetrate may be recognized by what comes out of them—faeces and wind—when using the bowel movements remedies.
And perhaps worms may come out of them. Some warts, if in the surrounding space, may penetrate into the bladder and urethra, or into the hip joint and sacrum.  A wart that penetrates the rectum may be recognized in the same way as one that does not penetrate.
Insert your index finger into the rectum and introduce a thin probe made of copper or iron into the wart. If the wart is not convoluted, then if there is a convolution, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair in your finger. If you do not feel it at all, and nothing—no faeces, wind, or worms, as we said—emerges from the opening, then know that it is not penetrating, and proceed with treatment.
If the wart penetrates the bladder or urethra, the sign is the discharge of urine from it, and the inability of the affected area to heal with medications. If it penetrates the hip joint or sacrum, the sign is the probe reaching there.
If there is no twisting towards the bone, and pain is present in a certain area, with persistent pus discharge, and if treatment is ineffective and it does not heal with ointment, then these penetrating warts have no cure.
Treatment is futile for those who attempt to treat them, if they are ignorant physicians.  As for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, there is hope for healing with iron, as I describe and have experienced.  The patient should lie on their back before you.
Raise the patient's legs and tilt their thighs towards their abdomen.  Then, if there is a twisting in the wart, insert a copper or lead probe until you ascertain where it ends. If the patient feels the probe near the buttock, you should insert your index finger into the buttock.
If you feel the probe has penetrated through your finger, exposed without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and the probe, then know for certain that it is penetrating.  Do not waste effort, for there is no cure for it, as we said.
They say that it heals in some people rarely.  A treatment that may be helpful is to heat a thin cautery iron, according to the size of the sore, as previously mentioned, and insert it into the sore until it reaches near the buttock, repeating this two or three times.
So that you know that all the excess, hardened flesh, resembling a bird's feather tube, has been burned. Then treat it with flax soaked in clarified butter, until the burned flesh is removed, and then treat it with healing ointments, if he heals.
Otherwise, it will not heal with any other treatment.  If, however, you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger in the buttock, and there is a thick membrane of flesh or membrane between the probe and your finger, and you see the sore is just beneath the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the sore. While passing through the incision with the probe inside the sore, extend the incision to where the probe tip ends.  The probe is then removed and falls out, and the hardened flesh, resembling a bird's feather tube, is thoroughly cleaned, leaving nothing behind.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If blood prevents you from removing the flesh, the best and quickest remedy is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two beneficial conditions: burning away excess flesh.
And stop the bleeding, and dry the moisture. Then treat it with flax soaked in clarified butter or powdered sulfur mixed with oil until the area becomes suppurative and the burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that promote the growth of solid flesh.
These are medicines that have a binding and drying effect.  If the wound heals in this way, it is cured, and a return is not to be expected. If the sore has reached deep into the buttock and is far from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the buttock and probe it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh, and it is close to the point of penetration, there is no course of action other than hope and expectation. This is to use one of three methods: either to cauterize it, as we have said.
Or to slit it until you reach its bottom, so you can easily insert flax and medicine from close to the area.  Do not make the slit too deep, lest you sever the muscle surrounding the buttock, causing the patient to involuntarily discharge stool. Then treat it as we have described, and perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or to pierce through that barrier and puncture it, after slitting it close to the buttock, as we said, either with a probe or another sharp-pointed instrument. Then, thoroughly cleanse all the accumulated flesh in the slit to the extent possible.
Then, treat the entire wound, including the slit and the upper opening, until it is closed, leaving the sore open inside the buttock, which will be less distressing for the patient.  The sore may heal in this manner.
If, when you insert the probe into the sore, it is located on one side of the buttock, near the surface of the body, with the skin and the rim of the buttock, then take a probe with a perforated tip, like a cobbler's awl, like this:
And insert a twisted thread of five threads or thereabouts into it.  Then, insert the probe with the thread into the sore until it reaches its bottom.  If it is perforated within the edge of the buttock from the inside, near it.
Then remove the thread from that perforation by inserting your finger into the buttock and pulling out the thread end.  Gather both ends together and tie them tightly. Leave it for a day or two.  Whenever the thread cuts through the flesh, tighten it firmly until the flesh between the thread ends cuts off and falls away.  Then treat the wound until it heals and is completely recovered.
If the opening of the sore is not perforated, then perforate it however you can, except if it is very deep, for you should not do this because of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then do with it as we described until it heals.
The shape of the lancet used to pierce sores should have a very sharp bend, and the other side should not be sharp, so as not to cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
Concerning hemorrhoids that bleed, their cutting, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the anus, resembling red swellings or like grapes, and they may be small or large, and blood constantly flows from them.
They may be single or numerous, and they may be outside the anus, at its edges. However, those outside the anus are generally less moist; a yellowish or slightly bloody fluid constantly drips from them, and they are the same color as the body.
The treatment for those inside the anus is to instruct the patient to defecate and strain until the anus is stretched and the polyps are visible.  Then quickly grasp them with forceps or your fingernail and cut them at their roots, if the forceps cannot grip them due to their moisture and laxity.
Then, grasp it with a rough cloth or a piece of sharpened wood, or pull it out with your fingers, and then cut it. Afterwards, sprinkle some sharp powders over the cut to act as cautery, or cauterize it as described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with other remedies until it heals. If the anus does not yield, administer an enema to the patient containing a little irritant to wash what is in the anus and encourage quick expulsion when the patient strains.  As for polyps outside the anus, their treatment is easy and simple.
To treat it, grasp it with your fingernail or forceps, cut it, then treat it with the methods mentioned until it heals.  If one dislikes cutting with metal, one should instead use a needle and thread.
Then, pull the polyp upward and pierce it with the needle at its root on the opposite side, wrap the ends of the thread around the needle, which is positioned across it, and firmly pull the polyp.  Tie the thread and remove the needle.  Do this with all the polyps, leaving one unpierced.
To cause the discharge of excess blood, then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or cypress oil on the anus, have the patient rest, and leave it until the polyps fall.  If the polyps fall, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often arise from dryness, and constipation. If they persist despite treatment, it is necessary to scrape them with a scalpel or fingernail until they become moist and the upper crust that prevents adhesion is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, scrape it more forcefully than before until it bleeds and peels, then treat it.  It will then heal.
The Eighty-second Chapter.
On the treatment of inverted and non-inverted nails, dry warts, and the nits.
The nail is a round lump the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail, found throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and toes, causing pain when walking.
It is necessary to cut around the nail, grasp it with a pick or a hook and pull it from its root, then treat it.  If you wish, cauterize it as described in the relevant chapter.  As for dry warts, each is a small, rough, hardened, round protuberance on the surface of the body.
They often appear on the hands of children.  Treatment involves stretching the wart, cutting or piercing it with silk thread or a hair until it falls off. Or, if you wish, cauterize it as previously described, using fire or a potent medicine.
Inverted and non-inverted warts can be removed with the instrument I mentioned in the treatment of nits.  Nits are also a small, hardened, thick protuberance on the surface of the body, extending deep into it. If exposed to cold, a sensation resembling a stinging of ants will be felt in them.
They occur throughout the body, most often on the hands.  Treatment involves taking a tube of strong goose or eagle feather, placing the tube around the nit so that the tube encircles it completely, then turning your hand with the tube until you split the surrounding tissue.
Place it deep into the flesh and the nit will be pulled from its root.  If you wish, do this with a copper or iron tube, in this manner:
The top of the tube should be smooth and twisted, so that the fingers can easily hold and turn it. If you wish, cauterize the nit as previously described in the chapter on cauterization of warts.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Images of instruments used for injections in diseases of the rectum, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, or porcelain, or of hollow or hammered copper.  Smaller and larger versions of this instrument may be made, according to those who use them. The ones used to treat young children, or those with narrow or painful rectums, should be very gentle.  Image of a large syringe:.
The upper funnel where the tube is attached should be wide in this manner, with a barrier where the tube is attached, as you see. The lower tip that enters the rectum should be very smooth and polished, with two holes on one side and one hole on the other side.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of a reed, or slightly thicker. The tube in which you place the medicine, made from an animal bladder or sheep's intestine, should be shaped like a small spoon, with a diameter of one span.
Pierce it with many holes, as if it were turning.  There should be a distance of a finger's width between each hole.  Then, thread a strong thread of doubled yarn, consisting of ten threads or so, through those holes to gather the tube together, like a spoon. When medicine is placed inside, tightly tighten the top of the syringe between the stones, then inject.  This is a diagram of the tube and its holes:.
To inject with it: fill the intestine or bladder with medication, warmed to the patient's tolerance.  Beware that it is not too hot or too cold.  Then, the patient should lie on their back with their legs raised.
A mat or blanket is placed underneath.  The tip of the syringe is then smeared with ointment, egg white, or licorice juice, or the like.  Gently insert it into the rectum. Then, the attendant holding the tube squeezes it firmly until all the contents of the syringe are emptied into the intestines.
Then, the syringe is removed and the patient lowers their legs. If the medicine is prompted to exit, let them hold it as much as possible. If they sleep with it all night, it will be more effective in expelling. If it is used for diarrhea and rapid expulsion is desired, repeat the enema until healed.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the syringe must be very smooth, gentler than syringes used for children.  This is a diagram of a gentle syringe with its skin tightened:.
Chapter Eighty-four.
On the treatment of injuries.
I have previously discussed the treatment of conditions similar to burns in the classification.  Here, I will discuss their treatment with the hand and remedies in full detail.  I say that injuries vary according to the thing that causes the injury and the location of the wound.
The causes of injuries are numerous, such as a blow from a stone, a cut from a sword or knife, a spear or stick or arrow wound, and many other things.
Injuries vary according to location on the body, such as wounds on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and other organs.
I am describing the treatment of some injuries to make them a standard and rule for all other injuries. I will begin with simple head wounds, especially since compound wounds will be discussed in the first chapter of the third book of this text.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head, and is not a bone fracture, and if it was caused by a blow from a stone or similar object, and only the skin was bruised, and the wound was large, and you fear the occurrence of inflammation, then promptly lance the wound in situ. Do not delay this, and draw blood from him according to his strength, if there is no impediment to cupping, especially if the blood flowing from the wound is meager. Let the cupping be on the opposite side of the injured area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinks, and heavy foods.  If there is an impediment to cupping, then let him reduce his intake of food, and avoid meat and drinks.  If a hot swelling develops on the wound, apply a cotton swab dipped in rose oil, alone or with a drink that has a binding effect.
If you are not afraid of inflammation, then apply to the wound, if it is fresh and still bleeding and the air has not changed it (the air that I will describe to you shortly), and if the air has changed it, then apply some ointments to it.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and caused by a sword cut or similar, and its edges have not joined, then bring them together with stitches, as described in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin has been peeled back from the bone and is only loosely attached, cut it away. Then treat the wound with medications that will cause the formation of strong flesh to replace the skin.  Instruct the patient to eat foods that promote strength, such as heads of animals and similar parts.
If there is gangrene in the skin, and it is not attached to the bone, then cut away all of the gangrene and dispose of it. Then treat it.  If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding, and the bleeding does not stop with medications, then search for the artery.
If you hit it and it does not stop, cut it off with the lancet and tie it off.  If the methods mentioned are not effective, then cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, treatment is easy; simply sprinkle this powder over them while they are still bleeding.
Before the air changes it, the powder recipe is: one part frankincense, two parts gum resin, and three parts slaked or unslaked lime.  Grind them all together, sift, and fill the wound with it.  Tie it firmly so it adheres well.
It will clot with the blood, then bandage it from above with dressings. Leave it undisturbed as long as it doesn't swell.  The medicine will not be removed until it heals.  The lime alone can do this, if you do not have frankincense and gum resin.
This procedure can also be done with the lime in many larger wounds, if its application and bandaging are done correctly. This is for wounds that are fresh and bleeding, as long as they haven't been affected by air. However, if some air has altered them, especially if it's summertime.
Therefore, there is no effective use of this powder in this situation.  Treat it instead with the remedies mentioned previously, by applying some ripening ointments or a poultice made of barley flour, water, and honey until the pus is discharged, then treat with other remedies until healed.
However, if the wound includes a minor fracture of the bone, pull it out with a dry cloth.  The treatment for head fractures is discussed in the next section, in the chapter on neck injuries.  If the wound is in the neck, there is no difference in treatment compared to a simple head wound.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treatments that constrict strongly, such as verdigris, alum, and the like, because they harm the nerve and cause spasms.
Absolutely no cold substance should be applied to the wound, because the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  Use remedies that are gentle, such as washed rose petals steeped in fresh water, repeatedly soaked in oil, or rose and tutty ointment.
If you wash it also with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner, and use moist ointments.  If the wound is large, use sutures or bring the edges together with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a recess and pus has collected in the bottom, drain it from the lowest point. If an artery has been severed and you see blood flowing from the wound, tie it off, or cauterize it if necessary.
If part or all of the cartilage of the larynx has been cut, but the vessels are intact, stitch the edges of the skin to the larynx cartilage.  Do not touch the larynx itself, but smooth it and restore it to its natural shape, then tie it firmly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, probe it.  If there are bone fragments, remove all that is possible.  If not all can be removed, leave it until the wound becomes necrotic and heals, making removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powder we described.  If the bone in the wound becomes necrotic and forms a fistula, treat it as described in the section on chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see a recess, look to see if air comes out of it when the patient breathes. If so, know that it is a fatal wound.  If it has no recess and is fresh with blood, do not immediately apply powder or tighten it, lest the blood be trapped in its recess.
Otherwise, it will recede to the heart and kill the patient.  Instead, apply a drawing ointment twice daily.  If no ointment is available, place a used cotton swab in the wound opening to absorb the moisture. Have the patient sleep with the wound uppermost.
To drain any accumulated matter.  If three days or more have passed, and the patient shows no spasms, poor palpitations, or shortness of breath, and their overall condition is good, then know the wound is safe. Treat it with bandages and other remedies until it heals.
If it is impossible to see and the discharge is always present, know that it has become a fistula. Treat it according to the relevant section. The patient should lie on the wound, to drain any accumulated matter. If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with the previously described suturing method.
If it is large, or if small, if it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, probe the wound and remove those fragments if they are loose. If they are not loose, leave them until later.
The wound will fester, and this will make it easier to remove them.  The treatment of other injuries occurring in other parts of the body is governed by the same principles as those previously discussed.
Chapter Eighty-five.
On wounds of the abdomen, intestinal prolapse, and their suture.
A wound in the abdomen may be large, small, or medium. A large wound may expose one or several intestines, making their replacement and suture more difficult and challenging.
A small wound, too, can present difficulties in another way, making its repair more challenging.  If the intestines are not immediately returned to their place, they swell and thicken, making their replacement difficult.  Therefore, the best type of wound is the medium one, because it does not present the same difficulty in returning the intestines as the other two types.
Know that the sutures for abdominal repair are of four types. Two are general types, suitable for abdominal sutures and for the sutures of other bodily wounds; the other two are specific, applicable to all wounds but particularly suited to abdominal sutures.
I will describe them to you one by one with explanation. If the wound is small and something of the intestine protrudes, its return is difficult for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we have said, or because the intestine has previously swollen due to exposure to cold air.
If this is the case, it is necessary to warm it by soaking a sponge or wet cloth in lukewarm water, alone or with cooked cumin, safflower, and poppy seeds, and applying it to the intestines until the swelling subsides.
A drink with astringent properties may also achieve this, and is better than water alone in dissolving the swelling.  If you see that the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestines with water in which crocus or fennel seeds have been cooked, for this will make its return easier.
If, after this treatment, its return is still impossible, make a small incision in the wound with the instrument used for lancing boils.  The initial treatment, if successful, is preferable to incision, and incision is only resorted to as a last resort when all other means fail.  This is a picture of the instrument.
Its one curved side is limited, and the other side is not limited. The thin end is not as thin as the scalpel, but a little blunt. It is a small, somewhat awl-like tool, as you see.  If the wound is wide and the intestines have entered, it is necessary that their return be to their natural shape.
Its specific location, if possible, is preferable. However, if the wound is large and located in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs elevated higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, the head and chest should be higher than the lower body.
Likewise, if the wound is in one side of the abdomen, always aim to keep the side with the incision higher than the other.  This is how it should be applied in major and minor surgeries.
For smaller incisions, position it as best you can. Then, have a helpful assistant hold the entire wound with their hands, bringing the edges together, and then slowly expose the area to the person in charge of stitching.
This is the general method of stitching: take a needle or several needles according to the size of the wound, then leave a space the thickness of a little finger from the edges of the wound, and insert one needle without thread into the edges of the skin. With the edges of the peritoneum beneath the skin, from the inside, so that it penetrates from that side.  The edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum are now brought together in four layers.  Then, a double-threaded suture is tied around the needle multiple times from both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly united.
Then leave a space equal to the width of a finger and insert another needle.  Then thread it with the suture, as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with the necessary needles until the entire wound is closed.  Let the needles be of medium thickness.
Because very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick needles are also difficult to insert into the skin, therefore, they should be of medium thickness.  Also, the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too close to the surface.
So that the flesh does not tear quickly and the wound does not reopen before healing, and do not leave the stitches too far apart so that the wound does not prevent healing.  Then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient during sleep.  Provide him with linen bandages to cover both ends of the needles.
Leave them until you know that the wound has healed.  This type of stitching with needles is best suited for minor surgeries, as only one or two needles, or so, may be required for the stitching.
As for the method of stitching for a common wound, gather the four edges – meaning the two edges of the skin and the two edges of the peritoneum – together in one step with a needle containing a suture thread of moderate thinness and thickness.  Then, after the needle has passed through these four edges, return the needle from the same side from which it began.
So that the thread is interwoven over the top of the wound, just like stitching bags used to secure goods.  Leave a space of the width of a little finger between each stitch.  This method of stitching is known by everyone.
With this method of stitching, a surgical procedure was performed on a man who had a knife wound in his abdomen.  The surgical incision was more than a span long, and about two spans of the small intestine had been exposed. The wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
After the intestines had been exposed outside the wound for twenty-four hours, he returned them to their place. The wound healed in about fifteen days.  He was treated until he was completely recovered and lived many years afterward, functioning normally in all aspects of his life.  Doctors had initially concluded that he would not recover.
Surprisingly, I did not use any ointments because I was in a place without any medicines.  I placed used cotton on the wound twice a day and treated it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the specialized types of stitching, I will describe it based on Galen's writings: Begin stitching from the skin, inserting the needle from the outside to the inside.  Once the needle penetrates the skin and the muscle extending straight through the length of the abdomen...
Leave the peritoneum edge on the side where you inserted the needle.  Then, insert the needle into the other edge of the peritoneum, from inside to outside, at the other edge of the mesentery.  If you have done this, then insert it again into this same edge of the mesentery, from outside to inside.
Leave the peritoneum edge on this side, and insert the needle into its other edge, from inside to outside.  And as you insert it into the peritoneum, also insert it into the edge of the mesentery on that side, so that it passes through all of them.
Then, begin again from this same side, following the edge of the peritoneum on the other side. Bring the needle out of the skin near it, then return it to that skin, and follow the edge of the peritoneum on the other side along this edge of the mesentery.
Bring it out of the skin on its side, and do this again and again, until you stitch the entire wound in this way.  In short, the characteristic of this suture is that it stitches like a furrier stitches fur.
That you stitch the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leave it once from the other side, until it is empty.  As for the second type of specialized suture, which Galen also mentioned, this is his wording exactly.
It is to suture it in the manner some practitioners suture, by bringing each part to its matching counterpart by nature; they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the mesentery to its other edge, and this is as I describe to you.
The needle should be inserted into the edge of the mesentery near you, from the outside, and penetrate it alone from the inside.  Leave the two edges of the peritoneum.  Then withdraw the needle and penetrate from the outside to the inside of both edges of the peritoneum, and then withdraw it again.
And penetrate it from the inside to the outside in the other edge of the mesentery on the opposite side.  This type of suture is better than the general, easy suture previously mentioned.  This is Galen's exact words.  Some have said that the abdomen may be sutured with two needles. That is, you put one thread through them, and begin by inserting the needle from your side, penetrating to the other side; and insert the other needle from the opposite side, penetrating to your side, according to the method of suturing sacks.
Know that if the perforation is in the middle of the abdomen, its repair is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen.  As for treating the wound, its treatment is like other surgical wounds.  If you find the wound fresh, with its blood, before the air changes it, and you have returned the intestines to their place and sutured and strengthened it, then apply a medicated plaster to it.
If the wound has been altered by the air, apply some of the ointments available to you until it suppurates, the sutures fall out, and the peritoneum and mesentery heal.  Then treat it as you would any other wound until it heals.
If you lack medicines, apply powdered cotton wool from the outset of suppuration.  Change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals.  You generally won't need further treatment if the injuries are minor, unless you fear that the wound will cause significant pain in major organs.
Therefore, you should dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpits.  If he feels pain or foul-smelling discharge in his intestines, which often occurs, irrigate him with a lukewarm, dark astringent drink.
Especially if the foul-smelling discharge has reached the intestines and has become a penetrating wound into its interior.  Know that the thicker parts of the intestines heal more easily, and the thinner parts more difficultly.
As for the jejunum, it does not heal from injuries inflicted upon it at all, because of its numerous and substantial blood vessels, its thin tissue, and its proximity to nerves.  However, if the protruding part of the wound is recognizable and fresh, return it to its proper place, just as you would any other part of the intestines.
If it has been some time and the protruding part has turned green or black, you should tie a thread above the darkened area to prevent bleeding, as the protruding part contains blood vessels and arteries. Then cut what is below the ligature.
And leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the incision, outside of it, so that it is easy for you to pull it out and remove the protruding part when the wound discharges pus.  This describes a wound that occurs in the intestine. Now, if a small perforation occurs in the intestine...
Sometimes, in some people, it can be repaired. I saw a man who had been stabbed in the abdomen with a spear, the wound to the right of the stomach. The wound festered, and feces and wind escaped from it.  I treated him, though I didn't expect a cure. I continued to treat him until he recovered and the area healed.  When I saw the area had healed, I feared that a mishap might occur in his body. But he suffered no mishap, and remained in the best of health. He ate, drank, had sex, and went to the bath, and was active in his service.  Experienced practitioners have noted that when a small wound occurs in the intestine, it should be treated in this manner.
Take large ants with prominent heads, then bring the edges of the wound together. Place one ant, its mouth open, onto the wound's edges. When it bites down and closes its mouth, sever its head; it will adhere and not dissolve. Then place another ant near the first.
Continue this process with several ants, depending on the size of the wound. Then, close and stitch the wound. Those heads remain stuck to the intestine until it heals and recovers; no harm occurs to the patient. The intestine can also be stitched with fine thread.
To remove the animal glue from the thread, first insert the thread into a needle; then, pull a length from the glue,  and tie a thin, spun linen thread to it. Then, insert that combined thread into the needle. The thread of the glue is used to stitch the intestine and then returned to the abdomen. This method of stitching with ants and glue is merely a matter of hope and expectation.
However, if the tear is large and extensive, especially if in one of the small intestines, there is no remedy and no cure at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
On the treatment of the cold and the nasal discharge.
Know that any wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic, old, and ulcerated, and does not heal, and continuously discharges pus without cessation, is generally called, in any part of the body, a fistula, and we call it a cold, a fistula.
It is, in fact, a hardened, dense, white mass without pain, with a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some call it a feather.  Sometimes it is moist, constantly discharging pus, and at times the moisture may cease. This moisture may be copious, scant, thick, or thin.
I previously stated in the classification of surgical cases that every ulcer that does not heal, and in which flesh does not grow, is due to one of nine causes. One of them is either due to a lack of blood in the body, or its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside it or on its edges which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or it may be due to excessive harm and filth, or that the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter discharging from it is of poor quality.  Or it may be due to inappropriate treatment.  Or to a general pestilence afflicting the region. Or to a specific local condition.
Or it may be due to excessive harm and filth, or that the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter discharging from it is of poor quality.  Or it may be due to inappropriate treatment.  Or to a general pestilence afflicting the region. Or to a specific local condition. As was observed in the city of Saragossa, where the maturation of diseases is difficult, and the healing of tumors is slow, naturally, or because of the presence of one or multiple bones.  I have detailed all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the classification.
Here, I need to inform you of the treatment of fistula and the cold that occurs through manual work. Know that fistulas may occur in all parts of the body, some leading to large vessels, arteries, or nerves.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of many components, such as the hand and foot.  Some lead to the proximity of a principal organ, and so on.
Those fistulas of this nature are among the difficult-to-cure illnesses, which do not accept treatment, and working on them is tedious and fraught with ignorance.  If the fistulas do not reach the locations mentioned, and I have hope and expectation for their treatment.
If you wish to understand this, take a probe of copper or iron. If the fistula runs straight, explore it with it. If the fistula has a curve, explore it with a thin lead probe, for lead is soft.
If it (the probe) glides upon entering and turns toward the curve, if the fistula has many openings which you cannot ascertain with the probe, inject one opening. The moisture you inject will travel to the other openings and flow out.
Then, thoroughly investigate in any way possible to determine if there is a bone or nerve, or if the fistula's bottom is far or near, or if it is a single fistula with many openings.  Understand all of this as much as your keen, skillful investigation and the pain when you touch the area allow.
And similar to that are the signs and causes of the swelling. If you have ascertained all of this accurately, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  If...
If the fistula is superficial, close, or in a healthy area far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places I have mentioned, then incise the fistula as previously described, and remove any hardened tissue, corrupted flesh, and excess tissue.
And treat any flesh or warts that grow in its opening, until it heals. If the fistula is deep and straight, you should incise it as deeply as possible and then clean it of all its corrupt flesh.
Then, use medicated lint infused with strong medications and insert it into the fistula's bottom, which you can feel with the iron.  Do this repeatedly until the strong medicine consumes all remaining corruption in the fistula's bottom.
Then, treat it with ointments that promote healthy tissue growth until it heals. If it does not heal this way, cauterize it as previously described. If the fistula's cause is bone, and you are sure of this, then probe and incise it as I instructed you, unless something prevents you.
If it is a vein, nerve, or major organ, as we said, and the bone is exposed and shows some corruption and blackness, then remove the corruption until it is gone, and then treat it with something that promotes healing until it heals; if it does not heal and the pus persists as before.
Know that you have not reached complete removal of the corruption.  Expose all of it again, thoroughly removing and cleaning it with all your effort, then treat it. If it heals, well and good. If not, know that the corruption lies deep within. Expose all of it again.
Thoroughly remove and cleanse it with all your effort.  If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, remove it with the delicate forceps suitable for that purpose.
If there are many bones, remove them all completely; do not leave any behind.  If any ligaments obstruct them, and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and the flesh, if there is any, and free the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones immediately, apply something that will rot the wound. Leave it for several days until the flesh and ligaments around the bones rot. Protect the wound so that the incision you made during the procedure does not heal.
If you fear this, apply a cotton ball soaked in powdered sulfur and oil, or a cotton ball soaked in Egyptian ointment or another green ointment. When the ligaments have rotted and the bones have loosened, removing and pulling them out will be easier.
If the bone is large, like a leg bone or thigh bone, and only its surface is corrupted, thoroughly cleanse it until the blackness and corruption are gone, and then close the wound. If a significant portion is corrupted, and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, it must be spread open.
Cut it all the way to where the corruption ends. Then treat it until it heals. I am telling you about a case of a man with a sore in his leg, to make it an example and a guide for your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years ago, he experienced pain in his leg due to a disturbance within the body, causing matter to reach the leg and a large swelling.  The cause was not external.  Then, time passed with the misjudgment of the physicians, until the swelling opened.
A great deal of matter flowed from it, and his treatment was poor, until the leg became diseased and contained many openings, all discharging pus and bodily fluids.  A group of physicians treated him for about two years, and none of them were skilled surgeons.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg, and a great deal of matter flowed from those openings. The man's body had wasted away, and his complexion had turned yellow. I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
I found that some of them connected to others from all parts of the leg. I then opened one of those openings, revealing some of the bone. I found it decayed, eroded, blackened, putrid, and riddled with holes, even penetrating to the marrow.  I reported what I found.
I removed the diseased bone, and I believe that there is nothing but this decay in the bone that I excised and reported. I then applied healing remedies to the wound for a long time, but it did not heal. Then I again exposed the bone, beyond the first exposure.
I found the decay connected to the bone, and I reported what decay I saw as well. Then I tried to heal it, but it did not heal or close. Then I exposed it again, and I continued cutting the bone piece by piece, attempting to heal it, but it would not heal, until I cut away about a span of bone.
I removed it with its marrow, then healed it with medicines, and it healed quickly and was cured. This repetition of work and opening was necessary due to the patient's weakness and poor tolerance, and my fear for his life, because he was often suffering from fainting spells due to excessive vomiting. He was completely cured.
Strong flesh grew in the place of the bone, and his condition in his body improved. His strength returned, and he managed his affairs.  He experienced no impediment in walking that harmed him at all.  If a bone in any part of the body broke,
It is necessary to treat it in this way: take a bandage and tie it to the protruding end of the bone, and have someone pull it upwards.  Then make another bandage of thicker wool than the first.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone, and pull the end so the flesh is drawn downwards.  While you expose the flesh at the location you want to treat, so that the saw does not harm the flesh, place a plank or board under the bone from below, firmly in place.
Because if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the decayed bone.  The incision should be slightly above the decayed area, so that there is no decay in the bone cavity, which would not be apparent to the senses.  Then you will be forced to make the incision again.
If the bone is decayed but not protruding, rather its parts are joined together, and the decay is in its middle or some portion of it, then expose the flesh on all sides.  Place the board underneath, then make the incision from the first side, where the decay is.
When you have finished the incision, make another incision on the other side.  Let the incision be a short distance from the decay, as we said. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and make the incision on the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the decay is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy other than removal.  If the decay is in the hand or foot, treatment is extremely difficult.  However, you should remove the decay, however it appears, and scrape and clean it in any way you can.
By any method that is possible, provided that no vein or nerve is encountered.  Understand that the saws and instruments for cutting these bones vary according to the position, structure, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, strength, and fragility of the bones. Therefore, a tool appropriate to each type of work must be prepared.
Understand that the work itself will show you the type of tool you need if you have long experience and knowledge of the craft, for the master craftsman, having seen many cases, knows the nature of diseases. He may invent tools similar to those needed for each disease, and I have illustrated for you a set of tools at the end of this chapter.

Chapter eighty-seven.
In the amputation of limbs and the exposure of bones.
The limbs may decay either from an external or internal cause. If you treat this decay with medicinal remedies and the treatment is ineffective, and you see the decay spreading through the limb unchecked.
Then it is necessary to amputate the limb where the decay has reached, so that the patient may be saved from death or a great affliction greater than the loss of the limb.  A sign of this affliction is that the limb darkens until it appears as if burned by fire.
Or it may decay after turning black, so that the decay spreads to the adjacent limb and the entire body.  Proceed to amputate it.  Similarly, if the cause of the decay is a sting of venomous creatures like the sea scorpion, viper, or centipede, and the like.
If the decay or sting is in a fingertip, amputate the finger and do not delay the spread of the decay to the hand.  Likewise, if it has reached the hand, amputate it at the wrist, and do not delay the spread of the decay to the forearm.
Similarly, if it has reached the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow, at the joint itself. If the decay has spread and you see it affecting the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder at all, for that will lead to the patient's death. Use other treatments according to your ability.
Likewise, do the same for the leg. If the decay reaches the toe, amputate the toe at one of the joints. If it reaches the foot, amputate the entire leg. If it ascends to the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
If the decay has reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to leave it and allow the patient to succumb to death.  The procedure for amputating or dissecting the limb is to tie a bandage below the area you intend to cut and tie another bandage above the area.
One attendant pulls the lower bandage downward, another pulls the upper bandage upward.  You use a wide scalpel to expose the entire flesh between the bandages.  Then cut or dissect it.  You should place linen cloths on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct area, causing the patient excessive pain and a hot swelling.  If bleeding occurs during your work, immediately cauterize the area or apply some sharp hemostatic agents, then return to your treatment until it is finished.
Then bandage the wounded limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I tell you an example of a man who had this exact affliction in his leg.  There appeared a blackening with a burning sensation like fire in his leg.
This decay first appeared in his finger, and eventually affected his entire hand.  The man, when he saw the decay spreading in the limb and experiencing intense pain and burning, had it amputated at the joint, and he recovered.
After a long time, this decay appeared again in his index finger.  He came to me, and I applied remedies to his hand, having first cleansed his body. But the decay did not cease and spread to the other finger.
Until the decay spread throughout his hand. He asked me to amputate his hand, but I refused, hoping to restrain the decay, and fearing that amputation might cause his death because his strength was failing.  When he despaired of me, he returned to his homeland.
I was later told that he eventually cut off his entire hand and recovered. I have related this story to be of assistance in understanding this type of illness, and as a guide for treatment.
Eighty-eighth Chapter.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject medications into them.
If a tumor forms in any of the fleshy organs and persists for a prolonged period, and then bursts or subsides and all the matter within it is discharged, leaving the area empty like a vessel.
The skin over it, like a torn garment, is thin and has not yet progressed to the point of affecting bone, nerve, or ligament. For this reason it is called an abscess, and not a fistula, unless the condition persists long enough that the decay affects one of these structures.
In that case, it is called a fistula or a cold sore.  Its treatment is to cut all the skin surrounding it, especially if it has become thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it does not adhere to the area due to the decay.
If you anticipate that the skin will adhere, and the decay has not reached that extent, and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it by injection.  Observe the abscess; if it is large and the pus emanating from it has a foul odor, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris, in equal parts, combined in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens and achieves the consistency of honey. Then, take what you need, thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess.  Close the opening and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then, remove it by squeezing.  Do this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.  You may also inject with ash water if you do not have this medicine. This is done by taking the ash of olive wood or ash of oak wood and pouring water over it.
Then, strain it and inject it until you are certain that the abscess has been washed out. If the patient cannot tolerate either the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, inject with water and honey mixed with some pulverized verdigris.
Or inject with honey and a drink mixed together, because honey is capable of cleansing and washing, and the drink adheres to the abscess, especially if the drink has a quality of constriction and dryness.  If you do this repeatedly and are certain that the abscess's corruption is gone, then inject with something to promote flesh growth there.
For example, take some of the palm ointment and dissolve it in rose oil and a constricting drink, or inject it with some of the other medicines and ointments whose properties we have described in the chapter on ointments. If the opening of the abscess is narrow and does not accommodate the syringe,
If it is narrow, widen it slightly with a tool, or place a wick soaked in Egyptian or Sirriqun ointment inside it until it widens. Also, if the opening is wide, bring together its edges with stitches, leaving an opening just large enough for the syringe to enter without excess.
Also, if the opening from which pus is flowing is located high up, cut it in the lowest place so the pus flows downwards, because if pus is congested in the depths of the abscess, it prevents the flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the abscess towards the bottom as you wish, then apply pressure to the limb so that the pus flows out easily as best you can, and ensure that no pus remains trapped inside.
As for the uniting medicines to be applied to the abscess from the outside, and how to apply them, take one of the uniting ointments appropriate to your needs, and spread it on a linen cloth. The cloth should be large enough to cover the entire abscess. Then, using scissors, cut a hole slightly larger than the opening of the abscess.  Spread a soft ointment onto another cloth, sized to fit the hole, and place it over the hole, keeping it away from the abscess opening to allow easy drainage of pus. Do not remove the larger cloth until later.
For several days, the one over the opening of the abscess is the one that is removed at all times so that the collected pus may drain out, and you may know whether the pus that flows from it is copious or scant, or mature or immature.
And also, examine the location of the abscess itself. Does the patient feel pain there, or not? Is there swelling, or not?  If your treatment continues in this manner, and you see the discharge from the abscess opening becoming slight,
Know that the abscess has adhered or is close to adhering.  Then, treat it again tomorrow or on the third day.  When you treat the wound, replace the cloths with new ones and renew the ointment. If after a long time, thin pus comes out of the abscess, do not despair of its adhesion.
For healing is near, because some people have slow tissue growth in their wounds, and others have the opposite. If, after many days, unripe pus comes out of the abscess, know that the abscess has not adhered. If the abscess's adhesion is delayed,
Then increase the drying of the medications. Let the medications be moist in consistency and dry in strength, such as the palm ointment, if more opoponax has been added to it.  The most effective treatment is to take the palm ointment.
It can be treated with rose oil and sprinkled with moderate-strength, aged liquid, then mixed with it and used.  One can do the same thing if palm ointment isn't available; honey, if cooked until it thickens, can be used, or take myrrh, aloes, and kandar and grind them all together and sprinkle them on the honey while it is on the fire. Then, apply it to a cloth and bandage it over the abscess, or apply the thickened cooked honey, sprinkle the remedies on it, and place cloths over it and bandage it.
Long zeraun, the rhizome of the asphodel, kresna flour, and rue, either used alone or combined, can also be ground, sifted, and sprinkled on the honey I described, and used. If the abscess persists and doesn't heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
If it has affected the bones, or other tough nerves or ligaments, treat it as a boil, as described previously.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
In treating the abscess, the injured finger, the excess finger, and the divided joining of fingers.
A daḥs is a thick fleshy growth that grows beneath the nail of the thumb or toe, and sometimes on other fingers. If it persists and is neglected, it swells into a hot, inflamed tumor.
Or it may fester and become so severe as to consume the root of the nail, and possibly destroy the entire nail.  Perhaps the decay reaches the bone, causing a foul odor, broadening the fingertip, and discoloration.  If you treat it with the methods we mentioned in the classification and your treatment is unsuccessful.
Then you should cut away all excess flesh growing from the nail with a knife, and then cauterize the wound.  Cauterization in this case is very effective.  If the bone is healthy and the nail also,
If the corner of the nail had pressed the flesh inwards, causing it to be pricked and irritated, then a thin strip of material should be placed under the corner of the nail that is pricking the flesh, lifting it upwards, and the flesh should be gently cut away.
Apply caustic and burning medications to the remaining flesh until it is all gone, then treat with ointments until it heals. If the decay has affected the bone, then that bone must be cut out and removed, for it will never heal as long as there is decayed bone.
If you see decay and erosion spreading in the finger, amputate it at one of the phalanges, as previously mentioned, and then treat it until it heals. If the nail is struck or injured and severe pain ensues, then you should first bleed the affected area.
Then split the nail with a sharp scalpel, making a slanted split from top to bottom. Be careful not to reach the flesh underneath the nail with the split, as this will cause intense pain to the affected area and lead to excess flesh growth at that spot. Then treat the area with something that relieves the pain.
Concerning the extra finger that develops in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may have bone in some parts, or it may even have a nail. Some of it may grow from the base of a joint of another finger.
Or it may grow on some of the finger's phalanges. Those that grow on some of the finger's phalanges do not move, and those that grow at the finger joint may move. Whatever part of it is purely flesh can be easily removed by cutting it at its base with a broad scalpel.
However, those that grow from the base of the joint are difficult to treat, so avoid cutting them.  As for those that grow on the finger at one of the phalanges, it is necessary to first cut the flesh in a circular manner down to the bone, then to use one of those appropriate saws to split the bone.
Then treat it until it heals.  As for the adhesion that sometimes occurs between fingers, this frequently happens, either present from birth, or resulting from a healed wound, burn, or similar injury.  In such cases, it is necessary to separate the adhesion.
So that the fingers return to their natural position.  Then place between them a cloth or a wick soaked in rose oil, so that they do not quickly adhere again, and separate them or place a thin sheet of lead between them, so that the area heals as it should.
Likewise, if adhesion occurs between some of the fingers and the palm, separate the adhesion according to what is suitable and appropriate for the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On cutting the veins.
Veins are twisted, thick blood vessels filled with dark, blackish phlegm, occurring in most parts of the body, and most frequently in the legs, especially in those who travel and in porters and carriers.  First, one should use vigorous rubbing of the body to remove the dark matter repeatedly.
Then, bleed the affected person using a lancet.  Treatment with iron is of two types: one is to cut and release the dark blood; the other is to cut the vein and remove it completely.  For cutting it, the leg is first warmed with hot water.
Until the thick, murky blood is loosened; then, the patient's leg is bound with a bandage from above the thigh to below the knee; then, the vein is cut in one, two, or three wide incisions.
Then, remove the black blood with your hand from the bottom of the leg upwards and from top to bottom, until you have removed as much blood as you deem sufficient or as much as the patient's strength allows; then, bandage it, and instruct the patient to avoid foods that produce black phlegm, and repeat vomiting and bleeding.
When the veins become full and harm the patient, as for its removal, it will be done in this way: shave the patient's leg if it has a lot of hair; then, place the patient in a bath and warm the leg with hot water until it turns red and sweats, or have them do vigorous exercise.
If a bath is not available to warm the limb, then cut the skin alongside the vein lengthwise, either near the knee or lower at the heel, then open the skin with hooks and peel the vein from all sides until it is visible to the touch.
When it appears, you will see it red and smooth. Once it is freed from the skin, it will also appear like a tendon. Then, insert a probe under it until it rises and protrudes from the skin; then, use a smooth, blunt hook to lift and remove it. Then, make another incision near that incision, about the size of three fingers.
Then peel the skin off the vein until it is exposed. Then lift it with the probe as you did before and hang it with another hook as you did at first, then make another incision, or multiple incisions, if needed.
Then remove it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel. Then pull it out and remove it until it emerges from the second incision, then pull it towards the incision above it, and do this until you pull it out from the third incision, above all the incisions.  Once it is all out, cut it.
If pulling and removing it does not work, then insert a needle with a strong, doubled thread, tie it, and pull it. Insert the probe under it and twist your hand in every direction until it comes out, and be careful that it doesn't break. If it breaks, it will be very difficult to remove it.
Leaving it untreated will harm the patient.  Once you have removed it entirely, apply wool soaked in a potion and rose-water or oil to the surgical site and treat it until it heals. If the varicose vein is twisted, winding, and has bends in various directions.
If it was not straight, as we said, then you should make an incision along every bend and twist.  Then hang it from hooks until you remove it completely.  Be careful when making your incisions to not cut or injure the vein, because this will make it more difficult to remove, thus conserving your effort.
Image of the incision used for the varicose vein.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not have a bend like other hooks, nor a sharp tip, so as not to injure the vein.  It should be thick and smooth in its bend, for if it is thin, it will cut the vein with its thinness.  Rather, it should have a desirable thickness, as we said.
Chapter Ninety-one.
In the case of the superficial vein.
This vein arises in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and the Arab lands, and in hot, sparsely populated, barren countries. It may also arise in other parts of the body besides the legs, arising from an infection that occurs beneath the skin.
As it occurs within the bodies of snakes and worms, and the swelling of the gourd, and the worm that grows between the skin and flesh. A sign of the beginning of this vein's occurrence is that a severe burning sensation occurs in the leg, then the area swells, and then the vein begins to emerge from the location of the swelling.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal.  Once its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing one to two dirhams, tied, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it outwards, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it grows excessively, cut off some of it and wrap the rest.  Do not cut it from its root before it is all out, because if you cut it, it will shrink and enter the flesh, causing a swelling, an infection, and a foul ulcer in the area.
Therefore, it should be slowly drawn out little by little until the last bit is out and nothing of it remains in the body.  In some individuals, this vein can extend to five or ten cubits in length.
It has come to my attention that a vein of twenty cubits emerged from a man. If it breaks during your treatment, insert a drain into the hole and extend it a long way into the body so that all the material within is expelled.  Try to heal the area with remedies for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or the leg itself, producing many openings and branches emerging from each opening. Treat it as described in the classification and as previously mentioned.
Chapter Ninety-two.
Concerning the abscesses that develop under the hoof and are called "the cow's disease".
This disease is called "the cow's disease" in some of our countries because it often affects cows. It is a small, single worm that develops between the skin and flesh, moving throughout the body, both up and down.
The worm's movement from one organ to another can be sensed, until it pierces the skin at a point.  Its formation is from the putrefaction of some humors, as occurs with worms, snakes, and the "gourd" swelling in the abdomen.
The danger it poses is that if it moves throughout the body and ascends to the head, reaching the eye, it may break through the eye and escape, causing blindness, which often happens.  Therefore, its removal is possible only when its movement and presence are perceptible.
Therefore, one should tie tightly above and below it with a strong bandage, then cut open and remove it. If it has burrowed deep into the flesh and cannot be found, apply cauterization with fire to the area until it is burned away.  Its greatest potential harm is to the eye, as we have said.
If it is seen in the head, near the eye, tie tightly below it on the forehead, then cut open and remove it.  The patient must take care to cleanse their body with medicines that ease the expulsion of putrid, poor humors and avoid foods that generate putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-three.
Concerning the incision for the disease known as the "nafar".
This disease, called "nafar" in our country, is a pain that appears in certain parts of the body and then moves from one part to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a woman in the countryside who revealed her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of her arm. After an hour, I saw the swelling moving up the forearm, like a worm moving quickly towards her shoulder, as quickly as mercury can move.
If it moves from one place to another, the pain disappears from that place and settles in the shoulder. After an hour, it ran throughout the rest of the body, even to the other arm.  She told me that it circulated through her entire body, as I observed.
I was amazed by the speed of its movement from one part of the body to another.  I had not previously seen this disease in this form with my own eyes, although I had heard of others who experience pain moving from one part to another.  However, I had not seen it firsthand as I saw it in this woman.
I attributed this only to the fact that the woman was from the desert, with a dry body and exposed veins.  Therefore, this moving sensation was apparent to the touch.  It is likely not to appear in the same way in those of comfort, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it, and the patient feels pain, if it appears visibly as described, then firmly press above and below it, and lance it so that the congested air escapes and is released from the location.
If you do not see it, treat it by shaking the body and dispersing the air, like the ointment for foul smell and the ointment for scabies, and similar medicines.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On extracting arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and the locations where they pierce the body.  Their variation by type includes large and small, hollow and solid glass, and those with three and four angles.
Some have tongues, and some have shards.  As for their location in relation to the body's organs, they are of two types: either they pierce major hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. If an arrow strikes one of these organs, and signs of death, which I will describe to you later, appear, then you should avoid extracting the arrow, for death will usually follow the sufferer. If these unfavorable signs do not appear, and the arrow has not been driven deep into the organ, extract it and treat the wound.  And among the signs of an arrow striking the brain, piercing the bone and wounding the brain's membranes, are severe headache, dizziness, and vertigo.
If these unfavorable signs do not appear, and the arrow has not been driven deep into the organ, extract it and treat the wound.  And among the signs of an arrow striking the brain, piercing the bone and wounding the brain's membranes, are severe headache, dizziness, and vertigo. Redness in the eyes and tongue, convulsions, confusion of mind, vomiting, and perhaps bleeding from the nostrils or ears; possibly the loss of speech and voice; and a white, porridge-like moisture, resembling the watery part of meat, flowing from the wound site.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow, if it has not already been removed.  As for the signs of an arrow striking the heart, if it is near the left breast and feels as if it has lodged in something solid rather than empty, ...
...and perhaps there is a pulsating motion like that of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound.  This may be followed by coldness in the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting; know that death is inevitable.  A sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels following the neck swell, the patient's complexion changes, and they breathe heavily, craving the inhalation of cool air.  If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be close to the small ribs, and breathing will be labored with severe pain.
And the patient sighs and all the muscles of the shoulders move.  If the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain will follow, and blood resembling the liver in its redness will flow from the wound.  If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food may issue from the wound.
The situation is obvious. If the arrow strikes the abdomen, causing a perforation, and something from the intestines or bowels is expelled from the wound, there is no hope for treatment or removing the arrow.  If the arrow strikes the bladder, urine will be expelled.
If something protrudes from the wound and the patient's pain intensifies, know that he is doomed.  As for the other organs, like the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, the majority of the time they will escape harm.
Unless the arrow strikes an artery or nerve, and unless the arrow is poisoned, I will tell you about some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may deduce treatment from it.  An arrow struck a man's eye socket at the root of his nose.
I removed it from the other side, under the earlobe, and he recovered without any harm to his eye.  I removed another arrow for a Jew, which had lodged in his earlobe beneath the lower eyelid.  The arrow had receded, and I only retrieved its small tip, which adhered to the shaft.
It was a large arrow, from a composite bow, with square, smooth iron, having no barbs.  The Jew recovered without any harm to his eye.  I also removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was Arab-made.
It had two barbs. I split it open between the windpipes.  It had lodged in his throat, and I gently worked on it until I removed it. The Christian survived and recovered. I also removed another arrow that had lodged in a man's stomach, and we thought he would die from it.
After about thirty days or so, and with no change in his condition, I split the arrow and worked on it until I removed it. He recovered and no harm befell him. I saw a man with an arrow lodged in his back, and the wound had become inflamed.
Then, seven years later, the arrow came out from the base of his thigh.  I saw a woman with an arrow lodged in her abdomen, where the wound had become inflamed.  The arrow remained, but there was no change in her condition, and she experienced no impediment in her normal activities.
I saw another man with an arrow lodged in his face, where the wound had become inflamed.  He felt little pain.  There are many like this.  I removed an arrow from the nose of one of the Sultan's officers. It had lodged in the middle of his nose, slightly to the right side.
The arrowhead disappeared completely. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow was lodged. I found the arrow wound very narrow.  I probed it with a fine instrument, but felt nothing.  He was experiencing a prickling sensation and pain under his ear, on the right side.
I hoped that the prickling was from the arrowhead, and so I dressed the area with a dressing that promoted attraction and maturation, hoping the area would swell and the arrow's position would become evident, so I could cut into it. But nothing happened to suggest that the arrow had reached that location.
I continued to apply the dressing for many days, but nothing happened.  The wound eventually healed.  The patient remained hopeless about extracting the arrow for a long time, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose. He informed me, and I applied a strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it. I felt the thin arrowhead, which was stuck to the bone.  Then I continued to enlarge the wound with that strong medicine until the arrowhead became visible.  I spent about four months with him.
Then, when the wound was widened and I was able to insert the forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it did not come out.  I continued to gently manipulate it with various instruments until I grasped it one day with strong forceps, as their form will be shown in the last section of this book.
The physicians had declared that his nasal cartilage could not be repaired, but I repaired it. The wound healed, and the patient recovered completely without any further discomfort. I am telling you how I extracted some arrows so that you may use this as a guide and example for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft, and their details, cannot be grasped by description or encompassed by a book. Instead, the skillful craftsman makes comparisons between the little and the much, between what is present and what is absent, and invents a new action and a new instrument when faced with unusual cases.
Concerning this craft, I say that arrows are extracted from the organs where they are lodged in two ways: either by pulling them from the point of entry, or from the opposite side, which is the point of exit.
If the arrow is protruding through flesh, it can be pulled out.  If it resists removal, you should leave it for several days until the surrounding flesh becomes infected, making its removal easier. The same applies if it is lodged in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for several days and try pulling and moving it each day.  It will eventually come out.  If it still does not come out after several days, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from every side with a delicate instrument until the arrow loosens, then pull it out.
If the arrow is lodged in the skull bone and has penetrated one of the brain cavities, and some of the symptoms I mentioned have appeared, do not pull out the arrow.  Leave it until its condition improves after several days.
Proceed with pulling out the arrow.  If it is deeply embedded and resists removal, use the instruments around the arrow as described previously. Then treat the wound until it heals.  If the arrow is hidden in a part of the body, and it is hidden from sight, probe for it with a probe. If you locate it, pull it out using appropriate tools. If you cannot, because the wound is narrow and the arrow is deeply embedded, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way,
make an incision to widen the wound and gain access to the arrow so that you can remove it.  If the arrow has two barbs, grasp them and carefully separate the surrounding flesh from each side using any method possible. If you cannot remove the flesh, break or twist the barbs until you are able to remove it.
When attempting to remove the arrow from any location, use forceps to twist it in all directions until you remove it. Be extremely gentle so the arrow does not break and make removal more difficult. If you cannot remove it after your attempt,
leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh decays. Then try again.  If bleeding occurs, apply the treatments mentioned in the section on bleeding, and be careful not to sever a vein, nerve, or tendon. Use your best judgment.
Use every method possible to remove the arrow, gently, carefully, and firmly, as described.  When pulling the arrow out, try to restore the wound to the shape it was in when the arrow entered. This is the best approach.
If that is not possible, use whatever methods are available. As for the arrow exiting from the opposite side, either a part of it may have already protruded outwards, or you can feel the arrowhead near the surface of the skin and see it protruding.
Cut along it, making the incision large enough for the forceps to grasp. Then pull it out, as it will be easier to remove. If it is lodged in bone, twist your forceps in a circular motion until the arrow loosens itself in the bone and makes room for removal. Then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days and try again until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is still present, push it out. If the shaft has fallen out and you wish to use pushing, insert the hollow instrument into the arrowhead's barb to push it out.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that fits into the hollow.  This will make removing the arrow easier. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cauterize all the affected flesh if possible, then treat it appropriately.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or side, and it is close enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision, cut and be careful not to sever a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if necessary, and treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps used to extract arrows:.
Its tips are like the beak of a bird, crafted like a file so that when it grasps an arrow or anything, it does not let go.  There are large, small, and medium sizes, all according to the size of the arrow, the size of the wound, and the width of the wound.
Image of the hollow cannon:.
Image of the solid cannon.
This is solid at the tip, like a rod, to easily enter the hollow arrow and push it out.
Chapter Ninety-five.
On the opening of veins.
