The veins customarily opened in the body are thirty.  Of these, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the *ḥassīsīn*; the two arteries visible on the temples; and the two veins at the corners of the eyes, known as the *nāẓirīn*.
The vein that stands out in the middle of the forehead; the vein at the tip of the nose; the two vessels in the neck; the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth; the two veins in the lower lip; these veins are known as the *jayyār*; and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins opened in the arm and hand, they are five. One is the *qayfāl*, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the vein of the head.  The *ʾakḥal*, the middle vein, is a combination of a branch of the *bāsīliq* and a branch of the *qayfāl*.  The common people call it the body vein.
And the *bāsīliq*, which is located on the inner side, and is also called the *ʾibṭī*. The common people call it the belly vein and the arm cord. It is located on the *zanad* (forearm), where it is attached. It is the one that clearly appears above the thumb, and the *ʾasīlim*.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, with two branches. In the leg and foot, there are three veins: one under the bend of the knee on the outer side; the second is the *ṣāfin*, located at the heel on the inner side; and the vein of the *nisāʾ*, located at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg, there are three veins like them. As for the two veins behind the ears, their incision is beneficial for chronic colds, migraine, ringworm, and chronic bad head sores.  The method of incision for them, as I will describe.
It is to shave the patient's head and rub the back of the head at the location of the veins with a rough cloth, rubbing well. Then the patient's neck should be strangled with a turban until the veins appear, behind the ears, in the low areas of the head.  Then probe them with your finger and where you feel a pulse under your finger, mark it with ink.
Then take a scalpel, a surgical knife, known as the *nishal*, and insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards and sever the vein with the skin in a complete cut, the length of the cut being about two clasped fingers.
Or something similar, and release the amount of blood you desire. Then tie them with bandages and leave them until healed.  The cutting and cauterizing of them was discussed earlier in the book.  As for the arteries in the temples, their incision is beneficial for chronic migraine, severe headaches, persistent eye inflammation, and severe eye discharge.
The method of their incision, as I will describe to you: the patient tightens his neck with a turban until the veins become clearly visible, and their pulse is evident beneath your finger. At that point, mark them with ink. Then lift the skin over the vein upward with your index finger.
Insert the *nishal* scalpel from below, lift the vein upward, and sever it as you did with the other veins. Release the blood as needed.  Then loosen the patient's neck restraint, and place your finger on the vein for an hour.
Then apply a pledget and bandage, securing it tightly from above. Leave it until healed.  Their mention, incision, and release were discussed earlier in the book. As for the incision of the forehead vein, its benefit comes after the incision of the temporal vein, for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, sores, and persistent redness.
The method of its incision, as I describe to you: the patient tightens his neck with a turban until the vein appears.  Then, take the instrument called the *fāʾis*.  Here is its image:
Place the pointed prong at the head of the *fāʾis* on the same vein. Strike from above with a comb or similar object, allowing the blood to flow as desired.  Then loosen the patient's neck restraint and apply a bandage until healed.
A wide scalpel may be used, but it should not be as sharp as other scalpels, but rather slightly wide at the tip.  Incision should be made with a twisting motion because the bone is nearby; a thin scalpel may break if used.
As for the veins located at the temples, their incision is beneficial for eye ailments such as scabies, redness, and abscesses, and for facial diseases.  The method for incision is that the patient should tighten their neck with a turban, then you should incise them, standing over their head. The incision should be made with a slightly elongated twisting motion using a small, slightly wide scalpel.
If the area has little flesh, a thin scalpel may break.  Then drain the desired amount of blood, place a bandage over the incisions, and bandage them for one night.  Then unbandage them.  As for incision of the nasal vein, it is beneficial for high fevers.
Severe headaches and facial diseases, such as the red inflammation that appears on the nose, especially if chronic, are treated by having the patient tighten their neck.  Then, hold the patient's nose with your left hand and take a long, thin scalpel.
Insert it into the center of the nostril itself, between the nasal septum, in a straight line.  Because the vein isn't perceptible there, blood will immediately appear.  You should push the scalpel slightly and drain the desired amount of blood.  Then bandage it for a night; it will clot quickly.
As for the jugular veins, their incision is beneficial for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and melancholic diseases that appear on the surface of the skin, such as black vitiligo, scabies, bad ulcers, and cancerous growths. The method of incision is to bind the patient's neck under them with a bandage, and the practitioner should stand over the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair.  Then, the vein is incised lengthwise, with a fairly wide incision.  Then drain a moderate amount of blood, or as much as needed.  Then do the same to the other vein.  Finally, remove the bandage.
Hold the veins with a moderate grip to prevent suffocation.  Leave it until the next day; the wound will heal.  As for the veins of the cheeks, incision after incision of the veins of the forehead is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum decay, bad ulcers, cracked lips, and bad sores.
which are in and around the nose.  The method of incision is to have the patient sit before you and bind their neck with a turban.  Then turn their lips inward and look for the two veins, one to the right of the lip and the other to the left, recognizing them by their blackness.
Also, there are thin, black veins around them.  Cut these veins completely off. If it is unclear and you do not know which one, aim for the largest and most visible. Do the same for the veins in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, incision after incision of the veins of the forehead is beneficial for the constrictions that occur in the throat from sore throat and mouth diseases.  The method of incision is to have the patient sit before you facing the sun, raise their tongue, and look under the tongue for a vein on each side. The veins will be dark in color.
Incise them, but be careful not to cut them deeply, as there are arteries underneath.  Excessive cutting might cause bleeding from these arteries.  The three veins that are customarily opened in the elbow are opened in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, fennel-shaped lancet or an olive-shaped lancet, or by making a slit with a scalpel, which is the incision [and this is its image], Image of the wide, fennel-shaped lancet:.
It should be wide, as you see, to be suitable for opening the hollow, full, prominent, visible, thick veins containing thick, dark blood.  This is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:.
This lancet is narrower and has a thinner tip, suitable for opening thin veins containing thin, yellowish blood.  This is the image of the incision lancet:.
This incision lancet, which is suitable for slitting, comes in various widths and thicknesses, depending on the width or narrowness of the veins.  It can be used as a model for other tools.  It is well-known among craftsmen.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the ailments that are under the throat and neck, along the chest and abdomen.  The phlebotomist must be cautious when opening it, and should be careful not to pierce any artery beneath it; if he makes a mistake and plunges the lancet too far, he may cut that artery.
This causes a blood flow, therefore it should not be opened with a lancet used for punctures but instead slit with the incision lancet. If the basilic vein is not clearly visible, it should be avoided and another vein used, or a branch of it sought out.
Or, open the brachial artery, for it is clear and can be slit with the incision lancet, as we said. If you wish to open the basilic vein itself, you should feel the area before tightening the arm to find the pulse point, then mark it with ink.
Then, the arm is tied off, and the vein is slit with an oblique incision using the incision lancet, as we said.  Care should be taken that the blow is kept away from the artery.  Whenever you see inflation in the marked area upon tightening the bandage, that inflation is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If, upon phlebotomy, you see the blood spurting like a child's urine, and the blood is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood.  In that case, quickly place your finger on it for a long time, then remove it; if the bleeding stops, it often does so; then tighten the arm.
Leave it alone, and warn the patient against neglecting it. Let it be on a pillow and do not move it for days until it heals.  If the bleeding does not stop and you are not able to provide a cure at the time, sever the artery if it is visible, as its ends contract and the bleeding stops.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it, and use one half to tightly bind the vein with a bandage and splints until the next day.  If the bleeding stops, well and good; otherwise, treat it with the previously mentioned application of cauterizing devices to stop the bleeding.  Cutting off the bleeding is not usually difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the effectiveness of the bandage on the arm, be aware of this.  As for the *akhal* vein, the benefit of its phlebotomy is that it draws blood from the top of the head and the bottom of the body, because it is composed of a branch of the *basaliq* and a branch of the *qifal*, as we said.
The phlebotomist should be careful to place the vein on a pillow, for underneath it are nerves.  If the lancet penetrates too deep and strikes a nerve, numbness will result, difficult to resolve, and perhaps it will not heal at all.  These nerves are often perceptible to the touch.
If in some people it is hidden and delicate and not easily visible, then make your incision with a gentle tearing motion and avoid the nerves as best you can.  If the vein is between two nerves, then cut the vein lengthwise.  As for the *qifal* vein, the benefit of its phlebotomy is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and in this vein, especially, if you wish to perform phlebotomy, use the olive-shaped lancet or the broad, rue-shaped lancet, for it is the safest of all veins, as it has neither artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, when performing phlebotomy, you should avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek out the soft spot.  If the vein is not struck on the first attempt, it does no harm to repeat the phlebotomy several times. However, it may swell in some people if not drained on the first strike.
But that swelling does no harm.  As for the method of phlebotomy, its effects, and what should be done to correct them, first you should know that phlebotomy is used either to preserve health.
Or it may be used in illnesses.  The kind used to preserve health and maintain it, and to prevent illness, should be in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the *qifal*, *akhal*, and *basiliq*.  Phlebotomy should be performed in the early spring, if signs of fullness appear.
Phlebotomy should be performed on a Sunday or Tuesday, after three hours of daylight have passed.  As for phlebotomy used for illness, it has no fixed time, but when need and necessity require it, whether night or day.
At any hour and at any time.  Boys should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should old men who have passed sixty years.  If anyone intends to perform phlebotomy for any reason...
He should cleanse his intestines prior to phlebotomy with a gentle enema if there is much retained phlegm, so that foul matter from the intestines is not drawn into the veins during phlebotomy and harms the main organs.  One who is feverish, intoxicated, or drunk should not be bled until these conditions pass.
Phlebotomy should also be avoided after dysentery, vomiting, fevers, excessive intercourse, exertion, exercise, staying up late, fasting, and any bodily or mental activity that weakens the body.  Then, one should consider thinning the humours if the blood is thick, before doing so.
With foods, drinks, and medicines, if possible.  Then he should take a bath, if nothing prevents him, or engage in some exercise, so that the blood will thin.  He should perform phlebotomy in the middle of the day, as we said, and strive to clear his mind of all bad psychological conditions that day.
Such as worries, anger, fear, and all bodily ailments such as excessive fatigue, exhaustion, intercourse, and the like.  Let him have in his presence the things that people commonly use, such as perfumes, herbs, and amusements, and the like, as each person can manage.
Then the phlebotomist should sit on a cushion higher than the one the patient sits on, then the phlebotomist should extend the arm and rub it with his hand two or three times, then tighten the bandage with the ligature and twist it two or three times.  The tightening should be moderate.
Because if the tightening is not moderate, either excessive tightening will prevent the flow of blood, or if it is too loose, it will also prevent the flow of blood.  Then, after tightening, the patient should rub his hands together.
Until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch.  Then the phlebotomist should wipe the lancet with a little old oil, especially.  Then he should place his left index finger on the same vein slightly below the place where he wants to make the incision so that the vein does not move and avoids striking it. Because some veins are like tendons, they move during phlebotomy, and some are filled with air.  When the lancet is placed on them, they recede under the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet will not open the vein.  If it does open the vein, it will only be a small opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be firm and careful in all these matters. Then he should lower the lancet. If the vein opens on that attempt, then he should do so. Otherwise, he should try again slightly below or above that spot.  If the area has not swelled, he should do so quickly.
If the patient swells or suffers a spasm, leave it for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage.  For it might bring about a hot swelling.  He should not enter the bathhouse.  Then he can repeat the phlebotomy if he wants. If the lancet penetrated and the opening was small and the flow of blood was thin...
And fear that not the desired amount of blood will be released.  Therefore, reinsert the lancet into the same hole gently in a straight line and slightly enlarge the opening. Do this quickly before the area swells, because in many people, the area may swell with a small opening.
If you see that it has swollen, do not return to it at all. It will not help you. Apply something from the clarified oil, for it facilitates the flow of blood. It is better in this place than the oil itself and other ointments.  Likewise.
Use clarified oil in all your venesections when the flow of blood is difficult.  The 'Faruq' remedy and 'Shukhznaya' may also do this.  If one of these is placed on the area, the blood will thin and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of the venesection (which often happens, especially for someone who has not been bled before, or if the vein opening was small), quickly apply a sponge soaked in warm salt water to the swelling and bind it for an hour.  It will dissolve.
This should be done after the blood has completely exited the vein itself or another vein. If, after a few days, some dark or greenish discoloration remains at the site, it will not harm. If you wish, apply something from the dissolved gentian and mastic.
Or something from the juice of the Foudjing and the like.  A swelling and protuberance often occurs when bleeding the Basilic vein. Place your hand upon it.  If you find it pulsates when you press on it, that is a bad protuberance. Be cautious about applying anything mentioned above to it.
For it may bleed arterial blood, but bandage it with something constricting to solidify the area, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.  One should draw blood from each person according to their strength and the prevailing color of their blood.
If the blood is dark, allow it to flow until it becomes red. Similarly, if it appears thick, let it flow until it becomes thin; and if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides.  This is appropriate for someone who is robust and strong and requires a single extraction of blood.
For someone strong, widen the vein incision, and make the lancet wide.  For the weak, do the opposite.  The blood should be extracted in stages, and the puncture should be narrow.  The best tool for vein incision is a slanted, oblique slit, not a piercing one.
This type of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and nerve damage.  It is better and safer than bloodletting across or lengthwise. If fainting occurs during bloodletting, the patient should be given something to eat beforehand, such as bread soaked in pomegranate juice or vinegar.
If the patient is free of fever and the blood is extracted in three or four stages, and if the patient is cold-natured, then they should take bread soaked in myrrh drink, or honey drink perfumed with fragrant herbs, or aromatic herbal drink, before bloodletting.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting, and the cause is excessive blood loss, then the patient should be given meat broth and a thin herbal drink.  Perfume with rosewater should be used, and the chest should be rinsed with it.  All other treatments mentioned in the section on fainting should be employed.
This is from vomiting.  As for someone who wishes to relieve their arm and release their blood again, if the bloodletting was for excessive vomiting and the strength is weak, the blood should be released little by little, according to strength, over several days.
For someone who wants to relieve their arm and release their blood again, and whose body is strong, let them do so after seven or nine hours of the initial bloodletting.  And for someone who wants to draw blood from their body to the opposite side where it tended, let them be relieved on the second or third day.
If a person has a lot of blood in their body, it has become hot and inflamed, and caused a fever, then blood should be extracted in a single, large quantity, and the opening should be widened until fainting occurs, after having checked all the conditions of bloodletting. Place your hand on their pulse while the blood is flowing, to prevent death from occurring instead of fainting.  This often happens if the bloodletter is unaware and carelessness occurs.  This should not be done if you wish to loosen the arm and release the blood again.
If the vein opening closes and blood flow is difficult, do not press hard or twist forcefully.  That is very bad.  Instead, either leave it until it can be opened again, or use the scalpel blade to remove the clotted blood from the vein opening.
Or apply something dissolved in water, perhaps some antidote, or some other substance, and gently prod it until the blood flows. If the vein is swollen, leave it alone.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If, however, further bloodletting is absolutely necessary, either open a new vein above the affected spot, or open a vein in the other arm or elsewhere.
As for bloodletting from the arm, it is done in place of bloodletting from the eye or temple if those are unavailable or hidden, because the arm vein is made up of them.  To do this, the patient should put their hand in hot water until the forearm turns red and the vein is clearly visible.  Then, a bandage should be tied around it with a medium tightness.
Then, open the vein slightly off-center, neither lengthwise nor across, and make the opening wide.  Cut the vein slightly above the wrist joint. If the blood does not flow, put the arm back into the warm water container and let the blood flow into the water.
Until you have reached your desired amount.  If it is summer, you may not need to return the arm to warm water.  Most often, the arm is placed in warm water during winter.  This vein is the safest of all veins for bloodletting. Because there is no vein or nerve directly beneath it.  As for bloodletting from the right-hand's ulnar vein, it is beneficial for liver ailments.  To do this, tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand after placing the arm in warm water until the vein swells.
Because there is no vein or nerve directly beneath it.  As for bloodletting from the right-hand's ulnar vein, it is beneficial for liver ailments.  To do this, tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand after placing the arm in warm water until the vein swells. It will be very apparent to the touch, then you should make the opening slightly off-center.  Even if you cut it completely, that will not harm him.  Be careful; do not use the lancet too forcefully, because underneath is the nerve of the fingers, and the area is exposed with little flesh.  Then, return the arm to warm water.
And leave the blood to flow. If you do not return it to warm water, the blood will clot in the opening of the vein and cease flowing.  When you have extracted the needed amount of blood, place a mixture of ointment and salt on the vein to prevent it from closing up quickly.
The same should be done with every narrow vein.  As for the benefits of bloodletting from the left hand, it is beneficial for spleen ailments.  The procedure for bloodletting from this vein is the same as for the second, or ulnar, vein.  Bloodletting from the saphenous vein is beneficial for ailments in the lower body, such as ailments of the uterus.
And for menstrual retention, kidney ailments, chronic sores on the thighs and legs, and similar illnesses.  The method for bloodletting is to place the leg in warm water and apply massage until the veins are dilated.  Then, tie above the joint of the leg with a bandage, and the vein is located near the heel, visible near the big toe.
Many branches extend from it in the direction of the leg.  Bloodletting should be performed in the widest branch, or at the heel where it joins, as this is the safest and best option.  If you bleed in the part of the leg facing outward, be cautious of the nerves beneath the surface of the leg.
Make your incision with a twisting motion, as if you were about to amputate, and use a sharp lancet. If the blood is difficult to extract, return the leg to warm water and allow the blood to flow until it is depleted. If the phlebotomist misses the vein on the first attempt.
If the phlebotomist misses the vein, re-approach the incision slightly higher.  The area is safe, if the nerve is avoided as we said.  The same goes for the saphenous vein of the other leg. As for the femoral vein, its location is, as we said, at the heel on the outer side.
Its usefulness for hip pain, if it arises from hot blood, is as follows:  Have the afflicted person enter a warm bath and quickly apply firm pressure with four fingers on the leg from the hip up to the heel with a long, thin bandage. It will only show itself in this manner.
If it appears, bleed it in any way; either by twisting (which is preferable), or by cutting off a portion, or by making a slit.  The area is safe.  It is very hidden in most people, if you do not find it.
If it does not appear at all, then bleed some of its branches, which appear on the back of the foot near the little and ring fingers.  Be mindful of the nerves, and draw off the amount of blood desired.
Then, release the pressure, place a cotton pad on the incision site, and bandage the area.  It will quickly heal.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On cupping and its use.
Cupping vessels can be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass.  Cupping can be done in two ways: one by applying a ligature and drawing blood, and the other without a ligature.
And this cupping without a ligature is of two types.
And the cupping of the collarbones, which are the sides of the neck on both sides, and the cupping of the chin, which is below the lower jaw of the mouth, and the cupping of the shoulders, and the cupping of the coccyx on the tailbone, and the cupping of the elbows, which are the middle of the forearms, and the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the delicate veins spread throughout the flesh.  Therefore, it does not weaken the body like bloodletting, and cupping, whether with or without fire, should not be used in any illness arising from fullness until the entire body is emptied.
If cupping is needed due to illness or custom, we use it at all times, at the beginning, end, and middle of the month, and at any time.
For some people, when blood increases to the point where cupping is needed, they experience heaviness and headaches in their head.  Others experience fullness, redness in their face, head, and neck. Some experience itching on their face, temples, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.  And some itch the site of the cupping.
Some laugh excessively, some taste blood in their mouths, gums bleed, and they spit up blood. Some sleep excessively, and some see blood, redness, slain people, and wounds, and the like in their dreams.  When we see any of this...
Especially if it occurs in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed of the day.  The benefit of cupping the temple is for heaviness in the head and water dripping into the eyes.
However, this should be done after the body has been emptied.  This cupping may be a substitute for venesection of the temples.  One should avoid using it if they have a cold head or a cold, as it will cause them great harm.
Therefore, elderly people and those with cold illnesses in the head should not use it.  And those who frequently use it develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should lower the cup slightly to avoid the development of forgetfulness.  As for cupping the shoulder, it is a substitute for venesection of the temples and venesection of the sides of the head.
Therefore, it is helpful for asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory apparatus, coughing, and fullness.  The shoulder cupping should be raised slightly, because if it is placed too low, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it is helpful for headaches, eye diseases, migraine, choking, toothache at the roots of the teeth.  It is a substitute for venesection of the sides of the head.  The cupper should be instructed not to insert the instrument too deeply into the skin lest they cut an artery and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it is helpful for mouth ulcers, gum disease, and similar mouth ailments.  It can take the place of venesection of the corners of the lips.  As for cupping the shoulders, it is helpful for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
As for cupping the hollows of the forearms, it is helpful for what venesection of the three veins—the basilic, the temporal, and the cephalic—is helpful, because it draws blood from all those thin veins in the flesh and draws those thin veins from other, thicker veins, until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to press the instrument too forcefully, for the area is lacking in flesh, and has nerves and arteries underneath. As for a single cupping on the coccyx, it is helpful for piles and sores of the lower region.
The cupper should be instructed to use a large, brass cupping vessel, because the area needs strong suction, and glass cupping vessels may break.  The cupping should be performed thoroughly.  As for cupping the legs, it clearly reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, it induces menstruation, and is beneficial for boils and abscesses. It can replace venesection of the safens and saphenous veins, but it greatly exhausts the body, and fainting occurs in most people.  Cupping of the saphenous veins has a benefit similar to cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping is to first place the empty cupping vessel and apply moderate suction, but do not prolong the application of cupping; rather place and remove it quickly, to allow the humors to flow evenly to the location.  Continue this process, until the area becomes red and open.
When the redness of the blood appears, then the cupping should be applied gently and repeatedly. Then, the condition of the bodies should be observed; those with thin, porous flesh should be cupped only once to prevent the area from becoming ulcerated.
The cupper should widen the cupping, deepen it slightly, and apply gentle, light suction. If the blood is thick, the cupping should be performed twice. The first time, to open a passage for the thin, watery blood, and the second, to remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very thick, the cupping should be repeated a third time to achieve the goal.  In general, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, one incision is sufficient.  If we want to remove a large amount, we make more incisions, if we judge the blood to be thick.
A deep incision should be made, the moderate depth being only the skin.  What ointments should be used for cupping, and what waters, and what should the cupper avoid?  For those with thick, hard, dry skin and narrow pores...
The areas for cupping should be smeared with softening, dissolving, opening ointments.  In summer, for example, the ointment of the *khairī* (a type of fragrant wood resin), or the ointment of violet, or sweet almond, or pumpkin seed ointment.
In winter, ointments like narcissus, lily, chamomile, or hyacinth, and similar ones.  If the blood is thick and cold, then use ointments like marjoram, anise, benzoin, or rue, and similar ones.
If the cupper has wide pores and soft flesh, then the ointment should be avoided.  These people should wash their cupping vessels after the cupping with rose water, or cold water, or elderberry water, or gourd water, or watercress water, and similar ones.  As for those whose blood is excessively moist...
They should wash their cupping vessels with vinegar, or rue and sumac water, and similar ones.  As for those whose blood is thick, they should wash their cupping vessels with old wine, or marjoram water, or rue or chamomile decoction, and similar ones.  Cupping should be avoided in the bath and after the bath.
Instead, it should be used an hour or two after leaving the bath.  One should not sleep after cupping.  This is what the cupper and the blood-letter should consider before and after cupping.
First, one must consider if the cupper or blood-letter is bilious, and their blood is predominantly sharp and inflammatory.  Then they should take cooling things like pomegranate, dandelion in vinegar, endive, vinegar and rose water, and similar things.  Their foods should be fowl, lamb, lean meat, and similar things.
If their temperament is cold, they should be given honeyed drinks, or pomegranate molasses, or spiced vinegar, and they should drink moderate aromatic wine, somewhere between old and new.  They should be given little food, and their diet should consist of poultry, quails, sparrows, and doves, lean and tender.  The drink on the day of cupping and blood-letting should be more than the food.
Sometimes, some people should be given a restorative antidote or musk medicine or shilah medicine before cupping and before or after blood-letting to strengthen the major organs and thin the blood.  This should not be given to the feverish.
As for cupping without stipulations, these are cuppings placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and hip joints, because these organs cannot tolerate a stipulation.  The purpose is either to draw blood from one organ to another.
For example, placing a cupping on the breasts in cases of catarrh, or we use it to remove cold wind that may have accumulated in an organ, like placing a cupping on the abdomen and navel; it loosens the organ, warms it, and removes the pain by dissolving that wind.
It can also be placed on the kidneys if a blockage or stones appear there; by the strength of its suction, it may open the blockage or remove the stones from their place.  Likewise, it does this when placed on the liver and spleen in cases of wind that afflicts them.  These cuppings can also be used empty, by suction alone.
It can be used with fire, or filled with lukewarm water in cases of gout.  This is done by filling a large cupping with hot water alone, or water in which certain herbs suitable for this have been boiled.
Then, filled, it is placed on the affected area, held, and removed, repeated as needed. This is the form of cupping that is used with fire.
Its opening is the size of two open fingers, as we have depicted, and its depth is half a span.  On one side, about halfway down, there is a small hole to fit a needle, made from thick-rimmed Chinese or brass.
Smooth, level, and polished so as not to harm the organ when placed on it, and a transverse notch of brass or iron is in the middle, where the candle is placed over fire.  This cupping can be made larger or smaller than described, depending on the illness.
The user's skill is important; cupping for children and thin people is not the same as for men and those with large frames.  As for how to apply this cupping with fire to the affected area, a wick of tightly wound linen or a small pitch candle is lit and placed on the hardened rod in the center of the cupping.
So that the fire ascends from the top towards the bottom of the cupping, to avoid burning the patient's body. Then it is placed on the affected area, and the finger is placed on the hole we mentioned, so that when we hold the cupping, we do not need to remove the finger.
Steam will emerge from that hole and the cupping will be released on the spot.  Then the wick is rekindled as needed.  However, a cupping used for dropsy does not have a hardened rod or a hole.
Instead, it is used by filling it with water and placing it directly on the affected area.  This is its depiction:
The larger the cupping, the more water it can hold, the better it is.
Chapter Ninety-seven.
On the application of leeches.
Leeches are used mostly on parts of the body where cupping cannot be applied, either because they are small, such as the lip, gums, and the like, or because the area is bare of flesh, like the finger and nose, and so on.
To use them, select leeches from clear, uncontaminated freshwater.  Then, leave them in fresh water for a day and night to make them hungry and empty their stomachs. Then, the body is first bled, or cupped, and then the affected area is cleansed.
Until it turns red, then a leech is placed on it.  If it fills up and falls off, this is effective.  If possible, cupping the area is more beneficial; otherwise, wash the area with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and massage and squeeze. If bleeding persists after the leech has fallen off, and it is a seepage, then soak a linen cloth in cold water.
Place it over the area until the seepage stops. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered alum or dried dates, or similar astringents, on the area to stop the bleeding, or place peeled halves of broad beans on the spot.
Leave it until the broad beans stick to the area; then the bleeding will stop.  If further leeches are needed, and other leeches are available, do not use the same ones. If the leeches refuse to attach, the area should be cleaned with fresh blood.
Or, pierce the area with a needle until some blood comes out, then place a leech. If it feels blood, it will attach to the spot.  If you want to remove it, sprinkle some sorrel, salt, or ash on it, and it will detach from the spot.
Chapter Three.
On Fractures.
This chapter also deals with the necessary aspects of medical practice concerning the setting of fractures and dislocations of bones.
Know, my children, that many ignorant physicians and lay people may claim expertise in this area, those who have never examined the writings of the ancients or read a single word from them.
For this reason, this art of medicine has become extinct in our land, and I have never encountered a single proficient practitioner in it. My knowledge of it was gained through extensive study of the writings of the ancients and my diligent efforts to understand them, from which I extracted this knowledge.  Then, I committed myself to practice and skill throughout my life.
I have presented to you in this chapter everything my knowledge encompasses. My experience has refined it for you, removing superfluous detail and condensing it to the utmost brevity, clarifying it to the greatest extent, and presenting numerous examples of the instruments used in it.
This is an addition to the clarity, as I did in the preceding two chapters. There is no power except from God, the Most High, the Great.
The first chapter.
This chapter contains general principles concerning the breaking of bones. Before we begin to discuss individual fractured and dislocated limbs, it is necessary to present some general observations and sections in the beginning of this chapter. This will first compel you to understand and grasp its reality, you and any others who are eager to learn this noble craft.
If a person suffers a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall, one must first apply poultices or laxatives, or both. Unless prevented by factors such as weakness, or if the person involved is a child or an elderly person, or if the weather is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, his diet should be limited to cold legumes, poultry, and venison.  Drinks, fatty meats, overeating, and any food that causes a surge of blood to the veins should be avoided, until swelling subsides and the outflow of fluid to the affected area is expected to cease.
Then, the patient should return to his usual regimen.  If the broken bone begins to knit, the patient should eat substantial, thick, and viscous foods such as hulled barley, rice, heads of barley, and the stomachs of oxen. Eggs, soft fish, and thick broths, and similar foods, will promote a quicker and better healing of the fracture.  Know that broken bones in strong men and the elderly will not always heal completely.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, bones that are exceptionally pliable, like those of young children, may fuse.  However, nature forms a substance around the broken bone, resembling glue, with sufficient thickness to hold it together and secure the fragments.
So that it attains the same strength and stability as before, and nothing impedes its function.
Therefore, the patient's diet should include foods with strength, viscosity, and thickness, as mentioned.  Know that fractures vary depending on the affected body part, for a fracture of the leg differs from a fracture of the head, and a fracture of the chest differs from a fracture of the back.
Likewise, all other parts of the body differ from each other. We will describe each type of fracture in detail in its own section, and the type of bone fracture can also vary within itself.
Because it may be a fractured break without splinters, or it may be a break along the bone, resulting in splinters and detached or non-detached fragments.  The fracture may accompany a wound or tear in the skin, or be a slight crack.
Each type requires a specific method of reduction, which will be detailed later in its appropriate place.  Signs of a broken bone include its deformity, unevenness, visibility to the touch, and the creaking sound when you press it with your hand.
If there is no visible deformity, no creaking, no feeling of disturbance when touching the bone, and the patient experiences little pain, then there is no fracture, but rather a strain, a minor fracture, or a minor crack.  Under these circumstances, the bone should not be moved by pulling or probing at all.
Instead, apply the appropriate medications, which will be described later, to the affected area and gently bind it.  Know that when a bone is fractured and splintered, but no fragments are created, each part may have moved away from its original position.
You should promptly realign and straighten it before a hot swelling develops.  If a hot swelling does appear, leave it for several days until it subsides, then realign it by any method of gentle manipulation that you can employ.
Know that reduction and realignment are easier when there are no fragments in the bone.  Then apply the appropriate bandage, as will be described later.  If there are fragments, the fractured limb (either an arm or a leg) must be stretched from both ends.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with both ropes and hands, position the limb on a flat surface in its natural shape.  Once the fractured bone parts have been stretched, reposition any protruding pieces in their proper places using any method of gentle manipulation.
Strive to avoid causing pain or discomfort to the patient with your actions.  Do your utmost to join the two bone parts together in the best possible alignment.  At that time, you should touch and feel them with your hand.  If you find anything abnormal, correct and realign it to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive stretching and forceful prodding, as many ignorant people do.  Often, their actions cause a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb, as I have witnessed repeatedly.
After the realignment, careful adjustment, and stretching, keep the limb still and at rest. Warn the patient not to move it during wakefulness, sleep, turning over, or restlessness, during bowel movements, and with all his movements as much as possible.  He should ensure that the position of the limb is one that prevents pain.
If, in that position, the patient feels pain or discomfort in the limb, he should move it to a position where he does not feel pain.  Furthermore, he must ensure that this new position is straight and aligned.
So that no deformity occurs in the limb if it is straightened.  Regarding the method of stretching a fractured limb, as I describe to you, know that fractured limbs differ in size, degree of injury, and characteristics.  Those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and similar parts, ...
The bandages should be soft and gentle for small parts like the arm and fingers.  For larger parts like the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be firm and strong, because a broad bandage is necessary for a large limb, stretching it equally from all sides without any flaws.
Then, after completing the alignment, apply the appropriate plaster. With a soft, gentle pressure, begin by wrapping the bandage around the fracture site three or four times, depending on the limb's requirements.  Slightly tighten the bandage, then move it to the upper part of the fracture.
With less tension than you used on the fractured area, gradually move the wrapping away from the fracture site, loosening the tension slightly until it aligns correctly.  Then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area as well.
Wrap again, then move the wrapping towards the lower part of the fracture.  Maintain the tension and looseness as described in the first wrap, above.  Place soft pads or cloths between the layers to correct any misalignment of the fracture.
If there is misalignment, correct it; otherwise, do not add anything.  Then wrap with another bandage.  Finally, apply firm splints over these layers, if the limb shows no swelling or inflammation.
If there is swelling or inflammation, apply something to reduce the swelling and inflammation. Leave it for several days, then apply splints.  The splints may be made from cut and prepared pieces of flat reeds, or from ebony wood.
Made from pine, palm leaves, reeds, or similar readily available materials.  Maintain the same dimensions and shape for each splint.
However, the splint directly over the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others.  The splint's length should be appropriate to the size of the fractured limb.  Image of the splint:
Then, tighten the splints with another bandage, using the same level of tension as the first.  Secure with strong threads from above, ensuring that the tension over the fracture site is greater. The less the tension as you move away from the fracture.
The threads should be of medium thickness and softness, preferably made from damp linen.  If the threads are thick, like those made by ignorant people from twisted linen strips, this is a serious error.
Because the tension will be unbalanced with those threads.  Thin threads are also unsuitable, as they will not achieve the desired tension.  There should be no less than a finger's width between the splints.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after applying the correct tension, place soft padding or fluffy wool underneath to prevent any discomfort.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and the skin is punctured, we will discuss this separately.
Know that not every fractured limb needs splints from the first day.  If the limb is large, splints should not be applied until five or seven days, or more, depending on your assurance that inflammation will not occur.
This is a description of the dressings used by the ancients to treat fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  It is a general, concise dressing that sets fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is not affected by heat or cold.
Take the flour dust, the residue from the grinding stone, knead it as it is, without sifting it with egg white.  Make the dough neither thick nor thin, then bake it.
Another dressing for fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take ten dirhams each of mastic, frankincense, acacia, raisin, and rose-water extract.  Take five dirhams each of myrrh and aloe, and twenty dirhams of acacia wood.  Use twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman clay. Grind all the ingredients together and mix with acacia water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot.  Then use this dressing.  It is well-suited, quickly sets broken bones, and is suitable for most people due to its balanced nature.
Another dressing useful for fractures and sprains: take ten dirhams each of rose-water extract, mastic, and white hellebore; five dirhams each of myrrh and aloe; six dirhams of acacia; and twenty dirhams of Armenian clay.  Grind all thoroughly, sift, and knead with water or egg white. Apply.
A dressing for dislocated joints and bones, relieving pain from throbbing and fractures: take softened wool, dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply to the affected area. This dressing does not have the power to set bones.
However, it is excellent for relieving hot swelling and pain.  Another dressing to set a broken bone: take prickly fig leaves and wild poppy leaves, grind them together, and apply them moist. Another concise dressing for setting a fractured bone.
To treat the remaining swelling, take equal parts of hellebore root, chamomile, violet, and chickpea flour. Grind all together and mix with paint if the limb is not inflamed; if it is inflamed, mix with coriander water or water and apply.
A stronger dressing for resolving the swelling that occurs after bone displacement: take equal parts of hellebore root, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, violet, and chamomile.
Grind all together and mix with vinegar water, fresh water, or paint, according to the heat of the limb and the reduction of its heat.  Hippocrates did not mention applying this to a fractured limb during the setting process in his writings.
Except for the ointment made from wax and oil, nothing else.  It should be of medium consistency, neither thick nor thin.  Galen believed that things with a drying effect and some heat, such as myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, should be applied to the fractured limb during the setting process.
A dressing for weakness and pain:  take equal parts of mugwort, chickpeas, shaved human hair (or bird feathers), hellebore, and salt.  Grind, sift, mix, and apply as a dressing.  The appropriate amount for the ligament should remain.
Then, consider the situation. If the patient does not experience pain, rubbing, or displacement of the fractured bone, do not remove the dressing for many days. If the affected area experiences severe rubbing, disturbing pain, or swelling, then remove the dressing.
Then, promptly remove the dressing immediately, do not delay.  Take a soft cloth or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area until the rubbing and pain subside. Then, allow the limb to rest for an hour.
Then, apply softened wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, and wrap it around the area overnight. This will help ease the hot swelling, reduce limb puffiness, and alleviate pain.  Then, apply gentle traction and a light dressing, but do not apply the initial level of tension.
Treat it gently until healed.  If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and puffiness, and all symptoms have completely disappeared, and a dressing and traction are needed, then reapply them as you did initially.  However, if nothing mentioned above has occurred on the limb.
Do not remove the dressing for three, four, five, or seven days.  Sometimes, twenty days may be needed.  All this depends on the condition of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture heals and the flesh begins to knit together.
Increase the traction then, beyond your initial level, and also increase the patient's diet, as previously mentioned. If you see that the fracture site has become excessively dry and emaciated, understand that the nourishment is not reaching it.
Unwrap it with lukewarm water each time you remove the dressing, every three days.  Slightly reduce the traction.  This will allow nourishment to reach the limb, and it will heal quickly.  As for what the unskilled among the bone setters do, of breaking the limb again...
For if it is not properly set at first, and is set crooked, that is a mistake.  A great deception.  Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it.  I have seen no trace of this among them. The correct approach is not to use it.
Chapter Two.
Concerning fractures occurring in the head.
Fractures of the head are numerous and varied in form, with diverse causes. Some fractures result from sword blows, and in these cases, the bone may break completely, reaching down to the peritoneum lying beneath the bone. As happens in wood when it is split.
And so, this type of fracture is called a splintering fracture.  Alternatively, the sword might only sever a portion of the bone, fracturing only one side, without penetrating the bone completely. This type of fracture is called a simple fracture, and the wound in either case may be large or small.
Other fractures are caused by crushing or contusion, resulting from a blow with a stone, a fall onto a stone, or similar. This type of fracture may either penetrate the membrane beneath the bone or affect only the surface of the bone.
The wound in these fractures may also be large or small. Some fractures are hidden within the bone, like a very slight crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture.  One such fracture occurs from a fall or a blow with a stone or similar, where a portion of the bone's surface is driven inwards, causing the area to become concave, just as a piece of copper would be dented if struck.
This is most common in the spongy skull, like those of children.  All these types of fractures may have detached or non-detached fragments.  We will describe the treatment for all of this in its proper place.  All these types of fractures can be identified through examination.
Inspecting them with probes and removing any corrupted flesh.  A hairline fracture is identified by examining the bone, rubbing and staining it with ink; the fracture will appear black.  Regarding fracture treatment, first consider the patient's symptoms.
If you observe clear symptoms of danger, such as vomiting bile, prostration, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, redness and bulging of the eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach or treat the patient, for in most cases, death is inevitable with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that are not alarming and hope for recovery, then proceed with treatment.  This is if the injured person comes to you at the earliest possible time after the injury, especially during winter.
In any case, you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day.  If it is during summer, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane beneath the bone is corrupted, leading to the symptoms we described.
If the fractured bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and is accompanied by fragmentation and contusion, you should cut away the fragmented and contused portion, as I describe to you: shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in any way possible, according to the shape of the wound and the patient's comfort. If, during the exposure of the bone, you see bleeding or a hot swelling, deal with it as necessary.
This means: pack the area with cloths soaked in rosewater and ointment, and cauterize the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding stops.  Then, proceed to manipulate and remove the bone, using one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a narrow, sharp incision.  This is its image/form.
Then, use another incision, slightly wider than the first.  This is its image/form.
Then, use yet another incision, wider still than the second.  You should have a set of various incisions, some wider than others, some shorter than others, and extremely sharp.  These should be made of Indian steel or good quality فولاد.
Use gentle force when striking the incision to avoid jarring the head and causing further injury.  If the bone is strong and firm, it should first be pierced around the area before using the incisions with instruments called *mithāqib ghayr ghā'isa* (non-dipping drills).
Because they do not penetrate the skull bone beyond its limits.  The drill has a rounded edge, below its sharp head, like a ring or small circle, preventing it from sinking into and passing through the thickness of the bone.
You should have many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of the bone.  For each skull, you need a drill with a sharp point whose length and shortness correspond to the thickness of that skull.  This is the image of three types. Of drills: large, medium, and small.
To drill around the fractured bone, place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated. Then, move the drill to another location, leaving a space between each hole equal to the thickness of a rod or similar.
Then, cut between each two holes using the chisels, with as much gentleness as possible, as we have stated.  This is done so you can remove the bone either with your hand or with some other tool that has been prepared for this purpose.
Like sponges and delicate pliers.  You must be extremely careful that the drill or chisel does not touch any of the peritoneum.  If you have smoothed the bone and separated it from any adhering peritoneum and removed it, then it must be cleaned.
Smooth any roughness remaining on the bone with another tool similar to the chisel, but it must be thinner and gentler than the other chisels.  If any small pieces of bone or splinters remain, remove them gently with the tools you have.
Then, treat the wound afterwards with the dressings and ointments that will be mentioned.  The other approach to this procedure is quite straightforward, free of risk, and was praised highly by Galen.  This is what he said. First, you should begin by exposing the portion of the bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent.  Once you have exposed that section, you should place the tip of this lentil-shaped chisel underneath it.  This is its image/shape.
The lentil-shaped part of it should be smooth, so as not to cut anything.  The sharp part of it, with its pointed ends extending lengthwise, as you see, should be positioned so that the lentil-shaped part rests on the peritoneum and the sharp edge of the chisel is against the bone. Then strike the chisel from one side.
With a small hammer, so that you cut through all the bone gently, as it turns, and you are safe from injuring the membrane; no frightful accident will occur, even if the practitioner is the most ignorant and timid of people, indeed, even if he were asleep.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some areas, scrape it off using the tip of the lentil-shaped chisel itself, gently removing it.  It will come off without harm or fear.
If the fracture of the bone did not reach the membrane, and the broken surface of the bone remained rough with small fragments, then you should remove those roughnesses and fragments using various small tools of different sizes, so that you may use the one most suitable for each area, according to what the work dictates.
The broken bone itself and its shape should be considered.  You should first use the widest of these tools to clean the bone, then use a narrower one after that, and continue this process, gradually working your way down to the use of the finest and most delicate ones.
As for the smaller, hairline fractures and slight breaks, each should be treated in a manner appropriate for its repair.  This is something obvious to anyone with even a basic understanding of this craft and who has grasped what we have written and explained regarding major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, you should take a linen cloth sized to the wound, soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound.  Then take another cloth, folded or triangular. Soak it in rosewater and rose oil, place it on the first cloth, and do this with as little weight as possible, so as not to burden the peritoneum.  Then apply a wide bandage over this, tightening it only enough to hold the wound in place.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two, until the hot swelling subsides.  Then remove it and use afterwards the drying remedies like iris roots, chamomile flour, coriander powder, and zira, and similar things.  Make a paste from these remedies.
Dust the wound with it, as it is dry.  Generally, use a remedy that cleanses without stinging.  Strive, during the treatment of the wound, to keep it clean, free from grease, dirt, and do not allow pus to collect in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the brain membrane, it corrupts and rots it, causing a great calamity for the patient.  This can occur in the lining of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If a dark discoloration appears on its surface, and if the patient shows the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he is doomed.  And if the discoloration was caused by a medicine applied to it, and if the strength of that medicine is such that it causes this discoloration.
You should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well, and apply them to the cloth, then place the cloth on the lining. Then treat it with the appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter Three.
In the setting of a broken nose.
Know that only the upper portions of the nose, either both or one, can fracture, because they are bones. The lower part is cartilage, and therefore does not fracture, but may be bruised, bent, or perforated.
If one side of the nose is fractured, you should insert the little finger into the nasal opening.  Straighten the fracture from the inside with your index finger and from the outside with your thumb, until the nose is restored to its natural shape.  Do this gently, ensuring you do not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is high up on the nose and cannot be reached by the finger, it should be straightened with a blunt, slightly thick instrument.  If the fracture is in both sides, do likewise.  The reduction should be performed on the first day after the fracture.
If possible, or else after seven or ten days, when the acute swelling has subsided, insert a linen thread into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two threads if it is on both sides.
The threads should be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some earlier bone setters say to soak the threads in ghee and change them daily.  I, however, do not think this is necessary.  The threads should be soaked in beaten egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the threads until the bone is set and the cartilage is solidified.  After the threads are in place, you may insert goose feather tubes wrapped in soft linen into the nose, to further hold the fractured nose in place and to prevent difficulty for the patient in breathing.
This is not essential; if you wish, do it, or if you wish, use the threads.  If a hot swelling develops on the nose during your treatment, bandage the nose with gauze or cotton soaked in vinegar, rose oil, or some kind of diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling appears, it is advisable to bandage the outside with semolina flour and powdered kandar mixed with egg white, then place a soft bandage on top. Do not bandage the nose at all if the nasal bones are fractured into small pieces.
Or if it splintered, you should cut through it and remove it with the appropriate instrument. Then stitch the wound and treat it with healing ointments suitable for the purpose. If a wound occurs inside the nose, you should treat it with threads and use lead tubes. Until it heals.
Fourth Chapter.
On setting the lower jaw if fractured.
If the lower jaw is fractured, and the fracture is not accompanied by a visible wound, if the fracture is only from the outside and did not break in two and has bent inwards, then its identification is easier.
If the fracture is in the right half, one should insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth.  Likewise, if the fracture is in the left half, insert the index finger of the right hand and gently lift the fractured prominence from inside to outside.
Your other hand, on the outside of the bone, controls its alignment.  If the jaw fracture has broken into two pieces, it is necessary to use traction from both sides in a straight line until its alignment is achieved. If it has affected the teeth, they should be treated.
Or if any part is expected to separate, tie it with a gold, silver, or silk thread.  Then, place a bandage on the fractured jaw, followed by a folded cloth.  Put a large, tight bandage or a piece of leather, the same length as the jaw, over the cloth. Then, tie it from above in a way that suits its joining, ensuring it does not come undone.  Order the patient to rest and abstain from strenuous activity.  Give the patient soft foods. If you suspect a change in the fracture's condition in any way, proceed to address it on the third day.
Then, tie it from above in a way that suits its joining, ensuring it does not come undone.  Order the patient to rest and abstain from strenuous activity.  Give the patient soft foods. If you suspect a change in the fracture's condition in any way, proceed to address it on the third day. Then, repair the bandage with flour from a millstone mixed with egg white or semolina, after removing the bandage. Place a soft padding over the bandage.  As long as the bandage adheres and the bone condition does not change, leave it in place.
Do not remove it until the fracture heals and strengthens.  This fracture often strengthens in three weeks.  If a hot swelling appears during that time, use the remedies we have previously mentioned to calm it until it subsides.
If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, examine it. If a piece or pieces of bone have separated, gently remove them with the appropriate instruments. If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
Then, if you have removed all the bone fragments, close the wound opening if it is wide, or apply one of the appropriate ointments if it is not. And heal the wound until it is healed.
Chapter Five.
On setting a fractured clavicle.
The clavicle is most often fractured in front, from the acromial end.  Its fracture can occur in one of three ways: either it fractures and splinters into two pieces without causing splintering, which is easiest to reduce;
Or the fracture may involve splintering, which is more difficult to reduce; or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound.  If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants; one should hold the arm next to the fractured clavicle, and the other should stretch the neck towards the opposite side.
Then, straighten the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correct and there are no protrusions or irregularities. If you need more traction, place a ball of linen or wool under the patient's armpit.
Use as much traction as needed, stretching and raising the clavicle, pressing the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned. If you cannot draw the clavicle outward because it has become recessed, the patient should lie on their back.
A moderate-sized pillow is placed under his shoulder, and the assistant presses down on the shoulder to raise the recessed clavicle.  Then you should correct and straighten the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a piece of the clavicle has fractured and is moving, you must carefully separate and remove that piece. If the fragment is lodged in the bone, use one of the specialized instruments to remove it. After placing the instrument that supports the diaphragm (it is made of wood or iron), which is depicted as follows:
It resembles a spoon without a curve, its width adjusted to the size of the bone, and its length to the work needed. It should have two ends; one wider and the other narrower, as you see.
If the wound you made to remove the bone fragment is wide and inflammation is present, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures. If the wound is minor, or if you fear severe inflammation, treat the wound with a superficial incision and splints, corresponding to the size of the wound opening.
If there is a hot inflammation, apply the dressing soaked in rose oil and vinegar or potion.  If the bone is dislocated without a wound or incision, apply a dressing made of mill dust mixed with egg white to the bone. Place a soft splint on it, then place a ball under his armpit if needed.  Then take a very long turban, about a span wide or so, and place a folded splint over the splint and dressing.  Then take a splint made of thin board. Its width should be three fingers, and its length the same. Then wrap it in a cloth, and wrap that cloth with the splint in the part of the turban that lies over the fracture site. Then tighten the turban around the fracture as you would tie a bandage. Hold it around his neck and under his healthy armpit and under his injured armpit, returning it repeatedly to each side.  How you see that the tension ensures a firm hold on the fracture is something you understand.
The whole point is that the splint should not move from the fractured bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient daily.  Whenever the ligaments loosen and you see the splint has shifted, correct this and tighten the bandage. Then, have the patient sleep on their back, and place a small pillow under their armpit at night.
So that his arm is raised from his side, causing the clavicle fracture to rise with the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck.  Do not loosen the bandage unless a wound or swelling occurs at the site within twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. Maintain the pressure and leave it until the clavicle fracture is set and firm, and it strengthens further in twenty-eight days. It may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six.
In the setting of a fractured shoulder.  The shoulder rarely fractures in its broad region, but rather in its processes.  If it fractures in one of these regions or in the middle, this is determined by touch.  Then, based on the nature of the fracture, apply a splint.
Reduce it and restore it to its natural shape by every means possible, then apply mill dust with egg white and soft plaster.  Place a folded cloth over this, and then apply a broad splint made of wood.
Make it as wide as the entire shoulder, or slightly wider.  If there is a depression under the splint in any part of the shoulder, smooth out that depression with soft plaster until the splint lies flat.  Then, secure it from above with a long bandage.
Apply a firm bandage and secure the splint so that it does not shift, and check the bandage daily.  Whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it moves out of place. The patient should lie on their healthy side, and the shoulder will be healed in twenty days.
One day or twenty-five days.  When this period is completed, remove the bandage and you will be safe, for it is one of the bones that one does not fear fracturing or dislocating.  If a piece of bone protrudes and is piercing the skin, lance it and remove it. Do what was mentioned for a broken collarbone in relieving inflammation if anything of that kind occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On the setting of fractured ribs.
The chest can fracture in the middle, though this is infrequent.  However, its ends are more often broken and fragmented.  If the middle is fractured, the symptoms include a downward inclination, severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
Sometimes blood may be expelled, and the depression in the fractured bone will be evident and palpable.  To set it, the patient should lie on their back with a pillow between their shoulders.  Then, the patient's sides should be pressed together, and the ribs brought together manually from the sides, carefully aligning them in all directions possible.
And hold it until the bone returns to its proper shape.  Then apply bandages and splints, placing a thin board of poplar, willow, or similar lightweight wood on top, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then gently secure the bandages around the fractured bone so that they do not slip.  Wind the bandage around the back in a circular motion repeatedly, tightening it firmly.  Then inspect the bandage frequently and tighten it whenever it loosens.
If the need arises to remove it due to inflammation, pain, or swelling in the area, promptly remove it, and address the issue with the appropriate treatment.  Then, if appropriate, reapply the bandage. And maintain the pressure until healed.
Chapter Eight.
Concerning the setting of fractured ribs, know that fractures occur in the thick parts of the ribs adjacent to the back, and their front ends.  Injuries occur because they are cartilaginous.  This is easily recognizable by touch when examining with the fingers.
Set it by aligning the fracture with the fingers in the best possible position until the shape is correct. Then bandage and support the broken bone with a splint if necessary. If the fracture of the ribs is angled inward, the patient will experience intense pain.
And the pain is like the pain that occurs in those with a hernia, because the bone pierces the diaphragm and also causes shortness of breath, coughing, and a large amount of blood spitting.  This is a difficult condition to treat.  The ancients devised many remedies for it.
Some have said that the patient's food should produce flatulence and wind, so that the abdomen swells and the fracture is pushed outward.  We dislike this, lest it aggravate the occurrence of inflammatory swelling. If it has not occurred, it will not cause it; but if it has already happened, it will worsen and confirm it.
Some said that a cupping device should be placed on the area and then vigorously suctioned, which is more in line with reason.  However, one fears that the cupping device might draw the fluid from elsewhere due to the weakness in the area.  Some said that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
The space between the ribs becomes filled, so the ligaments become even if you wrap it around the circumference.  Then the patient is treated for hernia with diet and medication.  If the patient is exhausted by a severe condition, they cannot endure it. The bone was piercing the diaphragm painfully, and we feared for the patient. Therefore, it is necessary to cut open the area, expose the broken rib, and then place beneath it the instrument that protects the peritoneum, which was previously described, and carefully remove the bone.
Then, if the wound is large, we bring together the edges with sutures and treat it with ointments until it heals.  If inflammatory swelling develops during this time, immediately apply rose-oil ointment to the area and treat the patient internally with remedies that alleviate the swelling.
He should lie on the side where he sleeps more easily until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture occurs, this is rare.  Most often, the neck experiences contusions.  Similarly, the vertebrae of the back are also more prone to contusions than fractures.  If this happens to someone, and you wish to know if they will recover, then examine: if you see that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and lifeless, and he is unable to move, extend, or grasp them, and if you pinch or prick them with a needle, he feels no sensation or pain, then know that in most cases he will not recover; he is doomed.
But if he can move them and feel the pinching and pricking, then know that the spinal marrow has been spared, and the patient will recover with treatment.  If a similar condition affects the vertebrae of the back, and you want to know if he will recover,
then examine his legs.  If you see that they are relaxed and the same condition has occurred in them as in the hands, and then, if he lies on his back, wind and stool pass without his will, and if he lies on his stomach, urine passes without his will, and if he lies on his back and wishes to urinate, he cannot, then know that he is doomed; do not concern yourself with his treatment.  If none of this occurs, the matter is less serious.  Treatment for what has occurred is to attempt to relieve the inflammation.
by placing rose ointment alone or with roasted egg yolks on the affected vertebrae three times a day, until the inflammation subsides. Then apply one of the strengthening, dry bandages to the area and bind it with a bandage.  Instruct him to rest and remain still.
He should sleep only on the side that does not cause him pain until he is healed.  If a splinter or something of the sort has formed from the injury to the bone, it should be necessary to cut open the skin and remove the bone. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges together with stitches, and treat with healing ointments until he is healed. If the last bone of the coccyx, the tailbone, is broken, it is necessary to insert the little finger of the left hand into the buttocks.
Adjust the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible and achievable, then apply a bandage and splint if needed, and then bind it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, cut open the area and remove it, and treat the wound with the previous treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Fractures of the thigh bones are rare.  If they do fracture, the fracture is typically a splintering at the ends and a longitudinal splitting.  The bone will tilt inward.  The patient experiences pain, stabbing, and numbness in the affected leg.
To set it, run your hand over it to determine the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at the ends, adjust the ends of the fracture so that the bone resembles its normal shape.
If the fracture is longitudinal or has tilted inward, lay the patient on his stomach to allow the fracture to be set.  Once set, apply a bandage. Then apply a splint of wood or leather and bind tightly.
Do not fear the displacement of the fracture or the removal of the splint.  Fill the hollows around the fracture to provide a smooth surface for the splint.  Instruct the patient to sleep on his back or on his sound side.  If a hot swelling appears,
Refrain from manipulating or setting it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply whatever remedies were previously mentioned to alleviate the swelling.  Then return to setting and securing the fracture as necessary.  If fragments or splinters are visible in the bone, they should not be removed.
And do not touch [the splinters]; rather, adjust the alignment from the outside as we said before.  Maintain the binding until healing occurs.
The eleventh chapter.
On setting a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, set it by one of two methods. One method is to take a smooth, slightly curved, medium-thick stick, like this:
And tie two straps at its ends. Then, suspend it from a high place, and have the patient sit on a chair.  Then, place their fractured arm on the stick until their armpit is positioned on the center of the curve of the stick. Then hang a heavy object from above it.
Or a servant supports it downwards, then the physician straightens the fracture with their hands, so as to return it to its proper position. The other method is for the patient to lie on their back, and have their arm suspended from their neck with a strap.
Then instruct two servants, one to hold what is above the fracture in his hands, and the other to hold what is below it.  Each should pull in their direction. If you wish a stronger pull, tie a strap below the fracture and one above, and have each servant pull in their direction.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, the middle of the strap should be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, the strap should be in that position and also on the elbow itself.
Then, gently straighten the fracture without force until the fracture is aligned properly and firmly joined.  Then, tighten it if no heat rash appears.  If a heat rash appears, postpone tightening until the seventh day.  Apply softened wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then tighten it.  The method of tightening is to apply the bandage to the fracture, then wrap a fresh cloth around the bandage. Then bring the arm to the upper arm, and place the hand open on the shoulder.
