Hold the cloth and tightening to the upper arm and forearm so that the arm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you from doing so and there is no change in the fractured bone. If you fear a change in the bone, use splints.
This means that you should place a splint on the fracture itself, one that is wider and stronger than the other splints.  Keep a finger's width between each splint. The length of the splints should be according to the fracture, with an additional three fingers on each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tightening described in the first section.
This means your tightening should be stronger on the site of the fracture.
And the further from the fracture, the less the tightening should be. If you see the placement of the splints and tightening as we described during the setting of the limb, do as we say.
If you fear a hot swelling, leave the tightening and splints until the seventh day, as we said. Then check the ligaments every three days, so that irritation, puffiness, or the limb's inability to receive nourishment due to excessive tightening do not occur.  Correct all of that according to what we have mentioned.
If you are certain that none of this will happen, do not loosen the ligaments until many days later.  The patient should lie on their back, with their hand on their abdomen.  A padded, moderate-sized arm sling filled with wool should be placed under the elbow.
Examine the broken limb at all times, day and night, to ensure its shape does not shift or the ligaments relax. Correct all of this with your effort, and make the patient's diet as we previously stated, to be gentle at first, until the bone strengthens.
Then, the diet should be thickened.  It is the custom of the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days.  At that time, the ligaments should be loosened, and the patient should use baths and be treated with ointments appropriate for that, if the fracture is severe or comminuted.
Do not loosen the ligaments for fifty days, or even up to two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called radii.  One is small, and is the one next to the thumb, and the other is large, and is situated beneath the small one, at the bottom.  Sometimes only the larger radius is fractured, sometimes only the smaller one, and sometimes both are fractured.
If the upper small radius is fractured, its setting is easy and its healing is quicker. If the lower radius is fractured, the fracture is bad and its healing is difficult. The worst case is when both bones are fractured. If the fractured bone is the upper small radius...
Therefore, the physician should, when setting it, apply a gentle, moderate traction until it is aligned. If the large radius is fractured, the traction should be stronger; if both radii are fractured, the traction should be very strong.
When setting and pulling the hand, it should be placed extended on a cushion. The thumb of the hand should be positioned higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers. The patient should be seated upright.
The cushion should be at the same height as him, so that the patient does not have to strain.
Then, one attendant supports the arm from below, either with their hand or with a bandage, and another attendant supports it from above.  Then, the physician aligns the bone to the best possible form. If the fracture has splinters, they are reduced.
Reduce each fragment to its proper place. If a detached splinter is found that pierces the skin and you are unable to set it, incise over it and remove it, as described earlier. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, a separate section addresses this.
Treat this accordingly from that section.  If a hot swelling appears during the initial setting, apply a bandage soaked in rose oil and white wax ointment, which should be of medium thickness.  Apply the bandage gently, until the swelling subsides.
Remove the bandage and apply a dressing prepared from mill dust and egg white. Then, apply the splints. The splint directly on the fracture should be wider and stronger.  Know that the arm usually requires six splints.
The fracture may be in one ulna or in both ulnae.  Then, make the tension over the fracture site stronger and firmer.  As you adjust the tension upwards or downwards, gradually loosen the tension, as previously described in the beginning of the chapter.
Use soft, damp bandages to wrap the fracture, avoiding overly stiff ones.  The thread used for tightening should be linen, of a medium thickness as described previously.  Check the limb and ligaments after several days.
If a problem arises, such as irritation appearing on the limb, the limb should be bathed in warm water until the irritation subsides. Leave the limb unconstrained overnight for rest, then resume the tension.
If the tension has become too loose and the bone has shifted or similar, correct everything to the best of your ability. Also, if the limb is not receiving adequate nourishment due to excessive tension, slightly loosen the tension and leave it for several days so nourishment can reach the limb, then re-tighten.
If none of the previously mentioned problems occur, the splint should not be removed until approximately twenty days have passed.  Then, suspend the patient's arm from their neck, keeping the arm straight.  The patient should avoid strenuous movements and sleep on their back.
Know that this arm fracture may heal within thirty days, or thirty-two, or perhaps even twenty-eight days.  This all depends on the individual's constitution and strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of fractured hand and fingers.
The metacarpals of the hand and the finger bones rarely fracture, but rather often suffer contusions.  Therefore, if a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand...
The patient should sit with their legs crossed, and a chair should be placed in front of them.
Then, the patient places their hand outstretched on the chair. Then the attendant extends the fractured bones and the physician straightens them, until they are properly aligned.  Then, a bandage and splint should be applied, unless a hot swelling has developed.  Then, a splint should be placed over the affected area.
And it should be enclosed in a soft cloth. If the fracture is toward the palm of the hand, make a ball shape from cloth and instruct the patient to grasp it with their injured hand. Then, fasten it with a long cloth.
Let the splint be made of soft leather, so that the skin can heal while the entire hand is bent.  Secure it as needed. If the fracture is outward, then a splint should be placed over and another splint under the hand to maintain the open and upright position of the hand.
Then, the splint is secured, wrapping around the hand and tying the fingers together with a bandage. If a fracture is present in any of the fingers, if it is the thumb, it should be aligned correctly and secured with the hand. Or, if desired, a small, separate splint can be applied to maintain the alignment of the fracture.
Do not move. If the fracture is in other fingers, like the middle, index, ring or little finger, they should be aligned and secured to the adjacent healthy finger, or all of them tied together with a bandage, this is better, or a small, separate splint placed on them.
As we said for the thumb, and carefully examine the injured area during and after the reduction of the swelling and inflammation.  Address any further issues as they arise, as described.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the reduction of a fractured thigh.
The thighbone often fractures and is readily apparent due to its shifting forward and backward.  Reduction is accomplished by applying a bandage above the fracture and another below, with the patient lying on their face.
Then each attendant pulls the bandage in their direction, maintaining a level position if the fracture is in the middle of the bone.  However, if the fracture is near the hip, a soft bandage, either of wool or similar material, should be applied.
At the hip, towards the pubic area, the pull should be upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture; likewise, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee, to pull downwards. Then the physician should straighten the bone with both hands until it is restored to its natural shape.
The bone will heal well.  Then, the bandage and traction should be maintained, unless the limb develops a hot swelling. If such swelling occurs, leave it for a few days until the hot swelling subsides, then resume the treatment.
As for the traction, a wide, firm bandage should be wrapped around the fracture two or three times, leaving excess material.  Then, the leg should be wrapped so that the heel is at the top of the thigh. A long thread should be inserted between the thigh and the leg near the lower part of the knee, with the ends of the thread extending upward from both sides.
Then, wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.  Next, apply splints to the thigh, at the site of the fracture itself, and create a single splint for the shin bone.  Then, fill the gap between the thigh and leg with soft cloths to ensure even traction.
Then begin with strong traction from the middle of the fracture site, wrapping three or four times.  As the bandage moves further from the fracture site, the traction should become less forceful, gentler, and looser.
Then, take the ends of the thread you inserted between the thigh and the leg, and tie them to the adjacent splints above. Then, bring the thread ends down to the ankle, and use them to tie the ends of the splints on the other side as well.
So that the bandage does not shift from its place.  Then leave the traction in place as long as no sores, swelling, or inflammation develop in the limb. If any of these occur, promptly loosen the bandage and correct any issues as previously described.
If there is a sharp fragment of bone protruding, it should be smoothed if possible. Otherwise, it should be cut and removed, and the wound treated in the same manner as previously described, until healed.  Sometimes, this fracture of the thigh can be treated without the addition of splints to the leg.
As we mentioned regarding the arm and forearm,  treating it this way does not cause the patient to limp, but if you treat it alone without including the leg, the patient will always limp.
Know that the thigh is immobilized for fifty days, or slightly more or less, depending on variations in temperament and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On fractures of the kneecap.
The kneecap is rarely fractured, but often bruised. If a fracture does occur, it is either a crack or a fragmentation of its parts, and this may or may not be accompanied by a wound.
All of this is assessed by touch. The fracture is treated by bringing together the separated fragments with the fingers, to the degree that is possible through gentle and careful alignment, then applying a bandage, and a circular splint, if needed.
Further supporting bandages are applied to match the alignment, and all conditions described for other fractures, such as inflammation and similar issues, must be treated according to their requirements until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the treatment of a fractured leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the radius.  They are subject to the same types of fractures as the arm bones. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is the same.  If both bones are fractured, the leg turns in all directions. If the thinner bone is fractured, the leg turns forward.  If the thicker bone is fractured from below, this is evident to you.
Therefore, use extension and realignment, and bind the splints accordingly, whether the fracture is of one or both bones. However, if the fracture of the leg is severe with many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and exert care in its treatment.
In treating the leg, there is something more to be done than with the arm.  After aligning the splints and completing all your work, take two pieces of pine wood, used for smoothing rooms (or similar materials, such as palm fronds).
Choose pieces that are somewhat thick, not thin, and have a length equal to the leg, from the knee down.  Then wrap two layers of cloth around each piece lengthwise. Place one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let them extend from the knee to the foot.  Then tie the two pieces in three places: at the ends and the middle.  With this arrangement, the leg will not tilt left or right, and will be well supported.  A wooden gutter along the leg may also be used.
Place it (the wooden gutter) to prevent movement, and this is especially important with a fracture that has an associated wound.  Then examine the leg each day, carefully observing for swelling, puffiness, or any other abnormality. If any of this occurs, treat it accordingly until healing.  The leg bone will heal within thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
In the fracture of the bones of the leg and fingers.
The heel bone is not susceptible to fracture.  The bones of the leg may be fractured, and the fingers are rarely fractured, but are more often bruised.
If a fracture of the leg bones occurs, and you see the bones overlapping, have the patient stand on the ground with their foot upright as if walking, then place your foot on the protruding bones and press them down and level them.
Until they return to their proper positions, then take the bandage and splint, place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot, and tightly bind the foot with the cloth and whatever else you need.
After three or four days, release the bandage, and you will find the bones leveled, whether they were fractured or dislocated.  The procedure is as described.  If some fingers are fractured, manipulate and level them as described for the fingers of the hand.
Then, make a splint for the fractured finger, along the finger's length and slightly wider than it.  Place the board you described under the foot and secure it tightly.  If two, three, or more bones are fractured.
Make a splint for each finger, sized appropriately and placed within soft cloth. Secure the board to the bottom of the foot, ensuring each end of the board extends beyond the sole of the foot for a good fit.
You should not forget to treat all the fractures with the symptoms we have described, addressing each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
Fractures of the female pubic bone and the male penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, place her in a squatting position, then gently lean her back slightly, supporting her from behind.  Then the midwife should pack her vulva with cotton until it is full and round like a ball.
Then, the woman is moved, and her pelvis is raised slightly, little by little.  The cotton will then come out until it is like a ball at the opening of the vulva, and the fractured bone will return to its place.  Then, a pad is placed on her back.  When she wants to urinate, the cotton is gently removed so that she can urinate.
Then, it is returned to its original position, and the initial pad is replaced.  This is done for seven days or so, and the fracture will be mended.  If desired, a sheep's bladder may be taken, a tube of reed placed on its opening, and the entire bladder inserted into the vulva.
Then, blow forcefully into the tube so that the bladder inflates inside the vulva, and the fracture will return to its place. Then pack it with cotton and keep it as described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this method is used for its repair.
Its treatment is the same as described for the hip bone.  The correct approach to these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, is evident to you, especially if you have some experience and understand my book, for the fracture itself often gives you clues.
Follow the correct method in setting and tightening it.  As for the penis, if it is fractured, take the neck of a goose and insert the penis into it.  Then place a bandage of cloth over it, and bind it up. Leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On setting fractures of bones when accompanied by a wound.
For someone who has a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large like the femur or humerus, or similar, one should immediately perform bloodletting if the conditions for bloodletting, as previously explained, permit.
If the wound is bleeding, one should quickly cauterize it with powdered alum, if nothing else is available.  Then, set the fracture that very same day, and do not delay.
If no inflammatory swelling occurs, then if inflammatory swelling does occur, postpone setting the fracture until the ninth day, until the inflammatory swelling subsides.  Do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, for it will expose the patient to bad symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin, exposed, you should gently try to reduce and align it with your hands and a slight extension.  If you are unable to reduce and align it with your hands, reduce it with this instrument, which is made of iron, seven or eight fingers long. And its width should be proportionate to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should make three or four of them, as needed for treatment of each type of fracture, and let them be round, slightly thick, so as not to bend when struck during use.
And let them be sharp-ended, with a bend at one end, and thicker at the top, gradually tapering to the bottom, very thin.  This is its depiction/illustration:.
It is called, in Greek, "permos," meaning a small lever.  The sharp, curved end should be placed on the protruding end of the bone, and it should be pushed once.  Then, if the bone is returned and is somewhat aligned, the ends of the fracture should be aligned with one another.
If the fractured end is thin and the instrument does not grip it well, then the end of that bone should be cut so that the instrument can hold it. If it is not possible to return the bone as described, then cut it with a similar cut to the ones we mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws, as you can, then remove any remaining roughness and thin scales from the bone.  If, after returning the bone, the patient feels severe, harmful pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can return it to its natural position, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient.  Once the bone is completely set, dip a cloth in a dark, astringent liquid, especially if it is summer. Do not place a bandage or anything oily on the wound.
So that there is no putrefaction or decay, then use splints when you have finished setting the bone. Leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the dressings, with scissors, according to the size of the wound.
Beware, above all, of tightening the wound with the fracture; often, ignorant physicians do this, causing their patients either death, or eating away, or a cold.  Let your tightening be soft and relaxed, unlike the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or large, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and if he experiences distressing pain at the location, you should not apply splints.  Make dressings from strong cloths in the place of the splints and bandage with them.
If, after a day or two, you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth you placed on it with the liquid, and then use the dressings and ointments that we use to treat wounds, like the Four-Element ointment and the like.  You should also loosen the bandage.
Examine the wound every day, morning and evening, until it heals and is cured.  The limb should be positioned so that the pus drains easily downward.  If the wound takes many days to heal, and the pus does not stop, know that there are small fragments of bone.
You should probe the wound with a probe.  Remove and extract any fragments that are loose.  If any fragments are not loose, and are irritating the limb and causing pain, then cut them and remove them by any means possible.
If the wound develops a cold, or an ulcer, or another form of decay or putrefaction, you should treat each symptom with a similar treatment as described in the relevant section.
If a large, prominent bone such as the femur or humerus, or similar large bones, is fractured, you should not attempt to pull or remove it. Often, death results from such attempts. Leave it alone until it becomes infected, perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place to treat it; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the complications that arise after some fractures.
This complication often arises after the fracture has healed, especially if it is near the joints. It spoils the appearance of the limb and may prevent the limb from its normal function.
If the complication is fresh, use astringent medications such as aloes, frankincense, myrrh, henna, acacia, and similar ones, taking some or all of them and mixing them with a contracting liquid, or egg white, or vinegar.
Apply it to the complication with difficulty, and bind it firmly. Leave the binding undisturbed for many days, then loosen it and repeat the process until the complication disappears, God willing, or bind a firm lead plate over it.
Lead possesses a property that removes all that accumulates in the limbs. If the complication has hardened and become severe, and necessity dictates its removal, cut through it from above and remove the protruding excess or remove it with some instruments until it disappears. Treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In treating a fracture, if the limb is subsequently thin and unlike its original form after being set.
If a fractured bone is set, and the limb remains thin and weak afterward, this is due to many reasons. One reason is either due to the excessive loosening and binding of ligaments improperly, or due to excessive tightening of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb, or due to excessive and inappropriate poulticing, or due to excessive and inappropriate movements, or due to a lack of blood and weakness in the patient's body.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich their body so that blood increases in them, and to use baths, bring joy and happiness to them, and the like.  Then, apply asphalt to the limb to draw nourishment to it, or continue poulticing with lukewarm water. Until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of broken bones that are rejoined crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
When a limb that has been set after healing shows crookedness or a bulge in the broken bone, or a complication, and the appearance of the limb is thus marred. However, if the limb is not hindered from its natural function, it is not proper to accept the claim of those who claim to break the limb from the head, and many ignorant physicians and bone setters in our country used to do this.
This action is highly reprehensible and leads to great danger, and the least of its consequences is injury. But if the crookedness and complication are recent, it is necessary to apply water in which relaxing herbs such as mallow leaves, roots, and kings crown and the like have been boiled.
And apply relaxing dressings, such as well-made diachylon, or take the sap of the mallow root and mix it with chicken fat and sheared butter, and apply it as a dressing, or take ripe figs, grind them with pigeon dung, and similar remedies that are called resolving the inflammation.
And the complication may be resolved by constant gentle massage with the hands, and the limb is moved in every direction at all times. If the crookedness is old, severe, and hardened, and necessity dictates treatment with iron.
Then it is necessary to cut above it, release the connection of the bone, and cut off any excess complication or bone with the finest instruments, using care and gentleness in this process, and then treat the wound with what was previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is a dislocation of a joint from its place, hindering movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing the patient intense pain and suffering.  Therefore, when a person experiences a dislocated jaw, one should promptly attempt to reposition it.
Do not delay at all.  If delayed, the area will swell and become a tumor, making repositioning the jaw difficult.  Therefore, it should not be delayed, nor moved nor stretched while it is swollen, because the patient will often experience spasms and painful ailments.
However, if this occurs, one should first bleed the patient, then wait until the swelling subsides somewhat, then apply warm water and ointment to the affected area.  Then gently reposition it, and treat each limb as described in its appropriate section.
The chapters on the jaw are also arranged, as was done with fractures, from the top of the body downwards.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of the lower jaw.
The jaws rarely dislocate, and their displacement occurs in one of two ways: either they shift slightly from their positions, becoming somewhat relaxed; or they completely dislocate, becoming completely relaxed. To the chest, causing the patient's saliva to flow uncontrollably, unable to retain it, or to close his jaw, and his tongue to stammer while speaking. If the dislocation is slight, it will often return to its place quite easily.
However, if the dislocation is complete, one must quickly return it and not delay at all.  A servant should hold the patient's head, while the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
Or, if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, both thumbs are inserted; the other fingers of the hand are positioned on the outside to align it.  The patient is instructed to relax and open his jaw wide in all directions, while the physician aligns and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to return, especially if both jaws are dislocated, apply a hot water and medicated compress until their return is easier.  Do not delay their repositioning, as we stated before.  Once they have returned and the patient's mouth is closed and not relaxed, place bandages on them with a concoction made from wax, oil, and rose.
Then, tie gently with a loose bandage. The patient should sleep on their back, with their head propped up between two pillows, so that they do not move it to the right or left, nor attempt to chew anything.  Their food should be soft and easily swallowed.
Once the pain subsides and the jaw is healed, let the patient eat whatever they desire.  However, they should be gentle and avoid forcing their mouth open for eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is completely healed.
If the jaws, once dislocated, do not return to their proper places, difficulty in returning them often leads to fevers, constant headaches, and possibly abdominal discomfort or repeated vomiting. If you see this, know that it is a serious complication.
Often, death occurs within ten days of this condition.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
In repositioning the collarbone and shoulder joint.
The collarbone does not dislocate inward due to its connection to the chest.  It may dislocate outward, which can be felt.  To reduce it, the patient should lie on their back with their arms extended.
Then press the area firmly with your hand; it will return to place.  Afterwards, apply a bandage and splints, and secure them.  As for the part of the collarbone that connects to the shoulder, it rarely dislocates.  If it does, it should be repositioned and straightened as described above, to the extent possible.
Then apply a bandage, splints, and dressings, and instruct the patient to remain still and quiet until healed.  By this same treatment, the shoulder joint can be repositioned if it too is displaced.
Chapter Twenty-six.
Concerning the reduction of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder joint dislocates in three ways: one, it dislocates downward towards the armpit; two, it dislocates towards the chest; and three, it may dislocate upward, away from the shoulder, although this is rare.
It does not dislocate behind the shoulder or in front of the nerve.  It dislocates most often downward towards the armpit, especially in those with little muscle, as it quickly exits and quickly re-enters.
However, in those with substantial muscle, it is different; it exits with difficulty and re-enters with difficulty.  Sometimes, a person experiences an injury or fall, and the shoulder swells with a hot inflammation.  One might suspect dislocation, but it is necessary to investigate thoroughly to ascertain the situation's accuracy.
Then, in treatment, if the dislocated shoulder is downward toward the armpit, by comparing the dislocated shoulder to the healthy one, a noticeable difference is found.  The head of the dislocated shoulder is twisted, and beneath the armpit, upon palpation, the head of the shoulder feels like an egg.
The patient cannot raise their arm to their ear or perform any other movements. Similarly, if it dislocates toward the chest or upward, the difference is apparent upon touch and not hidden.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  To reduce it, have an attendant raise the arm upward. Then, place the thumbs of your hands under the patient's armpit and lift the joint forcefully upward into its proper place while the attendant raises and extends the arm upward.
Then lower it downward; it will return quickly. If it does not return as described, and the dislocation has lasted for many days, the patient should bathe in warm water and apply poultices that relax and soften, such as boiling the root of the hyacinth, fenugreek, and crown flower in water and using it.
Then the patient lies on their back, and a ball of wool of moderate firmness is placed under their armpit.  The physician places his heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand, extending it downwards.
Push the ball with your foot, and it will return to its place.  Alternatively, to achieve reduction, have a man taller than the patient stand on the side, placing his shoulder under the patient's armpit and lifting the armpit upward so the patient is suspended in the air. Another attendant pulls the patient's hand down toward their abdomen. If the patient is light, something else should be attached to weigh them down, as this will return the dislocation immediately.  The dislocation may also be reduced in another way.
Another method involves placing a long, round-headed piece of wood, like a mortar pestle (not too thick, not too thin), under the patient's armpit.  A soft cloth is placed on the head of the wood.  The patient stands along the length of the wood.
Then the patient extends their hand downward on the other side, and also extends their body forcefully on the other side.  The joint will quickly return to its place. If reduction is difficult with all the previously mentioned methods, use this treatment: take a piece of wood about two arms lengths long. Its width should be about four fingers, and its thickness about two fingers.  It should have a rounded head, to easily enter the armpit in this way.
Then a soft cloth is tied to the rounded head to prevent the wood from hurting the patient.  It is placed under the armpit, and the entire hand and arm are extended along the wood downwards. The wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and wrist.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbar, the bar being placed across.  The hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging on the other side. The joint will then enter its place. Once it is in place in any way possible, a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared dressing, made of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white, is applied over the entire shoulder, circling upward.  Then a firm bandage is applied under the armpit, and the dressing is wrapped with bandages over it, upward. The arm is suspended from the neck.
The hand should not be moved for seven days. The patient's food should be limited so that the limb strengthens, which will speed healing. Then, after seven or five days, the bandage is removed and the patient is encouraged to move the limb. If it remains stable and does not relax, then healing has occurred.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture or another cause, cauterization with the three types of cauterizing agents, as previously described in the chapter on cauterization, should be used. If all of this is done, and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint still does not hold,
If the bandage and its tightening are repeated multiple times, but the joint still does not hold and drops, relaxing, and the arm cannot be raised upward, know that the nerve in the shoulder joint's head is severed, stretched, or relaxed.  In that case, know that the joint will never hold in place.
Regarding the dislocated jaw, which is situated near the chest and breast, and is displaced backward, it should be repositioned by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to its place.  Use all other bandaging and treatment until it heals. If, after healing, there is tenderness in the area and slow movement,
The patient should use a bath frequently until the tenderness subsides and returns to its original state.
The twenty-seventh chapter.
On the treatment of a dislocated elbow.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also returns with difficulty.  It dislocates in all directions, particularly forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you, since it is under observation and can be felt, regardless of the manner in which it has dislocated.
When the dislocated elbow is compared to the healthy one, its dislocation becomes apparent through the concave nature of the joint.  The arm cannot be bent, nor can the shoulder be placed against it.  One must act promptly to relocate the dislocation before a hot swelling arises.
If a hot swelling arises, its relocation becomes difficult, and it may never heal, especially if the dislocation is backward. This is the worst type of dislocation, the most painful, and often results in death.
To relocate it, if it is possible to return, a servant should extend both arms and hands with the arm outstretched.  The physician's hands should be positioned above and below the elbow, pushing the joint back into place using the thumbs of both hands or the base of the palms.
If the dislocation is forward, the arm can be bent one time so that the palm strikes the shoulder, which is aligned with it.  If the dislocation does not return, apply a very strong and forceful extension by having two servants extend the arm.
Two servants should also hold the patient to prevent movement during the extension. Then the arm is rotated in all directions after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around it. If the physician is working on the joint with their hands, they should apply ointment to the area to facilitate easy dislocation of the joint.
Then the joint is forcefully pushed back into place. After its return, a bandage containing compression, drying agents, and egg white should be firmly applied. The arm should be suspended from the patient's neck and left in place for several days.
Then it is untied. If the joint is firmly positioned and the ligament has been released, leave it alone. If you see that the joint is not firm, reapply the bandage and the splint, and leave it in place for several days until it becomes firm.  Then, untie it. If it produces pain after the joint's return and release,
If movement is difficult, use warm baths, gentle massage, and manipulation until it becomes flexible, or place a thick, soft cushion on the joint, then bind it, and leave it in place for a day and a night. Then remove it and place the patient in a warm bath. Once he sweats, massage the joint gently.
Then repeat the application of the cushion one, two, and three times while in the bath until it becomes flexible.  If you prefer, apply damp, finely ground cow dung mixed with ghee, and bind it on. Repeat this several times; this will soften the joint and restore it to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, but it is important to quickly reduce it as soon as it dislocates, before inflammation or a hot swelling develops in the area.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board, and have an attendant extend the arm. The physician should place their hand on the prominence of the joint and push until it returns; however, it is necessary to examine whether the dislocation has moved to the underside of the hand.
If the dislocation is outward, the patient should place the outside of their hand on the board during the extension and reduction.  If the dislocation is outward, have the patient place the inside of their hand on the board so the physician's hand is over the same prominence of the joint.  If it returns immediately, then do so; otherwise, apply a bandage to compress the swelling.
Leave it alone and don't try again, as it cannot be borne and you cannot reduce it after days have passed, except that the joint will remain deformed and will not harm the patient except that the hand will be weakened and unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve is severed or bruised, and there is no remedy but to tie it up. Perhaps this will help, and perhaps it will not. After the wrist is successfully reduced, apply the bandage we described, then tighten it and leave it on for five days.
Then it is removed, and the hand is exercised.  If movement is difficult and some stiffness is present, soften it with heated water and massage repeatedly until it becomes flexible.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction.  If a finger dislocates towards the palm's surface or its underside, extend the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumb until it returns to its place. Then bandage the fingertip and support it towards the direction to which it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and stretch it until it is straight and upright that day. If it is nighttime, bandage it in the same manner.  Continue to unbandage it during the day and exercise its movement, and bandage it at night.  Do this for several days until it strengthens.
And do the same if it dislocates towards the underside of the hand, bandaging it towards that same direction.  Do your initial procedure until it heals.  Do the same if it dislocates in other directions.
Chapter Thirty.
In treating the dislocation of the spine.
When a dislocation occurs in the vertebrae of the back or neck, a complete dislocation, or if many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, for death swiftly approaches the afflicted.
And a sign of this is that the afflicted's stool is expelled involuntarily; he cannot retain it. Often, some of his limbs, either his legs, or his arms, or one of them, become relaxed.  If one vertebra is displaced from its position, it often shifts.
And its displacement can occur in four directions. The one that shifts backward is called a hump.  As for its treatment, one should examine it.  If the hump occurred from childhood, there is no treatment or cure for it at all.  However, if it occurred from a fall, a blow, or the like...
The ancients described various treatments with lengthy explanations, much of which proved ineffective.  I have condensed this, making a little of what I have said sufficient for much of what they presented. I have clarified the meaning and explained it, and I have depicted the instrument differently from how they described and explained it.
I say that a hump that forms in the front of the chest has no cure, nor is there any recovery from it; likewise, those that occur to the sides.  Only the one that occurs in the back is treatable, specifically as I describe it: the patient should be laid on their face.
On a flat platform near a wall, and spread underneath a moist mat so as not to injure his chest. Then, a vertical plank, driven into a hole in the ground, is placed near his head, at one end of the platform, and another plank near his feet at the opposite end of the platform.
A servant holds one plank, which is not secured in the hole, and another servant holds the other in that manner.  Then, a soft, secure bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under his armpits.  A portion of the bandage is extended to the plank near the head and tied to it.
Then, another bandage is tightened over his hips, over his knees, and at his ankles.  Then, all the bandages are gathered and tied to the other plank near his feet.  Then, each servant pulls the plank toward their side with the bandage, and the planks remain in their fixed positions.
However, they are not secured, as we said. The physician places his hands firmly on the protrusion until it returns, or he places a plank on it, then rests his legs on the plank until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, a plank measuring about three cubits is taken.
A space is dug in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, where the end of the plank can be inserted.  The middle of the plank is placed on the lump, and the physician places his legs on the other end, pulling firmly to compress the protrusion.
And it returns to its place.  If you wish, you can achieve this with a hand-twisted coil, by driving two planks into the ground, a cubit in length, near the patient's head at the end of the shop, and two more at his feet at the end of the shop.
Let there be a span between each plank.  A hole has been made in each plank through which the coil runs.  All the planks are firmly secured in the ground so that they do not move at all.  A round piece of wood, which is the coil, is inserted into the holes of the two planks, where the bandage is wound.
At its end is a hole secured with a stick one span long, to which it is wound.  The same is done in the other two planks.  Then, the bandage fastened to the patient's chest is tightened on the coil near the head, and the bandages fastened to the legs are tightened on the coil near the feet.
Then, a servant stands at each coil, twisting the coil with the twisting implement. The physician adjusts the lump as previously described.  This is an illustration of the coil, the shop, and the patient.
Then, after the fracture returns and the area is aligned, the dried bandage should be applied, then the plaster.  Then, a splint of wood, three fingers or so in width, and the length of the bump, is placed over the bandage.
And on top of some healthy beads. Then it is tied with the necessary bandage.  The patient should eat soft food until healed. If some protuberance remains in the area after healing, then medicines that soften and relax should be used, along with the splint we described for a long time.
A lead plate may also be used.  A protuberance may appear at the end of the vertebrae, leading to the mistaken belief that a dislocation has occurred.  This might be an extra bone that has protruded, and should not be treated in this way, as death could occur.
Chapter Thirty-one.
In the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and are not subject to the slight displacement and subluxation that occurs in other joints.  The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inward, outward, forward, and backward.  It dislocates inward most often, and rarely forward or backward.  A sign of an inward dislocation is that if you place the patient's healthy leg alongside the affected one, the affected leg will appear longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient will be unable to bend the leg at the groin. The area next to the groin will be swollen and clearly swollen, indicating that the head of the thigh has moved there.
The signs of a dislocation outward are the opposite of these signs.  The sign of a forward dislocation is that the patient can straighten the leg completely, but cannot bend it without pain, and if they try to walk, they cannot do so forward.
The patient's urine will be retained, and their groin will be swollen. When walking, they will place their weight on their heels.  The sign of a backward dislocation is that the patient cannot straighten the knee, nor can they bend it before bending the groin, and the leg will also be shorter than the other.
The groin is relaxed, and the head of the thigh is clearly visible at the hip area.  As for the reduction of these dislocations, observe if the dislocation is old and has been with the patient for some time, and they have not attempted to reduce it, and remains in its condition, then there is no cure at all.
Therefore, it should not be presented to you.  As for the recent dislocation, of whatever type of the four types of dislocations, immediately twist the joint, extending it inward and outward, and move it to the right and left, perhaps...
If it returns without needing further treatment, then if it does not return in this manner, it is necessary to have a strong, capable assistant to stretch the leg from below, either with their hands or with a bandage tied to the leg above the knee, and another assistant to stretch it from above.
By inserting their hands under his armpits, then pulling with a soft bandage on the root of the thigh, and a third assistant holding the end of the bandage, and the stretching can be done either from the front, from the groin area towards the collarbone, or from behind towards the back.
All of them should stretch at once, until the patient rises with his body off the ground and remains suspended.  This type of stretching is common to all four types. If the dislocation returns as we have described, then otherwise each type requires its own specific treatment as mentioned.
As for its specific return if the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on their sound side, then a bandage should be placed on the root of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the area below the groin.
Then the bandage is stretched upward, from the groin area towards the upper body and collarbone.  Another strong assistant grasps the thick part of the affected thigh with both arms and stretches it outward forcefully, and this will return it to its place.
This type is easier than other types of treatment for returning this limb. If you cannot achieve it with this method of treatment, you must tie the patient's legs together with a strong, soft bandage around the ankles and knees.
Each one should be a distance of four fingers from the other.  The affected leg should be stretched out further than the other, by a distance of two fingers.  Then the patient is suspended from the head by a wooden beam inside the house, and it should be a distance of two arms from the ground.
Then command a strong servant to grasp the head of the thigh, and another servant grasps the patient and pushes the servant holding the thigh forcefully. The joint will return to its place quickly.  As for its specific return if the dislocation is outward.
The patient should lie on the bench, as described for the humpback patient.  The bandage should be tightened on the affected leg and chest.  Then two pieces of wood are placed, one at the patient's feet, the other at their head.  A further piece of wood is placed firmly in the middle of the bench.
Wet cloths should be wrapped around it so that it does not injure the patient, so that the board is between the patient's thighs to prevent him from being pulled downwards during stretching. Then each attendant should pull in their direction and the physician should adjust the hip with his hands. If it returns to its place.
Otherwise, place the board on him and press it as described for the humpback, except that the patient should lie on their healthy side. If the dislocation is forward, the patient's leg should be stretched once, in this exact manner, on the bench.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with the other hand.  At the same time, the pressure should be extended downwards towards the knee.  If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be stretched downwards.
He is elevated off the ground, but should be placed on something firm.  Also, the hip and thigh should be turned outwards, as described in his lying position on the bench, face down, with bandages tightened.
As we stated previously, the board should also be used to apply pressure to the area where the joint has dislocated. When the hip joint returns properly – and you will know it has returned because the patient's legs can be stretched –
If you see that they are level and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned correctly.  Then, join the thighs, apply the dressing, and secure it with a bandage so tightly that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain at rest for three or four days.  Then, loosen the bandages and dressing, and compare the legs.  If you find that they are the same size, know that the fracture has healed. Release the patient to walk. If you notice any weakness,
Return and re-dress and tighten it as you did initially. Leave him at rest for three more days, then allow him to walk slowly for a few days until the limb is strong, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On the treatment of the knee fracture.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (i.e., backward).  It never dislocates forward. A sign of its dislocation is to instruct the patient to draw their leg towards their thigh.
If the leg does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. To treat all forms of dislocation, have the patient sit down with their legs extended, if they can manage it.  A helper should sit behind the patient to hold the patient's middle and gently rotate their body backward slightly, then you should sit on the patient's thighs.
You should face the patient, place the patient's leg between your legs, and then grasp the knee with both hands, interlacing your fingers on the knee.  Then, firmly press the sides of the knee with your hands, while another assistant extends the leg, until the knee returns to its place.
A sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh gently without force. Then bandage it, fastening the leg to the thigh, and bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, then remove the bandage.  Only allow the patient limited walking for a few days until it strengthens.
If you are unable to return it using the method described, then use strong traction with the bandages I previously described for treating the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may dislocate slightly or completely.  Dislocation may occur either inward or outward.  A sign of dislocation is that the heel appears swollen and prominent toward the direction of the dislocation.
As for treating a slight dislocation, it is easily returned by gently pulling and aligning it until it is back in place.  But if it is completely dislocated, the patient should sit, and a strong, sturdy assistant should hold him from behind, in the middle of his back.
Then, with your right hand grasp his foot from above, and with your left hand from below, at the ankle.  Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand, twice.  Then straighten the foot with your left hand and push the front of the foot towards the leg with your right hand, without force.
Do this twice as described.  Then, on the third time, push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling the heel.  If it returns in one or two attempts, and you see the foot is aligned, then stop.  Otherwise, repeat the procedure; it will return.
If it will not return with the method we described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground, and firmly embed and secure a very strong stake in the ground, positioning it between his thighs.  A cloth should have been wrapped around it to prevent injury to the patient.  Then the assistant should hold the patient's thigh.
Then another assistant stretches the leg, either with their hands or with a bandage tied around the patient's neck.  Then each assistant stretches in the opposite direction of the other, with the stake firmly between the patient's thighs to keep his body from pulling downward during the stretching.  Then the physician straightens the joint with their hands.
And another assistant holds the healthy leg below.  Then the joint will return quickly.  If the joint returns and you are sure of its return, take the bandage and splint and secure it with tight bindings, and tie the foot to the bandage below.  Care must be taken to avoid the nerve that is behind and above the heel.
So that the binding is not too tight and causes pain.  Then leave it for two or three days.  If the bandage loosens, tighten it, and then release it on the third or fourth day.  The patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this period, there is a risk that the joint will dislocate and be ruined, and treatment will not be successful afterwards. If a hot swelling appears, the remedies described previously for other conditions in the treatment and poultice sections should be used to alleviate it until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of the joint of the toes.
One should gently straighten the dislocated part by a slight pulling motion without force; this is not difficult but easy. If the dislocation is in one of the toes on the top of the foot, the patient should place their foot on a flat surface.
or on a board while standing as if walking, then place your foot on the protruding joint, and press down firmly with your foot until it returns to its normal position and the protruding area disappears.
Then place a board under the sole of their foot, covering the entire foot and having two ends.  Bind it firmly for three days, then unbind it and keep them from walking for many days, until it strengthens and a relapse is avoided, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation which occur with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both.
When anything of that sort occurs, and you attempt treatment and reduction, it is frequently followed by death.
Therefore, one should not undertake such treatment unless one is skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, compassionate, meticulous, not rash or reckless.  In the beginning, one should use only medications to relieve inflamed swellings, and allow the patient to recover.
Except by God's grace, unless you hope for the patient's safe recovery from injury, despite the mildness of the illness, and you perceive some hope for it, then at once, in the beginning, before a severe inflammation occurs, return it.
If the limb returns to its proper place, as you intended, then use the treatment that relieves inflammation and treat the wound with suitable drying ointments. If the dislocated joint is accompanied by a fracture, and fragments of bone have become detached, then remove them. Follow in that regard what we have mentioned concerning simple illnesses in the preceding sections.
Strive diligently and purify yourself from entering the realm of uncertainty, as I have previously instructed you.  That will preserve your reputation and safeguard your standing, God willing.
